The Second Source: Pure Reason and Self-Evident Truths
Ghazali defines pure reason and gives an example of a self-evident rational division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali establishes pure reason as a source of necessary, self-evident truths for theological arguments.

Core Idea
Pure reason provides self-evident truths that no rational person can deny.

In this passage, Ghazali introduces the second source of theological knowledge: pure reason. He argues that certain divisions, like the world being either originated or eternal, are necessary and self-evident. This foundation allows us to build proofs without relying solely on sense perception or revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali defines pure reason and gives a concrete example.
The example is a logical syllogism.
2
Identify the key division and syllogism
Find the division: the world is either originated or eternal, with no third. Then trace the syllogism: major premise, minor premise, conclusion.
This shows how pure reason operates.
3
Reflect on self-evidence
Consider why Ghazali calls the premise 'self-evident'. What would it mean to deny it?
It challenges the very basis of rationality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : العقلي المحضُ : فإنا إذا قلنا : العالم : إما حادث ، وإما قديم ، وليس وراء القسمين قسم ثالثُ . وجب الاعتراف به على كل عاقل . مثاله : أنَّا نقولُ : كلُّ ما لا يسبق الحادث فهو حادث ، والعالم لا يسبق الحادث ؛ فهو حادث ، فأحد الأصلين قولنا : إن ما لا يسبق الحادث فهو حادث ، ويجب على الخصم الإقرار به ؛ لأن ما لا يسبق الحادث : إما أن يكون مع الحادث ، أو بعده ، ولا يمكن قسم ثالث ثالثاً .. كان منكراً لما هو بديهي في العقل ، وإن أنكر أنَّ ما هو مع الحادث أو بعده فهو حادث (۱) . . فهو أيضاً منكر للبديهة
The second: pure reason (al-ʿaqlī al-maḥḍ): For when we say: the world is either originated (ḥādith) or eternal (qadīm), and there is no third division beyond the two. Acknowledgment of this is obligatory upon every rational person. Its example: we say: everything that does not precede the originated is originated, and the world does not precede the originated; so it is originated. So one of the two principles is our statement: that which does not precede the originated is originated, and it is obligatory for the opponent to acknowledge it, because that which does not precede the originated is either concomitant with the originated or after it, and a third division is impossible. [If he denies that], he would be denying what is self-evident in the intellect. And if he denies that that which is concomitant with the originated or after it is originated, he is also denying the self-evident.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage defines the second source: pure reason (العقلي المحض). Ghazali states that certain divisions are necessary, such as the world being either originated or eternal, with no third possibility. This must be acknowledged by every rational person. The example is a syllogism: whatever does not precede an originated thing is itself originated; the world does not precede originated things (since there are originated events), so the world is originated. The key premise is 'what does not precede the originated is originated', and Ghazali argues this is self-evident reason: anything simultaneous with or after an originated thing is itself originated. A third division (e.g., being neither before nor after nor with) is impossible. Thus, denying this is denying the self-evident (بديهة). This establishes principles that need no further sense-based proof.

Key Terms
العقلي المحض (pure reason)
حادث (originated)
قديم (eternal)
بديهي (self-evident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that pure reason only gives hypothetical or probable truths, not certain ones.
Correction: Ghazali argues that pure reason provides self-evident certainties (بديهي) that cannot be denied without irrationality.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the status of the statement 'that which does not precede the originated is originated'?
A) It is known by sensory perception
B) It is self-evident to the intellect
C) It is known by tradition
D) It is probable but not certain

Key Takeaway
Pure reason supplies self-evident rational principles that are necessary for all theological reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you truly accept that it is either originated or eternal? How does that make you feel about your ultimate dependence on another?
?
If pure reason gives self-evident truths, why do you sometimes resist them? What keeps you from submitting to what your own mind knows?
?
In what area of your life are you denying a self-evident truth because it is uncomfortable? What would change if you accepted it today?

The Two Temporal Divisions Relative to an Event
Ghazali states a rational axiom about time relative to an event.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the self-evident rational axiom that any existent relative to an event must be either simultaneous with it or after it, and that both are originated.

Core Idea
Time relative to an event has only two rational divisions: with and after.

Before we can build arguments about creation and eternity, we must agree on the basic grammar of time. Ghazali states a simple but crucial axiom: when we speak of something in relation to an event, it can only be either simultaneous with that event or after it. There is no third possibility. Denying this is denying reason itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to feel its concise force. Then read the literal English translation. Notice how Ghazali presents the division and then ties it to self-evident rationality.
The passage uses the term 'بعد' (after) and 'مع' (with).
2
Identify the two divisions and the denial of a third
The passage states that someone who claims a division that is 'with' or 'after' the event, and denies a third division, is correct. It then says that denying that anything with or after the event is originated is also a denial of the self-evident.
This establishes that both the division and the originated nature of both divisions are self-evident.
3
Reflect on why this matters for theology
Ask yourself: How does this axiom support arguments for the creation of the world? If everything that exists relative to an event is originated, then the world itself, which is relative to the event of creation, must be originated.
This connects to later arguments about the temporal beginning of the universe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون مع، فإن ادعى قسماً الحادث، أو بعده، ولا يمكن قسم ثالث، كان منكراً لما هو بديهي في العقل، وإن أنكر أن ما هو مع الحادث أو بعده فهو حادث، فهو أيضاً منكر للبديهة.
It is with [the event]; for if he claims a division [being] with the event or after it, and no third division is possible, he is a denier of what is self-evident in reason, and if he denies that what is with the event or after it is originated, he is also a denier of the self-evident.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents a rational premise that is self-evident. It argues that any existent relative to an event must either be simultaneous with it or after it; there is no third possibility. Denying this division is a denial of reason itself. Furthermore, denying that whatever is with or after an event is originated (hadith) is also a denial of self-evident truth. This establishes a foundational axiom for arguments about temporal events.

Key Terms
حادث (originated/event)
بديهي (self-evident)
مع (with/simultaneous)
بعد (after)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'with the event' could refer to something external that is not co-temporal, or that a third temporal relation (like 'before') is possible.
Correction: The passage makes clear that relative to the event, only 'with' and 'after' are rational divisions. 'Before' is not a category here because the event itself is the reference point. Both 'with' and 'after' are necessarily originated.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what are the only two possible temporal divisions relative to an event?
A) Before and after
B) With and after
C) Before and with
D) With and without

Key Takeaway
For any event, existence relative to it is either simultaneous or subsequent, and both are originated — this is self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there truths about your relationship with Allah that you have been denying, even though they are as self-evident as this axiom? What 'third division' are you inventing to avoid that reality?
?
You believe everything after an event is originated and subject to Allah's decree. How does that reshape the way you view your daily actions—do you live as if they are fleeting or as if they carry eternal weight?
?
When you consider the event of your own death, do you accept that everything after it is also created and judged, or are you holding onto a false hope that some part escapes origination? What change must you make today?

The Causal Principle as a Necessary Truth of Reason
Ghazali demonstrates the self-evidence of the causal principle through rational analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the causal principle (every event has a cause) to be a self-evident, necessary truth of reason, grounded in the analysis of possibility and impossibility.

Core Idea
The causal principle is a necessary truth of reason, not an empirical generalization.

Ghazali faces an objection: the claim that every event has a cause is not self-evident. His response is a masterclass in conceptual clarity. He argues that once we properly understand what 'event' and 'cause' mean, the principle becomes undeniable to reason. This passage lies at the heart of Islamic theology's rational defense of causality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent denies that 'every event has a cause' is self-evident. Recognize that the challenge is about the principle's epistemological status.
2
Define the key terms
Ghazali defines 'event' as what was non-existent then exists. He insists that clarity on terms removes hesitation.
Notice he defines 'event' and 'cause' (though 'cause' is implicit in the principle).
3
Analyze the alternatives before existence
Before existence, something is either impossible or possible. The impossible never exists. The possible is not necessary by itself.
This is a crucial logical disjunction.
4
The need for a preponderating factor
If it is possible, its existence requires a factor tipping the balance from non-existence to existence. Otherwise, non-existence would continue.
The concept of 'preponderating factor' (مرجح) is central.
5
Conclude the self-evidence
Thus, once we understand 'event' as something that could exist or not, reason necessarily affirms it needs a cause to exist.
The principle is a priori, not empirical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحريجة : لا أسلم فإن قال : إنما أنازع في قولك : إنَّ كلَّ حادث فله سبب ، فمن أين عرفت هذا ؟ فنقول : إن هذا الأصل يجب الإقرار به ، فإنَّه أوَّلِي ضروري في العقل ، ومن يتوقف فيه .. فإنَّما يتوقف لأنَّهُ ربما لا ينكشف له ما نريده بلفظ ( الحادث ) ولفظ ( السبب ) وإذا فهمهما .. صدق عقله بالضرورة بأنَّ لكل حادث سبباً ؛ فإنَّا نعني بالحادث : ما كان معدوماً ثم صار موجوداً ، فنقولُ : وجوده قبل أن وُجد كان محالاً أو ممكناً ، وباطل أن يكون محالاً ؛ لأنَّ المحالَ لا يوجد قطَّ ، وإن كان ممكناً .. فلسنا نعني بالممكن إلا : ما يجوز أن يوجد ، ويجوز ألا يوجد ، ولكن لم يكن موجوداً واجباً ؛ لأنه ليس يجب وجوده لذاته ؛ إذ لو وجب وجوده لذاته . . لكان واجباً لا ممكناً ، بل قد افتقر وجوده إلى مرجح لوجوده على العدم ؛ حتى يتبدل العدم بالوجود ، فإذا كان استمرار عدمه من حيث إنَّه لا مرجح للوجود على العدم
Check: I do not accept. If he says: 'I only dispute your statement that every event has a cause; from where did you know this?' We say: This principle must be affirmed, for it is a primary, necessary principle in the intellect. Whoever hesitates about it only hesitates because perhaps what we mean by the term 'event' and the term 'cause' has not become clear to him. When he understands them, his intellect necessarily affirms that every event has a cause. For we mean by 'event': that which was non-existent and then came into existence. We say: its existence before it existed was either impossible or possible. It is false that it was impossible, because the impossible never exists. If it was possible, we mean by 'possible' only that which may exist and may not exist, but it was not necessary of existence; because if its existence were necessary by itself, it would be necessary, not possible. Rather, its existence is in need of a preponderating factor for its existence over non-existence, so that non-existence is replaced by existence. So if its non-existence continued, it is because there is no preponderating factor for existence over non-existence.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage addresses a hypothetical objection: an opponent denies that every event necessarily has a cause. Ghazali argues that this principle is actually self-evident to reason (أولي ضروري) once the terms 'event' (حادث) and 'cause' (سبب) are properly understood. He defines 'event' as what was non-existent and then exists. He then analyzes such an entity before its existence: it was either impossible or possible. Impossible cannot exist. If possible, its existence is not necessary by itself; otherwise it would be necessary, not possible. Therefore, for it to come into existence, there must be a preponderating factor (مرجح) that tips the balance from non-existence to existence. The continued non-existence indicates the absence of such a factor. This reasoning grounds the causal principle in the nature of possibility itself.

Key Terms
حادث
سبب
ممكن
محال
مرجح
ضروري
أولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle 'every event has a cause' is an empirical generalization derived from repeated observation.
Correction: Ghazali argues it is a necessary truth of reason: once we understand what an event is (something that could exist or not) and what a cause is, reason immediately affirms that every event requires a cause. It does not depend on empirical observation.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali consider 'every event has a cause' to be a necessary principle of reason?
A) Because it is proven by repeated observation.
B) Because once one understands 'event' and 'cause', reason necessarily affirms it.
C) Because the Qur'an states it.
D) Because all philosophers agree on it.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's argument grounds the causal principle in the very nature of possibility, making it a necessary truth of reason rather than an empirical observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every event, including your very existence and every choice you make, requires a preponderating factor, then what does that demand of you in terms of gratitude and accountability toward the Causer? Will you now live as if your life is a gift from Him, or will you continue to attribute your successes to your own effort and chance?
?
Reflect on a time when you explained something as 'just luck' or 'coincidence'. In light of Ghazali's proof, can you honestly maintain that such events had no cause? How does dismissing the Causer in these small moments weaken your reliance on Him?
?
If the causal principle is a necessary truth of reason, then doubt in God's existence is not a matter of lacking evidence but of rejecting clear reason. What hinders you personally from accepting this truth? Are you willing to examine the barriers in your heart that keep you from acknowledging the Necessity of a Creator?

The First Principle: Every Contingent Event Has a Cause
Ghazali establishes the first principle: every contingent event has a cause, and argues it is self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first principle that every contingent event has a cause, why he considers it a primary and necessary truth, and how he defines 'contingent event' and 'cause' to make it self-evident.

Core Idea
Every contingent event needs a cause because possibility alone cannot explain existence.

Before building his proof for the existence of a non-corporeal Creator, Ghazali must first establish a foundational principle accepted by reason itself. Here he argues that the principle 'every contingent event has a cause' is not a conclusion of complex reasoning but a primary, self-evident truth. The key is to understand exactly what 'contingent event' and 'cause' mean. In this passage, Ghazali clarifies these terms and shows how the intellect necessarily affirms the principle once they are grasped.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without interruption, then read the English translation. Notice how Ghazali sets up the context: he is returning to verify an earlier proof, and he states that there are two principles, the first being this one.
The passage includes a hypothetical objection from an opponent.
2
Identify the definitions of 'contingent event' and 'cause'
Ghazali defines 'contingent event' (ḥādith) as 'what was non-existent and then came into existence' and 'cause' as the 'preponderator' (murjīḥ). Find these definitions in the text.
Cause = preponderator: that which tips the balance from non-existence to existence.
3
Analyze Ghazali's reasoning for the principle
Follow his argument: before existence, the contingent event was either impossible or possible. If impossible, it would never exist. If possible, its existence is not necessary by itself; it requires a preponderator to bring it into existence. This shows that every contingent event must have a cause.
This is a conceptual analysis, not a demonstrative proof from premises.
4
Understand Ghazali's claim that it is self-evident
Ghazali says the principle is 'primary and necessary in the intellect' (awwalī ḍarūrī). Once the terms are understood, the intellect necessarily affirms it. He even says that his explanation is not a proof but an elucidation of terms.
This helps you see why he considers it an unassailable starting point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد عرفت أنَّ الجسم والعرض مدركان بالمشاهدة . الموجود يدرك فأما موجود ليس بجسم ولا جوهر متحيّر ولا عَرَض .. فلا يدرك بالحس أو بالدليل بالحس ، ونحن ندعي وجوده ، وندعي أن العالم موجود به وبقدرته ، وهذا يدرك بالدليل لا بالحس، والدليل ما ذكرناه ، فلترجع إلى تحقيقه : فقد جمعنا فيه أصلين ، لعلَّ الخصم ينكرهما ؛ فنقول له : في أي الأصلين تنازع ؟ تحريجة : لا أسلم فإن قال : إنما أنازع في قولك : إنَّ كلَّ حادث فله سبب ، فمن أين عرفت هذا ؟ فنقول : إن هذا الأصل يجب الإقرار به ، فإنَّه أوَّلِي ضروري في العقل ، ومن يتوقف فيه .. فإنَّما يتوقف لأنَّهُ ربما لا ينكشف له ما نريده بلفظ ( الحادث ) ولفظ ( السبب ( وإذا فهمهما .. صدق عقله بالضرورة بأنَّ لكل حادث سبباً ؛ فإنَّا نعني بالحادث : ما كان معدوماً ثم صار موجوداً ، فنقولُ : وجوده قبل أن وُجد كان محالاً أو ممكناً ، وباطل أن يكون محالاً ؛ لأنَّ المحالَ لا يوجد قطَّ ، وإن كان ممكناً .. فلسنا نعني بالممكن إلا : ما يجوز أن يوجد ، ويجوز ألا يوجد ، ولكن لم يكن موجوداً واجباً ؛ لأنه ليس يجب وجوده لذاته ؛ إذ لو وجب وجوده لذاته . . لكان واجباً لا ممكناً ، بل قد افتقر وجوده إلى مرجح لوجوده على العدم ؛ حتى يتبدل العدم بالوجود ، فإذا كان استمرار عدمه من حيث إنَّه لا مرجح للوجود على العدم ، فما لم يوجد المرجح .. لا يوجد ، ونحن لا نريد بالسبب إلا المرجح والحاصل : أن المعدومَ المستمرَّ العدم لا يتبدَّلُ عدمُهُ بالوجود ما لم يتحقق أمر من الأمور يرجُحُ جانب الوجود على استمرار العدم ، وهذا إذا حصل في الذهن معنى لفظِهِ .. كان العقل مضطراً إلى التصديق به . فهذا بيان هذا الأصل، وهو على التحقيق شرح للفظ ( الحادث ) و ( السبب ) لا إقامة دليل عليه
You have known that body and accident are perceived by observation. The existent is perceived; but an existent that is neither a body, nor a substantially located substance, nor an accident—it is not perceived by sense or by proof through sense. And we claim its existence, and we claim that the world exists through Him and by His power. This is perceived by proof, not by sense. The proof is what we mentioned. So let us return to its verification: We have included in it two principles, perhaps the opponent will deny them; so we say to him: Which of the two principles do you dispute? Objection: I do not accept. For if he says: I only dispute your statement that every contingent event has a cause; from where did you know this? We say: This principle must be acknowledged, for it is primary and necessary in the intellect. And whoever hesitates about it only hesitates because perhaps what we intend by the term 'contingent event' and the term 'cause' is not clear to him. When he understands them, his intellect necessarily affirms that every contingent event has a cause. For we mean by 'contingent event': that which was non-existent and then came into existence. So we say: Its existence before it existed was either impossible or possible. It is false that it was impossible, because the impossible never exists. And if it was possible, we mean by 'possible' only that which may exist and may not exist, but it did not exist necessarily; because its existence is not necessary by itself. For if its existence were necessary by itself, it would be necessary, not possible. Rather, its existence requires a preponderator for its existence over non-existence, so that non-existence is changed to existence. Since its continued non-existence is due to the fact that there is no preponderator for existence over non-existence, so as long as the preponderator does not exist, it does not exist. And we mean by 'cause' only the preponderator. The result is that a non-existent that continues in non-existence does not have its non-existence changed to existence unless there comes about something that preponderates the side of existence over continued non-existence. When the meaning of its expression occurs in the mind, the intellect is forced to affirm it. This is the explanation of this principle, and in truth it is an explanation of the terms 'contingent event' and 'cause', not a proof of it.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a definition and justification of the first principle in Ghazali's proof for a non-corporeal creator: every contingent event (ḥādith) has a cause. Ghazali begins by reminding the reader that bodies and accidents are perceived by the senses, but a non-bodily, non-accidental being is known only by proof. He then states that the proof rests on two principles. The first principle is that every contingent event has a cause. Ghazali presents an objection: an opponent disputes this principle. In response, Ghazali argues that the principle is primary (awwalī) and necessary (ḍarūrī) in the intellect; it is self-evident once the terms 'contingent event' and 'cause' are properly understood. He defines 'contingent event' as that which was non-existent and then came into existence. He then analyzes its prior non-existence: either impossible or possible. If impossible, it would never exist; if possible, it requires a preponderator (murjīḥ) to bring it into existence. This preponderator is what is meant by 'cause'. Ghazali concludes that the entire discussion is not an independent proof but an elucidation of the terms, because the intellect necessarily affirms the principle once the meanings are understood.

Key Terms
حادث (ḥādith)
سبب (sabab)
مرجح (murjīḥ)
ممكن (mumkin)
محال (muḥāl)
ضروري (ḍarūrī)
أولي (awwalī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali is providing a proof from scratch for this principle, as if it were a conclusion of reasoning.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that it is a primary and self-evident truth; once the terms 'contingent event' and 'cause' are properly defined, the intellect necessarily affirms it without further proof.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is the principle that every contingent event has a cause not in need of proof?
A) Because it is known through divine revelation.
B) Because it is a primary and necessary truth of reason once the terms are understood.
C) Because it is proven by the impossibility of an infinite regress.
D) Because the opponent already accepts it.

Key Takeaway
The first principle of Ghazali's proof is that every contingent event has a cause, and this is a self-evident truth once the terms are properly defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if every event in your life has a cause, or do you assume some things happen without reason? When something difficult occurs, do you stop to ask for the Preponderator behind it—the One who alone brings existence from non-existence? Or do you let circumstances feel random, ignoring the Hand that tips the scale?
?
Ghazali says that once the meaning of 'contingent' and 'cause' is understood, the intellect necessarily affirms the principle. But does your daily pattern of worry and planning reflect that you truly believe you are contingent at every moment? If you fully embraced that your very existence requires a Preponderator each instant, would your reliance on Him increase? What would you stop worrying about starting today?
?
If your existence is not necessary by itself—if it teeters on the edge of non-existence without a cause—how should that change the way you wake up each morning? Do you feel the weight of your need for the Preponderator, or have you grown comfortable as if you simply are? Commit to one action today that acknowledges your utter dependence on the One who brings you from nothing to something.

Proof that Motion is an Accident (Proof from Affirmation and Negation)
Ghazali presents a logical proof that motion is an accident additional to the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical proof that motion is an accident distinct from the body, based on the behavior of affirmation and negation.

Core Idea
Negation of motion leaves the body intact, proving motion is not identical to it.

How can we know that motion is not simply the body itself? Ghazali presents a simple but powerful logical argument: if motion were identical to the body, then denying motion would be denying the body. But we can deny motion while the body remains — so motion must be something additional. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then the English translation. Let Ghazali's own words set the stage.
2
Understand key terms
Clarify the meanings of motion (الحركة), atom (الجوهر), and accident (العرض), as these are central to the argument.
In kalam, 'jawhar' often means an indivisible atom.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Identify the premise (affirming motion adds something), the negation test (denying motion doesn't deny the body), and the reductio ad absurdum (if motion = body, negation would negate the body).
4
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why distinguishing accidents from substances matters for Islamic theology, especially in arguments about divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أردنا سياق دليل على وجود الحركة زيادة على الجسم.. قلنا : إنَّا إذا قلنا : إنَّ هذا الجوهر متحرك . . أثبتنا شيئاً سوى الجوهر ، بدليل أنا إذا قلنا : هذا الجوهر ليس بمتحرك .. صدق قولنا إن كان الجوهر باقياً ساكناً فلو كان المفهوم من الحركة عين الجوهر .. لكان نفيها نفي عين الجوهر! وهكذا يطرد الدليل في إثبات السكون ونفيه
And if we wish to present a proof for the existence of motion as additional to the body, we say: When we say 'This atom is moving,' we affirm something other than the atom. The proof is that when we say 'This atom is not moving,' our statement is true if the atom remains stationary. If the concept of motion were identical to the atom, then its negation would be the negation of the atom itself! And the same proof applies uniformly in affirming and denying rest.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained proof. Ghazali argues that motion is an accident (ʻarad) distinct from the body (jawhar). The argument: when we say 'this atom is moving,' we affirm something additional to the atom. This is shown by the fact that we can say 'this atom is not moving' and the statement is true while the atom remains. If motion were identical to the atom, then denying motion would be denying the atom itself, which is false. Therefore, motion is a separate entity—an accident. The same reasoning works for rest as well. This proof relies on the logical distinction between affirming and negating a predicate.

العرض
الجوهر
الوجود

Misconception: The proof is based on sense perception.
Correction: The proof is purely logical, based on linguistic affirmation and negation, not sensory observation.

Checkpoint

A) Because motion can be seen by the eye.
B) Because we can negate motion while the atom remains.
C) Because motion can be transferred.
D) Because motion is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Motion is proven to be an accident because we can deny it without denying the body's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If you were to truly negate that attribute—declare 'I am not patient'—would you still be the same person? What does your answer reveal about whether you treat that attribute as essential to your identity or merely accidental, as motion is to the body in Ghazali’s proof?

Ghazali’s proof relies on the logical relationship between affirmation and negation to distinguish what is truly essential. Think of a deeply held belief or conviction you have. If you were to deny it, would your very existence as a person be negated? If not, what does that imply about the nature of that belief—is it truly essential or accidental? How might this realization change how you hold it?
?
If your actions (like motion) are accidental to your essence, then your identity is not defined by what you do but by what you are. Does this comfort you or unsettle you? How would you live differently today if you truly internalized that your deeds do not constitute your core self?

Proof That Motion Is an Accident
Ghazali provides a proof that motion is an accident additional to substance, then notes the futility of proving self-evident things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's proof that motion is an accident distinct from substance, and why he warns against over-proving self-evident things.

Core Idea
Motion is an accident added to substance, not the substance itself.

Imam al-Ghazali presents a concise logical proof to show that motion is an accident (ʿaraḍ) distinct from the substance (jawhar) in which it inheres. He then makes a wise methodological observation: demanding elaborate proofs for self-evident truths only makes them more obscure. This passage teaches both a specific theological conclusion and a general principle of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the logical structure of the proof.
Pay attention to the conditional reasoning.
2
Identify the proof's structure
The proof uses a conditional: if motion were identical to substance, then negating motion would negate substance. Since we can negate motion while substance remains, motion must be distinct.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Reflect on the methodological lesson
Ghazali ends with a caution against over-proving the obvious. Consider why this is important in theology and reasoning.
Self-evident truths do not require elaborate proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أردنا سياق دليل على وجود الحركة زيادة على الجسم، قلنا: إن الدليل على أن الحركة عرض إذا قلنا: إن هذا الجوهر متحرك أثبتنا شيئاً سوى الجوهر، بدليل أنا إذا قلنا: هذا الجوهر ليس بمتحرك صدق قولنا إن كان الجوهر باقياً ساكناً، فلو كان المفهوم من الحركة عين الجوهر لكان نفيها نفي عين الجوهر! وهكذا يطرد الدليل في إثبات السكون ونفيه. وعلى الجملة: فتكلُّفُ الدليل على الواضحات يزيدها غموضاً، ولا يفيدها وضوحاً.
And if we wish to present a proof for the existence of motion as additional to the body, we say: the proof that motion is an accident is that when we say 'this substance is moving' we affirm something other than the substance, because if we say 'this substance is not moving' our statement is true as long as the substance remains quiescent. If the concept of motion were identical to the substance, then negating it would negate the substance itself! And the same proof applies to affirming and negating quiescence. In general, forcing a proof for self-evident things increases their obscurity and does not give them clarity.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a proof by Imam al-Ghazali that motion is an accident (ʿaraḍ) distinct from the substance (jawhar) in which it inheres. He argues: when we say 'this substance is moving,' we affirm something beyond the substance itself—the state of motion. The proof is that if we say 'this substance is not moving,' the statement is true provided the substance remains quiescent. If motion were identical to the substance, then denying motion would require denying the substance itself, which is absurd since the substance persists. The same reasoning applies to quiescence (sukūn), which is also an accident. Ghazali then adds a methodological remark: it is pointless to demand elaborate proofs for things that are self-evident; such efforts only make them more obscure.

Key Terms
حركة
عرض
جوهر
سكون
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Ghazali is proving the existence of motion itself.
Correction: He is proving that motion is an accident additional to the substance, not the substance itself.

Checkpoint

B) Denying motion would require negating the substance itself.
C) Motion would be eternal and uncreated.
D) The substance would cease to be a substance.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's logical proof demonstrates that motion is an accident added to substance, and he reminds us that not all truths require elaborate proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you move from one state to another—from heedlessness to awareness, from sin to obedience—do you define yourself by that motion, or do you know it is an accident that can change? How would your spiritual struggle change if you truly internalized that your actions are not your essence?
?
Ghazali says forcing proof for self-evident things only obscures them. Is there a truth about your own soul that you have overcomplicated with analysis, when deep down you already know it? What would it take to stop proving and start acting on what is already clear?
?
If motion is an accident, then every movement of your body—every prayer, every glance, every step—is something added to you, not you. Are you taking ownership of these accidents as acts of worship, or are you letting them pass as if they are not yours to direct?

The Creator Is Not an Accident: A Logical Proof
Ghazali proves the Creator is not an accident and clarifies terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical argument that the Creator cannot be an accident and how he clarifies the terminology regarding divine attributes.

Core Idea
The Creator is eternal, but accidents are originated—so He cannot be an accident.

In this passage from al-Iqtiṣād fī al-Iʿtiqād, Imam al-Ghazali presents the sixth claim: the Maker of the world is not an accident. He carefully defines what an accident is, provides a proof from the nature of accidents and the Creator's eternity, and then addresses an alternative understanding of 'accident' as a quality. He shows that the real dispute is not about attributes but about the proper application of terms like 'maker' to the essence rather than the attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and definition
Begin by reading the passage's opening claim and Ghazali's definition of 'accident' (ʿaraḍ) as something that inheres in a substance. Notice how he sets up the proof by defining his terms.
The first definition is crucial for the logical argument.
2
Follow the proof from eternity
Ghazali argues that accidents are originated because they inhere in bodies that are originated, and since the transmission of accidents is impossible, an eternal being cannot be an accident. Trace each step of this reasoning.
This proof relies on earlier discussions about the world's origination.
3
Understand the clarification about attributes
Next, Ghazali considers a second definition of 'accident' as a quality. He affirms that God has attributes but insists that 'maker' applies to the essence, not the attributes. Use the carpenter analogy to grasp this distinction.
This addresses a common misunderstanding that denying God is an accident denies His attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى السادسة
ندعي : أن صانع العالم ليس بعَرَض ؛ لأنَّا نعني بالعرض : ما وجوده ذاتاً يقوم به ، وذلك الذاتُ أو جوهر ، ومهما كان الجسم بعرض جسم واجب الحدوث . . كان الحال فيه أيضاً حادثاً لا محالة ؛ إذ بطل انتقال الأعراض وقد بينا أن صانع العالم قديم ، فلا يمكن أن يكون عرضاً ، وإن فُهِمَ من العرض ما هو صفةٌ لشيء من غير أن يكون ذلك الشيء متحيزاً .. فنحن لا ننكر وجود هذا ، فإنا نستدل على صفات الله تعالى
نعم ؛ يرجعُ النزاع إلى إطلاق اسم الصانع والفاعل ، فإن إطلاقه على الذات الموصوفة بالصفات .. أَوْلَى من إطلاقه على الصفات
فإذا قلنا : الصانع ليس بصفة .. عَنَيْنا به أنَّ الصنع مضاف إلى الذات التي تقوم بها الصفات ، لا إلى الصفات ؛ كما أنَّا إذا قلنا : النجار ليس بعرض ولا صفة . . عَنَيْنا به أن صنعة النجارة غيرُ مضافة إلى الصفات ، بل إلى الذات الواجب وصفها بجملة من الصفات حتى يكون صانعاً ، فكذا القول
في صانع العالم وإن أراد المنازعُ في تسميته بالعرض أمراً غير الحال في الجسم ، وغير الصفة القائمة بالذات .. كان الحقُّ في منعه للغة أو الشرع ، لا للعقل
The Sixth Claim. We claim: that the Maker of the world is not an accident; because we mean by 'accident' (ʿaraḍ): that whose existence is in a substance (dhāt) which subsists by itself, and that substance is a substance (jawhar) or essence. Whenever a body is an accident [in] a body necessarily originated, the state in it is also originated without exception, because the transmission of accidents is impossible. And we have already shown that the Maker of the world is eternal, so He cannot be an accident. And if by 'accident' is understood what is a quality (ṣifa) of a thing without that thing being located in space... then we do not deny the existence of this, for we use it to argue for the attributes of God Most High. Yes; the dispute returns to the application of the name 'maker' (ṣāniʿ) and 'agent' (fāʿil), for applying it to the essence (dhāt) qualified by attributes is more appropriate than applying it to the attributes. When we say 'the maker is not a quality', we mean that making is attributed to the essence which subsists by the attributes, not to the attributes; just as when we say 'the carpenter is not an accident nor a quality', we mean that the craft of carpentry is not attributed to the qualities, but to the essence which must be qualified by a set of qualities in order to be a maker. So too the statement regarding the Maker of the world. And if the opponent intends by naming Him 'accident' something other than what inheres in a body and other than the quality subsisting in the essence, then the right is to prevent it by language or Law, not by reason.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative proof that the Creator is not an accident. It begins by stating the claim: the Maker of the world is not an accident. Ghazali then defines 'accident' as something whose existence inheres in a substance (dhāt) that is a self-subsisting entity (jawhar). Because a body that has an accident must be originated, the accident itself is also originated. Since transmission of accidents is impossible (as previously proven), and the Maker is eternal, He cannot be an accident. Next, Ghazali considers an alternative definition: if 'accident' is understood as a quality of something that is not spatially located, then he does not deny that such qualities exist (they are the divine attributes). However, the real issue is linguistic: the term 'maker' (ṣāniʿ) is more properly applied to the essence (dhāt) that possesses attributes, not to the attributes themselves. Ghazali illustrates with an analogy: when we say 'the carpenter is not an accident', we mean the crafting activity is attributed to the essence, not to the qualities. Similarly, when we deny that the Maker is a quality, we mean the act of creation is attributed to His essence. Finally, if an opponent insists on calling the Creator an 'accident' in a sense different from the two discussed, the objection is not rational but linguistic or legal, and can be dismissed on grounds of language or divine law.

Key Terms
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
جوهر (jawhar)
ذات (dhāt)
صفة (ṣifa)
صانع (ṣāniʿ)
فاعل (fāʿil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying that God is an accident means denying that God has attributes.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that when we say God is not an accident in the sense of a quality, we do not deny His attributes; rather, we affirm that attributes belong to His essence and that the term 'maker' applies to the essence, not the attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the primary reason the Maker of the world cannot be an accident (in the first sense)?
A) Because accidents are spatial and God is not spatial
B) Because accidents are originated and the Maker is eternal
C) Because accidents are multiple and God is one
D) Because the word 'accident' is linguistically inappropriate

Key Takeaway
Ghazali proves that the Creator is not an accident because accidents are originated, and He is eternal; attributes are affirmed for His essence, not as separate entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you sometimes reduce Him to a set of attributes in your mind, forgetting that they belong to a living, eternal essence? How can you train your heart to worship the Essence, not just ideas?
?
Ghazali shows that arguments can hinge on definitions. Is there a belief you hold that you defend fiercely, but perhaps the dispute is really about words, not reality? Are you willing to examine that?
?
If the Creator is not an accident, then He is not a passing feature of your life. But do you treat Him as if He is an accidental part of your week—something you attend to when convenient? What would it mean to make Him the substance of your daily existence?

Refuting the Objection That Accidents Would Be Measured
Ghazali refutes an objection about accidents' measurement by appealing to dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that direction-implied measurement would also apply to accidents fails, and how Ghazali uses the concept of dependence (tābi'iyya) to uphold divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Accidents are measured only by dependence, not by themselves.

In this passage, Ghazali takes up a subtle objection: if being in a direction implies measurement, then accidents (which inhere in substances) would also have to be measured. He responds by clarifying that accidents have direction and measurement only derivatively, through their dependence on substances. This distinction is crucial for preserving the absolute transcendence of God, who is not a substance or accident and thus has no measurement whatsoever.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure of objection (فإن قيل) and response (قلنا).
The objection attempts to force a contradiction.
2
Identify the key concept of dependence
Understand how Ghazali argues that accidents have measurement only by dependence (tābi'iyya), not intrinsically. He gives an example: ten similar accidents can only exist in ten substances.
The example shows derivative measurement.
3
Connect to divine transcendence
See why this refutes the objection: God is not a substance or accident, so no measurement applies.
This protects the doctrine that God is beyond all spatial categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو كان الاختصاص بالجهة يوجب التقدير .. لكانَ العَرَضُ مقدراً ؟ قلنا : العرضُ ليس في جهة بنفسه ، بل بتبعيَّةِ الجوهر ، فلا جرم هو أيضاً مقدر بالتبعية ؛ فإنَّا نعلم أنه لا توجد عشرة أعراض متماثلة في عشرة جواهر ، ولا يتصوَّرُ أن تكون في عشرين ، فَتَقَدُّرُ الأعراض بالعشرة لازم بطريق التبعية لتقدر الجواهر ، كما لزم كونه بجهة بطريق التبعية
If it is said: If particularization by direction necessitates measurement, then would an accident be measured? We say: The accident is not in a direction by itself, but by dependence on the substance. Thus it is also measured by way of dependence; for we know that ten similar accidents do not exist in ten substances, nor can they be in twenty. So the measurement of accidents by ten is necessary by way of dependence on the measurement of substances, just as being in a direction is necessary by way of dependence.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector argues that if direction implies measurement (taqdīr), then accidents (a'rād) would also need to be measured, which might be considered absurd. Ghazali responds that accidents do not have direction independently; they inhere in substances and thus have measurement only by dependence (tābi'iyya). For example, a limited number of similar accidents can exist in a limited number of substances. The accident's measurement is derivative, not intrinsic. Similarly, an accident's being in a direction is also derivative. This does not pose a problem for the argument about God because God is not a substance or accident, so the analogy fails. Ghazali is refuting the objection by showing that measurement by dependence is acceptable for accidents, but God's case is different because He is not subject to such dependence.

Key Terms
عَرَض ('araḍ)
جَوْهَر (jawhar)
تَبَعِيَّة (tābi'iyya)
تَقْدِير (taqdīr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali is saying accidents are measured intrinsically.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies it is only by dependence, not an intrinsic property.

Checkpoint

B) By dependence on the substance.
C) Not measured at all.
D) By God's direct action.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali upholds divine transcendence by showing that measurement applies to accidents only derivatively, not to God.

What would change if you truly internalized that your very existence and attributes are derivative of Him?

5 min
Logical possibility of a non-spatial existent
Ghazali distinguishes existents that accept spatial attributes from those that do not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali argues that an existent that does not accept spatial attributes can be free from spatial opposites, using an analogy with incapability and power.

Core Idea
An existent that does not accept connection can be free from both connection and separation.

Ghazali addresses a key objection to divine transcendence: that any existing thing must be either connected or separated. In this passage, he uses an analogy to show that this only applies to things that accept spatial attributes. The condition for spatial opposites is spatial occupation; if that is absent, freedom from spatial opposites is logically possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: a conceded principle, a contrasting claim, and an analogy.
2
Identify the conceded principle
Ghazali states that it is given that any existent that accepts connection to a direction cannot be free from both connection and separation. Understand why this is conceded.
This principle is the basis for the objection against a non-spatial God.
3
Examine the analogy
Ghazali compares the situation to the opposite pair of incapability and power. A lifeless object is free from both because it lacks the condition (life). This illustrates how freedom from opposites is possible when the condition is absent.
The condition for spatial opposites is spatial occupation (taḥayyuz).
4
Formulate the core question
After establishing that freedom from opposites is possible for things lacking the condition, Ghazali reduces the inquiry to whether a non-spatial existent is possible at all.
This sets up the next step in the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : مسلَّم أنَّ كلَّ موجود يقبل الاتصال بجهة فوجوده لا متصلاً ولا منفصلاً محال ، وأنَّ كلَّ موجود يقبل الاختصاص بجهة فوجوده مع خلو الجهات الست عنه محال وأما موجود لا يقبل الاتصال ولا الاختصاص بالجهة .. فخلوه عن طرفي النقيض غير محال ، وهو كقول القائل : يستحيل موجود لا يكون عاجزاً ولا قادراً ، ولا عالماً ولا جاهلاً ؛ فإن أحد المتضادين لا يخلو الشيء عنه ؛ فيقال له : إن كان ذلك الشيء قابلاً للمتضادين .. فيستحيلُ خلوه عنهما ، وإن كان غير قابل لهما .. لا يستحيل خلوه عنهما ، كما الجماد الذي لا يقبلُ واحداً منهما ؛ لأنَّه فَقَدَ شرطهما ؛ وهو الحياة ، فخلوه عنهما ليس بمحال ، فكذلك شرطُ الاتصال والاختصاص بالجهات التحيز والقيام بالمتحيز ، فإذا فُقِدَ هذا .. لم يستحل الخلو عن متضاداتِهِ ، فيرجع النظر إذن إلى أنَّ موجوداً ليس بمتحيز ولا هو في متحيز ، بل هو فاقد شرط الاتصال والانفصال . . هل هو محال أم لا ؟
We said: It is granted that every existent that accepts connection to a direction, its existence without being connected or separated is impossible, and that every existent that accepts specification to a direction, its existence while empty of the six directions is impossible. But as for an existent that does not accept connection nor specification to a direction, its emptiness from the two extremes of the opposite is not impossible. It is like someone saying: It is impossible for an existent to be neither incapable nor capable, neither knowing nor ignorant; for the thing is never empty of one of the two opposites. So it is said to him: If that thing is receptive to the two opposites, then its emptiness from them is impossible; but if it is not receptive to them, its emptiness from them is not impossible, like the lifeless object (al-jamād) which does not accept either of them because it has lost their condition, which is life, so its emptiness from them is not impossible. So likewise, the condition of connection and specification to directions is spatial occupation (al-taḥayyuz) and subsisting in a spatial being (al-qiyām bi l-mutaḥayyiz). So when this is lost, emptiness from their opposites is not impossible. Then the inquiry reduces to whether an existent that is not spatial (mutamayyiz) and not in a spatial being, but rather lacking the condition of connection and separation, is impossible or not.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage defines the logical possibility of an existent that is neither connected to nor separated from a direction, using an analogy with incapability/power and ignorance/knowledge. The author begins by stating a conceded principle: if something accepts connection to a direction, then it cannot be free from both connection and separation (e.g., it must be either connected or separated). Similarly, if it accepts specification by a direction, it cannot be free from all six directions. However, if something does not accept connection or specification at all, then being free from both opposites is not impossible. The analogy clarifies: for a thing that is receptive to both incapability and capability, it cannot be free from both; but if it is not receptive (like a lifeless object that lacks the condition of life), it can be free from both. The condition for spatial opposites is spatial occupation (taḥayyuz) or being in a spatial being. If that condition is absent, the existent can be free from spatial opposites. Thus the question reduces to whether an existent that is not spatial and not in a spatial being (i.e., lacking the condition for spatial opposites) is impossible.

Key Terms
اتصال
انفصال
جهة
تحيز
متضادان
شرط
جماد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any existent must be either connected or separated, regardless of its nature.
Correction: This only applies to existents that accept connection; a non-spatial existent that does not accept connection can be free from both.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Ghazali use to show that a non-spatial existent can be free from spatial opposites?
A) An object that is both high and low
B) A lifeless object free from inability and power
C) A body that is both moving and still
D) A sound that is both loud and quiet

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the impossibility of being free from spatial opposites only applies to existents that accept spatial attributes; a non-spatial existent can be free from them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you envision Allah in your mind, do you ever unconsciously assign Him a direction or location? How does this logical argument force you to purify your conception of Him, and what concrete step will you take today to remind yourself that He is beyond spatial attributes?
?
This passage shows that lack of a condition (like spatiality) does not mean lack of existence. In your own life, do you sometimes equate 'not having a certain attribute' with 'not being' or 'being less'? How can you apply this lesson to trust that Allah's mode of existence is perfect precisely because it is free from creaturely limitations?
?
Ghazali uses an analogy with a lifeless object to argue that something can exist without being subject to certain opposites. If you truly internalize that Allah is not subject to spatial opposites, how should that change the way you pray, knowing that He is not 'above' or 'distant' in a spatial sense?

Distinction between unimaginable and unintelligible
Ghazali responds to the objection that the proven existent is unintelligible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between something being unimaginable (not formable in the mind) and something being unintelligible (not known by rational proof).

Core Idea
What cannot be imagined can still be rationally known.

An opponent objects that the existence proven by rational argument is 'unintelligible'. Ghazali responds by first clarifying what the opponent might mean. If by 'unintelligible' they mean 'unimaginable', that is true—but it does not affect rational knowledge. This passage is a masterclass in distinguishing between the limits of imagination and the reach of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the objection and the two-part response.
Pay attention to the key terms: غير مفهوم, متصور, وهم, خيال, معقول.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'unintelligible'
Ghazali asks the opponent what they mean. Identify the first meaning (unimaginable) and the second meaning (not known by proof). Notice how he responds differently to each.
3
Understand why imagination fails
Reflect on why imagination cannot grasp non-physical realities. The passage says imagination is habituated to visible things and can only picture bodies with color and quantity.
This shows that imagination is limited to physical objects.
4
Contrast imagination with rational knowledge
Ghazali defines 'rationally intelligible' as what the mind is compelled to accept by an undeniable proof. This is different from forming a mental image.
Rational knowledge does not require imagination.
5
Apply the distinction to the proof of God
The opponent's objection fails because the proof for God's existence is rational, not imaginative. The existence is known through reason even if not pictured.
This protects the theological argument from a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال الخصم : إن مثل هذا الموجودِ الذي ساق دليلكُم إلى إثباته غير مفهوم . فيقال له : ما الذي أردت بقولك : ( غير مفهوم ) ؟ فإن أردت بقولك أنَّهُ غير متخيَّل ، ولا متصوَّرٍ ، ولا داخل في الوهم .. فقد صدقت ؛ فإنه لا يدخل في التصور والوهم والخيال إلا جسم له لون وقدر ، فالمنفت [المنفي؟] اللون والقدر لا يتصوره الخيالُ ، فإنَّ الخيال قد أَنِسَ بالمبصرات ، فلا يتوهم الشيء إلا على وفق ما رآه ، ولا يستطيع أن يتوهم ما لا يوافقه . وإن أراد الخصم أنه ليس بمعقول ؛ أي: ليس بمعلوم بدليل العقل .. فهو محال ؛ إذ قدَّمنا الدليل على ثبوته ، ولا معنى للمعقول إلا ما اضطر العقل إلى الإذعان والتصديق به بموجب الدليل الذي لا يمكن مخالفته ، وقد تحقق هذا .
If the opponent says: 'Such an existent, to which your proof leads, is unintelligible (ghayru mafhūm).' Then it is said to him: 'What do you mean by your saying “unintelligible”?' If you mean by it that it is not imaginable (mutakhayyal), nor formable in the mind (mutasawwar), nor entering into the imagination (wahm) – then you have spoken truly; for nothing enters into conceptualization, imagination, and fantasy except a body that has color and quantity. So that which is negated [of color and quantity] is not imagined by the imagination. Indeed, imagination has grown accustomed to visible things, so it only imagines a thing in accordance with what it has seen, and it cannot imagine what does not conform to it. But if the opponent means that it is not rationally intelligible (ma‘qūl), i.e., not known by a rational proof, then that is impossible, because we have already presented the proof for its existence. And 'rationally intelligible' means nothing but what the mind is compelled to accept and affirm by virtue of a proof that cannot be contradicted, and this has been realized.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The opponent claims the existence proven by the kalam argument is 'unintelligible'. The author first asks for clarification. If the opponent means it cannot be pictured in the imagination or conceived as a mental image, that is true: only bodies with color and size can be imagined. If, however, the opponent means it cannot be known by rational proof, that is false because we have already given a proof. The author defines 'rational intelligible' as that which the intellect is compelled to affirm by an incontrovertible proof. Since the proof has been given, the existence is indeed rationally known.

Key Terms
غير مفهوم
متصور
وهم
خيال
معقول
دليل العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something cannot be imagined, it cannot be known at all.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes between imagination and rational knowledge: what cannot be imagined can still be rationally proven and known.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali distinguish between 'unimaginable' and 'unintelligible'?
A) Both are impossible
B) Unimaginable is acceptable but unintelligible is not
C) Unintelligible is acceptable but unimaginable is not
D) Both are the same

Key Takeaway
God's existence is rationally proven even though He cannot be imagined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
What does the fact that your imagination cannot contain Allah tell you about His greatness? Does this humble you or unsettle you – and why?
?
The author says the mind is 'compelled to accept and affirm' by proof. Are you truly compelled by the proofs for Allah’s existence, or do you still entertain doubts? What step will you take today to strengthen your certainty?

Refutation of the Claim that Only Imaginable Things Exist
Ghazali refutes the opponent's claim with counterexamples from human experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali argues against the criterion that existence is limited to what can be imagined, using counterexamples from human experience.

Core Idea
Existence is wider than imagination—mental states and qualities prove it.

Ghazali now addresses a common rationalist objection: that only things that can be pictured in the mind truly exist. He shows this view is self-defeating by pointing to things that clearly exist but cannot be imagined. This passage helps us understand that Allah's existence, being beyond human imagination, is not disproven by its unimaginability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the opponent's claim and Ghazali's response.
2
Identify the counterexamples
List the specific things Ghazali mentions that exist but cannot be imagined. These include imagination itself, vision, knowledge, power, sound, smell, and internal states like shame and fear.
3
Understand why they are effective
Ask yourself why each counterexample undermines the opponent's criterion. For example, imagination cannot imagine itself, yet we know it exists.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that the opponent's position is self-defeating. Think about how this opens the door to accepting the existence of God, who is not limited by human imagination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنْ قال الخصم : فما لا يُتصوّر في الخيال لا وجود له ؟ فلنحكم بأنَّ الخيال لا وجود له في نفسه ؛ فإنَّ الخيالَ نفسَهُ لا يدخل في الخيال ، والرؤية لا تدخل في الخيال ، وكذا العلم والقدرة ، وكذا الصوت والرائحةُ ، ولو كلّفَ الوهم أن يتحقَّقَ ذات الصوت .. لقدَّر له لوناً و مقداراً ، وتصوَّرَهُ كذلك . وهكذا جميع أحوال النفس ؛ من الخجل ، والوَجل ، والعشق ، والغضب ، والفرح ، والحزن ، والعجب ، فمن يدرك بالضرورة هذه من نفسه ، ويسومُ خيالَهُ أن يتحقَّقَ ذاتَ هذه الأحوال ، فيجده يقصُرُ عنه إلا بتقدير خطا ، ثم يُنكِرُ مع ذلك وجود موجود لا يدخل في خياله . فهذا سبيل كشف الغطاء عن المسألة.
If the opponent says: 'What is not imagined in the imagination has no existence?' Then let us judge that the imagination itself has no existence in itself; for imagination itself does not enter into imagination, and vision does not enter into imagination, and likewise knowledge and power, and likewise sound and smell. And if the imagination were tasked with realizing the essence of sound, it would assign to it a color and a quantity, and would imagine it thus. And likewise all states of the soul: from shame, fear, love, anger, joy, sorrow, and wonder. Whoever perceives these [states] necessarily in himself, and asks his imagination to realize the essence of these states, finds that it falls short of it except by a false estimation. Then despite that, he denies the existence of an existent that does not enter into his imagination. This is the way to unveil the matter.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer. The opponent argues that whatever cannot be imagined does not exist. The author refutes this with counterexamples: imagination itself cannot be imagined (as a mental picture), nor can the act of vision, knowledge, power, sound, smell, or internal emotions like shame, fear, love, anger, etc. Yet we know these exist through direct experience. If we forced the imagination to picture sound, it would falsely give it color and size. Thus the argument that existence is limited to the imaginable is self-defeating.

Key Terms
خيال
رؤية
علم
قدرة
صوت
رائحة
أحوال النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Something must be imaginable to exist.
Correction: Many things exist that cannot be pictured, such as mental states, faculties, and sensory qualities.

Checkpoint

B) The sense of smell
C) The color red
D) The number five

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that existence is not limited to what can be imagined, as many real things (like emotions and senses) are unimaginable.

Refuting the Claim: Non-Imaginable Means Non-Existent
Ghazali refutes the opponent's claim that non-imaginable things do not exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that existence is not limited to what can be imagined, and how he uses examples from self-evident realities to refute the opponent's claim.

Core Idea
What cannot be imagined still exists; even the imagination itself cannot be imagined.

This passage presents Ghazali's response to an opponent who denies God's existence because God cannot be imagined. Ghazali begins by clarifying that if the opponent means God is not known rationally (ma'qul), that is false. Then he directly tackles the claim: non-imaginable things do not exist. He offers a powerful refutation by pointing out that many undeniable realities—like the imagination itself, vision, knowledge, and inner states of the soul—cannot be imagined. This lesson will walk through his argument and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the objection-answer structure.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
What exactly is the claim that Ghazali is refuting? It's that whatever cannot be imagined does not exist.
3
See Ghazali's examples
He lists several things that exist but are not imaginable: imagination itself, vision, knowledge, power, sound, smell, and states like shame, fear, love, anger. Find them in the text.
These are self-evident existents that are not imaginable in the sense required.
4
Understand the fallacy
The opponent confuses 'imaginable' with 'existent'. Ghazali shows that existence is broader than what the imagination can picture.
This is key to the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد صدقت ؛ فإنه لا يدخل في التصور والوهم والخيال إلا جسم له لون وقدر ، فالمنفت اللون والقدر لا يتصوره الخيالُ ، فإنَّ الخيال قد أَنِسَ بالمبصرات ، فلا يتوهم الشيء إلا على وفق ما رآه ، ولا يستطيع أن يتوهم ما لا يوافقه. وإن أراد الخصم أنه ليس بمعقول ؛ أي: ليس بمعلوم بدليل العقل .. فهو محالٌ ؛ إذ قدَّمنا الدليل على ثبوته ، ولا معنى للمعقول إلا ما اضطر العقل إلى الإذعان والتصديق به بموجب الدليل الذي لا يمكن مخالفته ، وقد تحقق هذا تحريرًا، فما لا يتصوره الخيال لا وجود له ؟ فإن قال الخصم : فما لا يُتصوّر في الخيال لا وجود له ؟ قلنا : فما لا يتصوره الخيال غير موجود بل موجود ؛ فإنَّ الخيال نفسَهُ لا يدخل في الخيال ، والرؤية لا تدخل في الخيال ، وكذا العلم والقدرة ، وكذا الصوت والرائحةُ ، ولو كلّفَ الوهم أن يتحقَّقَ ذات الصوت .. لقدَّر له لوناً و مقداراً ، وتصوَّرَهُ كذلك. وهكذا جميع أحوال النفس ؛ من الخجل ، والوَجل ، والعشق ، والغضب ، والفرح ، والحزن ، والعجب ، فمن يدرك بالضرورة هذه من نفسه ، ويسومُ خيالَهُ أن يتحقَّقَ ذاتَ هذه الأحوال ، فيجده يقصُرُ عنه إلا بتقدير خطا ، ثم يُنكِرُ مع ذلك وجود موجود لا يدخل في خياله. فهذا سبيل كشف الغطاء عن المسألة. وقد جاوزنا حد الاختصار ، ولكنَّ المعتقدات المختصرة في هذا الفن أراها مشتملة على الإطناب في الواضحات ، والشروع في الزيادات الخارجة عن المهمات ، مع التساهل في مضائق الإشكالات ، فرأيتُ نقل الإطناب من مظان الوضوح إلى مواقع الغموض أهم وأولى.
You have spoken truly; for nothing enters imagination, fancy, and estimation except a body having color and magnitude. What denies color and magnitude is not imagined by the imagination, for the imagination has become familiar with visible things, so it does not fancy a thing except in accordance with what it has seen, and it cannot fancy what does not conform to it. And if the opponent means that it is not 'ma'qul' (rational/intelligible), i.e., not known by a rational proof, then that is impossible; for we have already presented the proof of its existence, and the meaning of 'ma'qul' is nothing but what the intellect is compelled to assent to and affirm by virtue of a proof which cannot be opposed, and this has been established. So, what is not imagined in the mind, does it not exist? If the opponent says: 'What is not imagined in the imagination does not exist?' We say: Rather, what is not imagined by the imagination is not non-existent but existent; for the imagination itself does not enter the imagination, and vision does not enter the imagination, and likewise knowledge and power, and likewise sound and smell. If one were to task the fancy to realize the essence of sound, it would assign it a color and a magnitude and imagine it thus. And likewise all states of the soul: shame, fear, love, anger, joy, sorrow, and wonder. Whoever perceives these in himself necessarily, and tasks his imagination to realize the essence of these states, he finds it fall short except by estimating a representation, and then he denies the existence of an existent that does not enter his imagination. This is the way of unveiling the matter. And we have exceeded the limit of brevity, but I see that the abridged creedal works in this art are full of verbosity on obvious matters and beginning on additions beyond the essentials, along with negligence regarding the narrow points of difficulties. So I considered that transferring verbosity from places of clarity to places of obscurity is more important and more appropriate.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. Ghazali's opponent has argued that God cannot exist because He is not imaginable. Ghazali first clarifies a point: if the opponent means that God is not 'ma'qul' (known by rational proof), that is false because proof has been given. Then he addresses the real claim: that what is not imaginable does not exist. Ghazali refutes this by pointing out that many existing things are not imaginable: the imagination itself, vision, knowledge, power, sound, smell, and all inner states like shame, fear, love, anger, etc. If one tries to imagine these, one inevitably gives them a color and magnitude, which is not their true nature. So the opponent's denial of God based on lack of imaginability is invalid. He concludes by noting that he has gone beyond brevity, but that it is necessary to clarify such confusions.

وهم
معقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that if something cannot be imagined, it cannot exist.
Correction: Ghazali shows that many existents, including the imagination itself and states of the soul, are not imaginable but undoubtedly exist.

Checkpoint

B) Many things exist that are not imaginable, such as the imagination itself and states of the soul.
C) God is actually imaginable in a human form.
D) The opponent failed to prove that God is not imaginable.

Key Takeaway
Existence is not limited to what is imaginable; many self-evident realities are beyond imagination, so the opponent's claim fails.

?

?
