God's Visibility: Definition and Rational Proof
Ghazali defines visibility and gives a rational proof for its possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of visibility as a potential property of every existent essence, the distinction between rational possibility and revealed occurrence, and the rational proof for God's visibility.

Core Idea
Visibility follows existence, not physicality.

Imam al-Ghazali tackles a challenging question: can God be seen? He first clarifies what 'visibility' means—not actual sight but potential visibility inherent in existence itself. Then he provides a rational argument that establishes its possibility without relying on revelation. This lesson walks through his definition, the crucial distinction between possibility and occurrence, and the proof that vision is a type of knowledge that does not imply change or origination.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's definition of visibility
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice how Ghazali defines visibility as a potential based on existence alone, not on action or attributes.
He uses an analogy: water in the river is potentially quenching.
2
Understand the two aspects: possibility and occurrence
Ghazali separates the inquiry into rational possibility (which reason can prove) and actual occurrence (only known from revelation). This is key to his method.
3
Follow the rational proof step by step
The proof argues that anything true of an existent without implying origination or contradicting divine attributes can be attributed to God. Knowledge is such a thing; vision is a type of knowledge; therefore vision is possible for God.
The proof uses analogy between knowledge and vision.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Some think visibility implies a body or direction. Ghazali's definition based on existence alone refutes this. Reflect on why this is important for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أنه تعالى عندنا مرئيٌّ لوجودِهِ ووجود ذاته وليس ذلك إلا لذاته فإنه ليس لفعله ولا لصفة من الصفات بل كلُّ موجود ذات فواجب أن يكون مرئياً كما أنه واجب أن يكون معلوماً ولست أعني به أنه واجب أن يكون معلوماً ومرئياً بالفعل بل بالقوة أي هو من حيث ذاته مستعد لأن تتعلق الرؤية به وأنه لا مانع ولا محيل في ذاته له فإن امتنع وجود الرؤية فلأمرٍ آخر خارج عن ذاته كما تقول الماءُ الذي في النهر مرو والخمرُ الذي في الدَّنّ مسكر وليس كذلك فإنه يسكر ويروي عند الشرب ولكن معناه أنَّ ذاته مستعدة لذلك فإذا فهمت المراد منه فالنظر في طرفين أحدهما في الجواز العقلي والثاني في الوقوع الذي لا سبيل إلى دركه إلا بالشرع ومهما دل الشرع على وقوعه فقد دل لا محالة على جوازه ولكنا ندلُّ بمسلكين واقعين عقليين على جوازه الأول هو أنا نقولُ إن الباري تعالى موجود وذات وله ثبوت وحقيقة وإنما يخالف سائر الموجودات في استحالة كونه حادثاً أو موصوفاً بما يدلُّ على الحدوث أو موصوفاً بصفة تناقض صفات الإلهية من العلم والقدرة وغيرهما فكلُّ ما يصح لموجود فهو يصح في حقه إن لم يدل على الحدوث ولم يناقض صفة من صفاته والدليل عليه تعلُّق العلم به فإنه لما لم يؤد ذلك إلى تغير في ذاته ولا إلى مناقضة صفاته ولا إلى الدلالة على الحدوث سوي بينه وبين الأجسام والأعراض في جواز تعلق العلم بذاته وصفاته والرؤية نوع علم لا يوجب تعلقه بالمرئي تغير صفة ولا يدلُّ على الحدوث فوجبَ الحكم بها على كل موجود
And the second: that He, exalted, is visible to us because of His existence and the existence of His essence, and that is only due to His essence, for it is not due to His action nor to any attribute among the attributes. Rather, every existent that is an essence must be visible, just as it must be known. And I do not mean by this that it must be known and visible in actuality, but rather in potentiality; that is, by its essence it is disposed for vision to be related to it, and there is no preventing or impeding factor in its essence. If the existence of vision is impossible, it is for some other reason external to its essence, as you say: water in the river is quenching, and wine in the cask is intoxicating, but it is not like that; for it intoxicates and quenches when drunk, but the meaning is that its essence is disposed for that. So when you understand the intended meaning, the inquiry is in two aspects: one is concerning rational possibility, and the second is concerning occurrence, which cannot be grasped except by revelation. And whenever revelation indicates its occurrence, it indicates – necessarily – its possibility. But we indicate by two rational methods its possibility. The first is: we say that the Creator, exalted, exists and is an essence, and He has subsistence and reality. He only differs from other existents in the impossibility of His being originated, or being described by what indicates origination, or being described by an attribute that contradicts the attributes of divinity – of knowledge, power, and others. So whatever is correct for an existent is correct in His regard, if it does not indicate origination and does not contradict any of His attributes. The proof for this is the relation of knowledge to Him; for since that did not lead to a change in His essence, nor to contradiction of His attributes, nor to indication of origination, equality was made between Him and bodies and accidents in the permissibility of knowledge relating to His essence and attributes. And vision is a type of knowledge; its relation to the visible does not necessitate a change in attribute, nor does it indicate origination. So the judgment of it is obligatory upon every existent.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of God's visibility and provides a rational proof for its possibility. It begins by stating that God is visible to us not because of any action or attribute, but solely because of His existence as an essence. Every existent essence is potentially visible, just as it is potentially knowable. Potential visibility does not require actual vision; it only means the essence has no intrinsic impediment to being seen. If vision does not occur, it must be due to an external cause, just as water in the river is potentially quenching even when not drunk. The passage then distinguishes two aspects: rational possibility (which reason can establish) and actual occurrence (which only revelation can confirm). Revelation proving occurrence also proves possibility, but the author wants to provide a rational argument for possibility. The argument proceeds: God exists as an essence with reality; He differs from other existents only in being eternal, not originated, and in possessing divine attributes. Any property that applies to an existent without implying origination or contradicting divine attributes can be applied to God. Knowledge applies to God without such implications; therefore, vision, as a type of knowledge, also applies. Just as knowledge relates to something without changing it or implying temporality, so does vision. Hence, vision is possible for every existent, including God.

Key Terms
مرئي (visible)
رؤية (vision)
جواز عقلي (rational possibility)
وقوع (occurrence)
ذات (essence)
بالقوة (potentially)
علم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That visibility of God implies He has a body or is located in a direction.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that visibility is based on existence alone, not on physicality. Potential visibility does not require a body; it only requires that the essence has no intrinsic impediment to being seen.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what makes God potentially visible?
A) His actions
B) His attributes
C) His existence as an essence
D) His location in a direction

Key Takeaway
Visibility is a potential property of every existent essence, and reason can prove its possibility for God by analogy with knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that God is potentially visible to you. But do you live today as if you are already seen by Him, watching your every thought and action? If vision is a type of knowledge, then consider: what would change in your life if you truly believed that God sees you and knows you completely—right now, without any veil?
?
Ghazali says that God’s essence has no obstacle to being seen. But the real obstacle is often in us: our hearts are blind. Reflect: what ‘manīʿ’ (preventing factor) in your own soul blocks you from experiencing the reality of God’s presence? What would it take for you to remove that barrier so that your faith becomes as certain as sight?
?
If vision is a relation that does not change God, what does it say about you that you desire to see Him? Is your longing for God’s vision a sign of love or a sign of lack? And if you truly want to see Him, what steps are you taking now to purify your heart so that you can be among those who will see Him in the hereafter?

Refuting the Direction Objection: Ghazali's Argument
Ghazali refutes the objection that visibility implies direction, using a comparison to anthropomorphists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the claim that God's visibility requires a physical direction, by exposing the fallacy of generalizing from sensory experience.

Core Idea
Visibility does not imply direction; the objection is an empirical overgeneralization.

The objector argues that if God is visible, He must be in a direction relative to the seer, which leads to an impossible consequence (being a substance or accident). Ghazali shows that the crucial premise—that visibility entails direction—is neither known by necessity nor proven by reflection. He then draws a parallel to anthropomorphists who argue from limited experience, revealing the weakness of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection syllogism
Read the objector's argument carefully: if visible then direction, if direction then substance or accident, which is impossible. The conclusion is that visibility is impossible.
This syllogism has two premises and a conclusion.
2
Note Ghazali's concession and denial
Ghazali concedes the second premise (direction leads to impossibility) but denies the first premise (visibility implies direction). Understand why this is a strategic move.
The burden of proof shifts to the objector to prove the first premise.
3
Examine the critique of necessity and proof
Ghazali asks whether the first premise is known by necessity or by reflection. He argues it is not necessary, and the objector's only proof is empirical: they have never seen a visible thing without direction. This is weak.
Empirical generalization cannot establish a universal rational necessity.
4
Follow the analogy with anthropomorphists
Ghazali shows that if such empirical reasoning were valid, anthropomorphists could prove God is a body or must occupy space. This exposes the fallacy.
The analogy highlights the inconsistency of the objector's method.
5
Reflect on the core fallacy
The ultimate mistake is judging the unseen by the seen. Ghazali compares it to someone who knows only bodies and denies accidents.
This principle is crucial in kalam: absence of experience does not prove impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل كونه مرئياً يوجبُ كونه بجهة وكونه بجهة يوجب كونه عرضاً يثبت له الجهة أو جوهراً وهو محال ونظم القياس أنه إن كان مرئياً فهو بجهة من الرائي وهذا اللازم محال فالمفضي إلى الرؤية محال قلنا أحد الأصلين من هذا القياس مسلَّم لكم وهو أن هذا اللازم محال ولكنَّ الأصل الأوّل وهو ادعاء هذا اللازم على اعتقاد الرؤية ممنوع فنقول لمَ قلتم إنَّه إن كان مرئياً فهو بجهة من الرائي أعلمتموه ضرورة أم بنظر ولا سبيل إلى دعوى الضرورة وأما النظر فلا بد من بيانه ومنتهاهم أنهم لم يروا إلى الآن شيئاً إلا وكان بجهة من الرائي مخصوصة فيقال وما لم يُرَ فلا يحكم باستحالته ولو جاز هذا للمجسمي أن يقول إنه تعالى جسم لأنه فاعل فإننا لم نر إلى الآن فاعلاً إلا جسماً أو يقول إن كان فاعلاً وموجوداً فهو إما داخل العالم وإما خارجه وإما متصل وإما منفصل ولا تخلو عنه الجهات الست فإنه لم نعلم موجوداً إلا وهو كذلك فلا فضل بينكم وبين هؤلاء وحاصله يرجع إلى الحكم بأنَّ ما شوهد وعلم ينبغي ألا يعلم غيره إلا على وفقه وهو كمن يعلمُ الجسم وينكرُ العرض ويقول لو كان
For if it is said: His being visible necessitates His being in a direction, and His being in a direction necessitates His being an accident to which direction is attributed or a substance, and that is impossible. And the formulation of the syllogism: if He is visible, then He is in a direction relative to the seer, and this necessary consequent is impossible, so what leads to vision is impossible. We say: one of the two premises of this syllogism is conceded to you – that this necessary consequent is impossible – but the first premise, which is the claim of this necessary consequent upon the belief in vision, is denied. So we say: why do you say that if He is visible then He is in a direction relative to the seer? Do you know it by necessity or by reflection? There is no way to claim necessity. As for reflection, it must be explained. Their ultimate reasoning is that they have not seen anything up to now except that it was in a specific direction relative to the seer. So it is said: what has not been seen should not be judged impossible. And if this were permissible, then the anthropomorphist would be allowed to say that God, exalted, is a body because He is an agent, for we have not seen up to now an agent except a body. Or he would say: if He is an agent and existent, then He is either inside the world or outside it, either connected or disconnected, and cannot be free from the six directions, for we do not know an existent that is not like that. So there is no superiority between you and these. The gist of it returns to the judgment that what has been witnessed and known should not be known otherwise except in accordance with it. And it is like one who knows a body and denies an accident, and says: if it were
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection against the rational possibility of seeing God. The objector argues: if God is visible, He must be in a direction relative to the seer; being in a direction implies being either an accident (which subsists in direction) or a substance; both are impossible for God. Therefore, visibility is impossible. The refutation proceeds: we concede that the consequent (being in a direction) is impossible, but we deny the first premise – that visibility necessarily implies direction. The objector claims this implication is known either by necessity or by rational proof. It is not necessary, for no intellectual necessity dictates that every visible must be in a direction. As for proof, they only rely on empirical observation: everything they have ever seen was in a direction. This is a weak argument, for what has not been experienced cannot be deemed impossible. Ghazali draws a parallel: if such reasoning were valid, then anthropomorphists could prove God is a body because all agents they have seen are bodies, or that God must occupy one of the six directions because all existents they know do so. This shows the fallacy: generalizing from limited experience to an absolute rule. The ultimate flaw is judging that unseen things must conform to seen things. This is like someone who knows only bodies and denies the existence of accidents, refusing to accept any reality beyond his limited observation.

Key Terms
جهة (direction)
عرض (accident)
جوهر (substance)
قياس (syllogism)
ضرورة (necessity)
نظر (reflection)
مجسم (anthropomorphist)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visibility of an object necessarily implies that object has a physical direction relative to the observer.
Correction: Ghazali argues that this is not a necessary truth; it is a generalization from observed objects, which cannot be applied to the unseen divine being.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali refute the claim that visibility implies direction?
A) He accepts the implication and argues direction is possible for God
B) He denies that visibility necessarily implies direction, calling the premise unproven
C) He argues that God is a substance
D) He says vision is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the direction objection by denying that visibility logically entails direction and exposing the fallacy of arguing from limited experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that God must be like something you have experienced, even though you know He is beyond creation? Where in your life does this assumption creep in?
?
This lesson shows that our lack of experience does not prove impossibility. What is one area where you have ruled out God's involvement because you haven't seen it happen? Will you now leave that door open?
?
If you were to truly internalize that God is not bound by the limitations of your experience, what fear or expectation about Him would you need to let go of today?

The Mirror Analogy: Vision Without Direct Opposition
Ghazali uses the mirror example to prove vision without direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the mirror analogy to prove that vision can occur without spatial opposition or direction.

Core Idea
Vision does not require facing the object—the mirror proves this.

Imam al-Ghazali presents a striking analogy to counter the Mu'tazilite claim that seeing Allah would require spatial opposition. He uses the common experience of seeing oneself in a mirror to show that vision can happen without direct facing. This passage walks through the logical steps of that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the logical structure: claim, objection, refutation, elimination, conclusion.
2
Identify the core claim
Ghazali asserts that the mirror example shows vision without opposition. Identify the initial statement and the Mu'tazilite objection.
The objection claims the image is on the mirror's surface.
3
Follow the refutation by distance
Ghazali points out that the image appears at a distance behind the mirror, which cannot be on its thin surface. Understand why this refutes the objection.
The distance (e.g., two cubits) contradicts the negligible thickness of the mirror.
4
Trace the elimination of locations
The image is not behind, to the right, left, above, or below the mirror. Verify each elimination based on the text.
The six directions of the mirror are all considered and dismissed.
5
Conclude with the necessary conclusion
The only remaining location is the viewer's own body, proving that vision occurs without spatial opposition.
Thus, direction (jiha) is not necessary for vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَنْكَرَ مِنْهُمْ .. فَلَا يَقْدِرُ عَلَى إِنْكَارِ رُؤْيَةِ الْإِنْسَانِ نَفْسَهُ فِي الْمِرْآةِ : مِثَالٌ لِتَوْضِيحِ إِمْكَانٍ وَمَعْلُومٍ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي مُقَابَلَةِ نَفْسِهِ، فَإِنْ زَعَمُوا أَنَّهُ لَا يَرَى نَفْسَهُ، وَإِنَّمَا يَرَى صُورَةً مُحَاكِيَةً لِصُورَتِهِ، مُنْطَبِعَةً فِي ظَاهِرِ الْمِرْآةِ انْطِبَاعَ النَّقْشِ فِي الْحَائِطِ .. فَيُقَالُ : إِنَّ هَذَا الْقَوْلَ ظَاهِرُ الِاسْتِحَالَةِ؛ فَإِنَّ مَنْ تَبَاعَدَ عَنْ مِرْآةٍ مَنْصُوبَةٍ فِي حَائِطٍ بِقَدْرِ ذِرَاعَيْنِ .. يَرَى صُورَتَهُ بَعِيدَةً عَنْ جِرْمِ الْمِرْآةِ بِذِرَاعَيْنِ، وَإِنْ تَبَاعَدَ بِثَلَاثَةِ أَذْرُعٍ .. فَكَذَلِكَ، وَالْبَعِيدُ عَنِ الْمِرْآةِ بِذِرَاعَيْنِ كَيْفَ يَكُونُ مُنْطَبِعاً فِي الْمِرْآةِ وَسُمْكُ الْمِرْآةِ رُبَّمَا لَا يَزِيدُ عَلَى سُمْكِ شَعِيرَةٍ ؟! فَإِنْ كَانَتِ الصُّورَةُ فِي شَيْءٍ وَرَاءَ الْمِرْآةِ .. فَهُوَ مُحَالٌ؛ إِذْ لَيْسَ وَرَاءَ الْمِرْآةِ إِلَّا جِدَارٌ أَوْ هَوَاءٌ أَوْ شَخْصٌ آخَرُ وَهُوَ مَحْجُوبٌ عَنْهُ، وَهُوَ لَا يَرَاهُ، وَكَذَا عَنْ يَمِينِ الْمِرْآةِ، وَعَنْ يَسَارِهَا، وَفَوْقَهَا، وَتَحْتَهَا، وَجِهَاتِ الْمِرْآةِ سِتٌّ، وَهُوَ يَرَى صُورَةً بَعِيدَةً عَنِ الْمِرْآةِ بِذِرَاعَيْنِ، فَلْتُطْلَبْ هَذِهِ الصُّورَةُ مِنْ جَوَانِبِ الْمِرْآةِ، فَحَيْثُ وُجِدَتْ فَهُوَ الْمَرْئِيُّ، وَلَا وُجُودَ لِمِثْلِ هَذِهِ الصُّورَةِ الْمَرْئِيَّةِ فِي الْأَجْسَامِ الْمُحِيطَةِ بِالْمِرْآةِ إِلَّا فِي جِسْمِ النَّاظِرِ .. فَهُوَ الْمَرْئِيُّ إِذَنْ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، وَقَدْ بَطَلَتِ الْمُقَابَلَةُ وَالْجِهَةُ.
And whoever among them denies it... cannot deny a person's vision of himself in a mirror: an example to clarify the possibility, and it is known that he is not facing himself. If they claim that he does not see himself, but rather sees an image resembling his form, impressed on the surface of the mirror like the impress of an engraving on a wall... then it is said: This statement is clearly impossible. For if someone distances himself from a mirror fixed in a wall by two cubits, he sees his image two cubits away from the body of the mirror; and if he distances himself by three cubits, likewise. How can something distant from the mirror by two cubits be impressed in the mirror when the thickness of the mirror perhaps does not exceed the thickness of a grain of barley? If the image were in something behind the mirror, that is impossible, for there is nothing behind the mirror but a wall or air or another person, and it is hidden from him and he does not see it. Likewise to the right, left, above, and below the mirror—the directions of the mirror are six—and he sees an image distant from the mirror by two cubits. Let this image be sought from the sides of the mirror: wherever it is found, that is the visible object. But no such visible image exists in the bodies surrounding the mirror except in the body of the viewer. Therefore, it is the visible object by necessity, and opposition and direction are refuted.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy (tamthīl) demonstrating that vision can occur without spatial opposition (muqābala) and direction (jiha). Ghazali presents the mirror example: a person sees himself in a mirror without facing himself. The Mu'tazila might object that what is seen is a copy of the form impressed on the mirror's surface. Ghazali refutes this by noting that the image appears at a distance (e.g., two cubits behind the mirror), but the mirror's thickness is negligible, so the image cannot be on its surface. It cannot be behind the mirror either because that space contains a wall, air, or another person. It is not to the left, right, above, or below. The only place the image could actually exist is in the viewer's own body, since the viewer perceives the form in himself. Thus, the image is not in the mirror or outside; vision occurs without direct opposition. Hence, the necessity of direction for seeing God is disproven.

Key Terms
مِرْآة (mirror)
صُورَة (image/form)
مُقَابَلَة (opposition/facing)
جِهَة (direction)
رُؤْيَة (vision)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The image seen in a mirror is actually on the mirror's surface.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the apparent depth of the image cannot be on the thin mirror surface, so it must be in the viewer.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's mirror analogy, where does the image seen in the mirror actually exist?
A) On the surface of the mirror
B) Behind the mirror
C) In the body of the viewer
D) In the surrounding air

Key Takeaway
Vision does not require spatial opposition or direction, as demonstrated by the mirror analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your vision of yourself in the mirror is you, not a copy. Where in your life have you been treating your own actions—your prayer, your charity, your worship—as mere copies or performances rather than your true self? What would it mean to accept that they are genuinely yours, even if you are not 'facing' them directly?
?
The proof frees vision from the need for a specific direction. Do you still feel that God is only present when you are facing a certain way (e.g., the qibla) or in a certain place? How might this lesson challenge you to live with the awareness that God can be perceived without spatial limitations—and are you ready to make that shift today?
?
This argument shows that what is impossible (like an image floating behind a thin mirror) forces us to reconsider our assumptions. Is there a belief or habit in your spiritual life that you hold as necessary, yet this passage suggests it is not? Will you reexamine it now, or cling to comfort?

Defining Ru'ya: Vision as Intellectual Unveiling
Ghazali defines ru'ya as an intensified intellectual perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defines ru'ya as an intensified intellectual perception, not sensory sight, and why this is not rationally impossible.

Core Idea
Ru'ya is a higher form of knowledge, not physical vision.

Ghazali begins by clarifying what is meant by 'seeing' Allah. He argues that if a deeper unveiling of knowledge is possible, it can appropriately be called 'vision', just as we call imaginative perception vision. This sets the stage for proving its rational possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of vision
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Ghazali defines 'vision' (ru'ya) in terms of intellectual unveiling (kashf) and completion (istikmal). Note the analogy with imagination.
2
Understand the analogy
Notice that Ghazali says we already call imaginative perception 'vision'. So by extension, a higher intellectual perception can also be called 'vision'.
This is a key conceptual move.
3
Grasp the rational possibility
Ghazali asserts that such completion is not impossible for non-material realities like knowledge and power, nor for Allah's essence. In fact, the mind naturally demands it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان ذلك ممكناً . . سمينا ذلك الكشف والاستكمال بالإضافة إلى العلم : رؤيةً ؛ كما سميناه بالإضافة إلى التخيل : رؤية ، ومعلوم أن تقدير هذا الاستكمال في الاستيضاح والاستكشاف غير محال في الموجودات المعلومة التي ليست متخيَّلَةٌ ؛ كالعلم والقدرة وغيرهما ، وكذا في ذات الله تعالى وصفاته ، بل نكاد ندرك ضرورة من الطبع : أنَّه يتقاضى طلب مزيد استيضاح في ذات الله تعالى وصفاته، وفي ذوات هذه المعاني المعلومات كلها فنحن نقول : إن ذلك غير محال ؛ فإنَّه لا مُحيلَ له ، بل العقل دليل على إمكانه ، بل على استدعاء الطبع له ، إلا أنَّ هذا الكمال في الكشف غيرُ مبذول في هذا العالم ، والنفسُ في شغل البدن وكدورة صفاته ، فهو بسببه محجوب عنه وكما لا يبعد أن يكون الجفنُ أو السِّتْرُ أو سواد ما في العين سبباً ـ بحكم اطراد العادة - لامتناع الإبصار للمتخيلات .. فلا يبعد أن تكون كدورة النفس وتراكم حجب الأشغال ـ بحكم اطراد العادة ـ مانعاً من إيصار المعلومات فإذا بُعثِرَ ما في القبور ، وحصل ما في الصدور ، وزكت القلوب بالشراب الطهور ، وصفيت بأنواع التصفية والتنقية .. لم يمتنع أن يستعد بسببها لمزيد استكمال واستيضاح في ذات الله تعالى ؛ إذ في سائر المعلومات : يكون ارتفاع درجته عن العلم المعهود كارتفاع درجة الإبصار عن التخيل ؛ فيعبر عن ذلك بلقاء الله تعالى ، أو مشاهدته ، أو رؤيته ، أو الرؤية إبصاره، أو ما شئت من العبارات ؛ فلا مُشاحَّةَ فيها بعد إيضاح المعاني وإذا كان ذلك ممكناً : فإن خُلقت هذه الحالة في العين .. كان اسم الرؤية بحكم وضع اللغة عليه أصدق ، وخلقه في العين غير مستحيل ، كما أن خلقه في القلب غيرُ مستحيل ، فإذا فهم المراد بما أطلقه أهل الحقِّ من الرؤية .. عُلم أنّ العقل لا يحيله ، بل يوجبه ، فإنَّ الشرع قد شهد له ؛ فلا يبقى للمنازعة وجة إلا على سبيل العناد ، أو المشاحَّةِ في إطلاق عبارة الرؤية ، أو القصور عن دركِ هذه المعاني الدقيقة التي ذكرناها ، ولنقتصر في هذا الموجز على هذا القدر
If that is possible... we call that unveiling and completion in relation to knowledge a 'vision'; just as we called it in relation to imagination a 'vision'. And it is known that the estimation of this completion in clarification and unveiling is not impossible regarding known existents that are not imaginary, such as knowledge, power, and others, and likewise regarding the essence of Allah Most High and His attributes. Rather, we almost perceive a necessity from nature that it demands seeking more clarification regarding the essence of Allah Most High and His attributes, and regarding the essences of all these known meanings. So we say: that is not impossible; for there is nothing to render it impossible. Rather, the intellect is evidence for its possibility, rather for the natural demand of it. However, this perfection in unveiling is not granted in this world, and the soul is occupied with the body and the turbidity of its qualities, so because of this it is veiled from it. And just as it is not improbable that the eyelid or the curtain or the blackness of the eye may be a cause—by the continuity of habit—for preventing sight of imaginable things... so it is not improbable that the turbidity of the soul and the accumulation of the veils of occupations—by the continuity of habit—prevent insight into the known. So when what is in the graves is scattered, and what is in the breasts is obtained, and the hearts are purified with the pure drink, and cleansed with various kinds of purification and refinement... it is not impossible that they become prepared thereby for more completion and clarification regarding the essence of Allah Most High; for with respect to all other known things, the elevation of its degree above ordinary knowledge is like the elevation of the degree of sight above imagination. So that is expressed as the meeting of Allah Most High, or beholding Him, or seeing Him, or the vision of seeing Him, or whatever expressions you wish; there is no quibbling over them after clarifying the meanings. And if that is possible: if this state is created in the eye, then the name 'vision' (ru'ya) by the convention of language is truer for it, and its creation in the eye is not impossible, just as its creation in the heart is not impossible. So if the intended meaning of what the people of truth have released regarding 'vision' (ru'ya) is understood... it is known that the intellect does not deem it impossible, rather it necessitates it, for the Law has testified to it. So there remains no room for dispute except by way of obstinacy, or quibbling over the employment of the expression 'vision' (ru'ya), or inadequacy in comprehending these subtle meanings which we have mentioned. And we will confine ourselves in this summary to this extent.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). Ghazali begins by defining a higher form of knowledge, which he calls 'unveiling and completion' (al-kashf wa al-istikmal). He notes that we already call imaginative perception a kind of 'vision', so analogously, an intensified intellectual perception can also be called 'vision'. He argues that it is not impossible for existent non-material realities (like knowledge, power) to have further clarification; similarly, for Allah's essence and attributes, the mind naturally seeks more clarity. This demand shows possibility, not impossibility. He then explains that in this worldly life, the soul's attachment to the body and its turbidity veil us from this higher vision. Just as physical obstructions (eyelid, curtain) prevent sight by habitual causation, so do spiritual turbidities prevent insight. After death and purification, the soul becomes prepared for this higher vision. The difference between ordinary knowledge and this vision is like the difference between imagination and sight. Hence, terms like 'meeting', 'beholding', 'seeing' are permissible. If such a state were created in the eye (as a physical organ), it would be called 'vision' (ru'ya) properly; but even if created in the heart, it is still not impossible. Once the meaning is understood, reason confirms it is not only possible but also necessitated by the Law (revealed texts). So disputes are only due to obstinacy, terminological quibbling, or intellectual inadequacy. Ghazali concludes this section briefly.

Key Terms
رؤية
كشف
استكمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ru'ya means seeing Allah with physical eyes like seeing a physical object.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that ru'ya is an intellectual unveiling, not sensory sight.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the basis for calling intellectual unveiling 'vision'?
A) because it involves the physical eye
B) because it is analogous to imaginative perception which is also called vision
C) because it is a metaphor without basis
D) because the Law requires it

Key Takeaway
Ghazali redefines 'vision' of Allah as a rational, not sensory, concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt a genuine, restless demand in your nature to know Allah more deeply, or have you been content with a superficial knowledge? What concrete step will you take today to seek that unveiling, even if only for five minutes of sincere reflection?
?
If Ghazali is right that the intellect itself demands more clarity about Allah's essence and attributes, what does it say about your own intellect if you ignore that demand? What worldly distraction are you using to silence that natural call?
?
You believe it is possible to have a deeper, intellectual 'vision' of Allah—truths beyond imagination. What will you do tomorrow to act on that belief, rather than merely assent to it? Name one habit you will change.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
7 min
The Rational Proof for the Possibility of Seeing Allah
Ghazali proves ru'ya is possible and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that seeing Allah is not only possible but also demanded by reason and confirmed by revelation, and why it is not realized in this world.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is rationally possible and spiritually attainable in the afterlife.

Ghazali now explains why this vision is not realized in this world: the soul is veiled by bodily attachments. He draws an analogy with physical obstructions to sight. After purification, the soul becomes prepared, and reason affirms its possibility, supported by the Law.

Guided Path
1
Understand the worldly veil
Read how Ghazali describes the soul's turbidity and occupations as veils that prevent this higher vision in this world.
Compare with physical obstructions to sight.
2
Analyze the analogy
Notice the analogy: just as an eyelid or curtain prevents physical sight by habitual causation, so does soul-turbidity prevent insight.
3
Consider the afterlife purification
Ghazali explains that after death and purification, the soul becomes prepared for this vision.
4
Conclude with reason and law
See how Ghazali concludes that reason necessitates the possibility and the Law has testified, leaving no room for dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ghazali says that after purification, the soul becomes prepared for a vision far beyond ordinary knowledge. If you truly believed that seeing Allah is possible for you in the afterlife, how would you change your priorities today? What would you start purifying that you have neglected?
?
You may have heard that seeing Allah is impossible, but this lesson shows it is not. Is there any fear or doubt in your heart that prevents you from longing for this vision? How can you cultivate that longing right now?

The Three Considerations: Wajib, Muhal, and Mumkin
Ghazali defines the three considerations for existence: necessary, impossible, possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the same contingent thing can be considered necessary, impossible, or possible depending on whether divine will is attached, absent, or disregarded.

Core Idea
A thing's necessity, impossibility, or possibility depends on the consideration of divine will.

Ghazali presents a key distinction in Islamic theology: the three rational judgments that apply to any contingent thing relative to God's will. Before this, we have only considered existence in general. Now we see how a single entity can be described in three different ways depending on the perspective. This passage lays the foundation for understanding divine attributes and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own reasoning come through before you interpret.
Notice how Ghazali uses a conditional structure for impossibility.
2
Identify the three considerations
Find the three conditions: existence of will (wajib), absence of will (muhal), and disregarding will (mumkin). Notice how each is defined in relation to the will.
3
Understand the nuance
Ghazali clarifies that a thing can be possible in itself but impossible due to an external factor. Avoid thinking these categories are absolute.
This resolves a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما كونه محالاً .. فهو أنه لو قُدِّر عدم تعلق الإرادة بإيجاده . . فيكون - لا محالة - حدوثه محالاً ؛ إذ يؤدي إلى حدوث حادث بلا سبب ، وقد عرف أنه محال . وأما كونه ممكناً . . فهو بأن ينظر إلى ذاته فقط ، ولا يعتبر معه لا وجود الإرادة ولا عدمها ؛ فيكون له وصف الإمكان ، فإذن الاعتبارات ثلاثة : - الأول : أن نشرط فيه وجودَ الإرادة وتعلقها ، فهو بهذا الاعتبار واجب . - الثاني : أن نعتبر فقدَ الإرادة ، فهو بهذا الاعتبار محال . - الثالث : أن نقطعَ الالتفات إلى الإرادة والسبب ، فلا نعتبر وجوده ولا عدمَهُ ، ونجردَ النظر إلى ذات العالم ، فيبقى له بهذا الاعتبار الأمر الثالث ، وهو الإمكان ، ونعني به : أنه ممكن لذاته ؛ أي : إذا لم نشترط قد يكون الشيء غير ذاته .. كان ممكناً ; فظهر منه : أنه يجوز أن يكون الشيء الواحد ممكناً و محالاً من وجه آخر ممكنا من وجه محالاً ، ولكن ممكناً باعتبار ذاته ، محالاً باعتبار غيره ، ولا يجوز أن يكون ممكناً لذاته محالاً لذاته ؛ فهما متناقضان
As for it being impossible: it is that if the non-attachment of the will to its bringing into existence were supposed, then its occurrence would inevitably be impossible, because it leads to an event occurring without a cause, and it is known that this is impossible. As for it being possible: it is that one looks only at its essence, without considering the existence of the will or its non-existence; so it has the description of possibility. So there are three considerations: First: that we stipulate the existence of the will and its attachment, and it is in this consideration necessary. Second: that we consider the absence of the will, and it is in this consideration impossible. Third: that we cut off attention to the will and the cause, we consider neither its existence nor its non-existence, and we isolate the consideration to the essence of the world, so it retains in this consideration the third category, which is possibility. We mean by it: that it is possible in itself; i.e., if we do not stipulate, a thing may be other than itself ... it was possible. So it became clear: it is permissible that one and the same thing be possible and impossible from another aspect, possible from an aspect and impossible from an aspect, but possible in consideration of its essence, impossible in consideration of other than it, and it is not permissible that it be possible in itself and impossible in itself; for they are contradictory.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This is a definition and classification passage. Ghazali explains the three considerations for any contingent thing's existence relative to divine will. First, if we stipulate the will's attachment, the thing becomes necessary (wajib) due to the cause. Second, if we consider the absence of will, it becomes impossible (muhal) because an uncaused event is impossible. Third, if we consider the thing's essence alone without reference to any cause, it is possible (mumkin) in itself. He then emphasizes that a single thing can be possible in itself and impossible due to another factor, but not both in the same respect, as that would be contradictory.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الامتناع
الإمكان
الإرادة
الاعتبارات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility and impossibility are absolute properties of things.
Correction: Ghazali shows they are relative to the consideration of divine will; a thing can be possible in itself but impossible due to the absence of will.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what makes something wajib (necessary) in the first consideration?
A) Considering its essence alone
B) The absence of will
C) Stipulating the existence of will and its attachment
D) The fact that it is a possible thing

Key Takeaway
A single entity can be wajib, muhal, or mumkin depending on whether divine will is attached, absent, or disregarded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every possible thing in your life (your health, wealth, relationships) is only actual because Allah's will is attached to it, which area do you most often take for granted as being 'necessary' without thinking of His will? Will you today consciously thank Him for that thing as a gift of His will?
?
The passage shows that possibility arises when we ignore the will and cause. When you plan your future, do you often think of it as 'possible' in itself, forgetting that it entirely depends on Allah's will? How can you train yourself to phrase your plans with 'insha'Allah' more sincerely?
?
Ghazali teaches that the same thing can be impossible from one aspect and possible from another. Is there a character flaw or sin you consider impossible to change? How does this lesson challenge that belief, and what step will you take today to act on the 'possible' aspect?

The Example of Zayd's Life: Muhal li-Dhatihi vs. Muhal li-Ghayrihi
Ghazali applies the three considerations to Zayd's life and divine foreknowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between impossibility in essence and impossibility due to another applies to a concrete scenario involving divine foreknowledge and the possibility of creating life.

Core Idea
A thing can be possible in itself yet impossible due to conflict with God's unchanging knowledge.

Ghazali has just introduced the three rational judgments — wajib, mumkin, muhal — and now he provides a concrete example to clarify the subtle distinction between impossibility in essence (muhal li-dhatihi) and impossibility due to another (muhal li-ghayrihi). This example anchors an abstract concept in a tangible case, helping the student see how theological reasoning works in practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the scenario: God's knowledge determines Zayd's death on Saturday morning.
The passage begins abruptly with 'Saturday, for example'.
2
Identify the two types of impossibility
Find where Ghazali distinguishes between impossibility in itself (muhal li-dhatihi) and impossibility due to another (muhal li-ghayrihi). Note the example of combining black and white for the first type.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider why Ghazali uses the example of black and white. Reflect on how Zayd's life is like a possible thing that becomes impossible because it would force a contradiction in God's knowledge.
This helps internalize the concept.

السبت مثلاً يوم فنقول : إذا سبق في علم الله تعالى إماتة زيد صبيحة فنقول : خَلْقُ الحياة لزيد صبيحة يوم السبت ممكن أو ليس بممكن ؟ فالحق فيه أنه ممكن ومحال ؛ أي : هو ممكن باعتبار ذاته إن قطع الالتفات إلى غيره ، ومحال لغيره لا لذاته ؛ وذلك إذا اعتبر معه الالتفاتُ إلى تعلق العلم بالإماتة ، والمحال لذاته : هو الذي يمتنع لذاته ؛ كالجمع بين السواد والبياض ، لا للزوم استحالة في غير ذاته . وحياة زيد لو قدَّرَتْ .. لم تمتنع لذات الحياة ، ولكن يلزم منه استحالة في غير ذاتها ، وهو ذات العلم ؛ إذ ينقلب جهلاً ، ومحال أن ينقلب جهلاً .. فبانَ أنَّه ممكن لذاته ، محال للزوم استحالة في غيره
Saturday, for example, a day. So we say: If it preceded in the knowledge of God Almighty the death of Zayd in the morning, then we say: Creating life for Zayd on the morning of Saturday is possible or not possible? The truth in it is that it is possible and impossible; i.e., it is possible in consideration of its essence if one cuts off attention to other than it, and impossible for other than it, not in itself. That is when one considers with it the attention to the attachment of knowledge to death. And the impossible in itself is that which is impossible in itself; like combining black and white, not due to a necessary impossibility in other than itself. And Zayd's life, if it were supposed... it is not impossible for the essence of life, but there follows from it an impossibility in other than its essence, which is the essence of knowledge; because it would turn into ignorance, and it is impossible that it turn into ignorance. So it became clear that it is possible in itself, impossible due to a necessary impossibility in other than it.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage provides an example to clarify the distinction between impossibility in essence (muhal li-dhatihi) and impossibility due to another (muhal li-ghayrihi). Ghazali posits a scenario: God's foreknowledge has determined that Zayd will die on Saturday morning. Is creating life for Zayd at that time possible? He answers that it is possible in itself because life is not inherently impossible, but it is impossible due to the conflict with divine foreknowledge, which would turn knowledge into ignorance. Impossibility in essence is like combining black and white, which is inherently contradictory. This example shows that a thing can be possible in its own essence yet impossible due to an external factor.

Key Terms
المحال لذاته
المحال لغيره
العلم
الإماتة
الحياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is impossible, it must be impossible in itself (muhal li-dhatihi).
Correction: Ghazali shows that impossibility can be contingent on external relations (muhal li-ghayrihi). In the example, Zayd's life is possible in its essence but impossible due to its conflict with God's foreknowledge.

Checkpoint
In the example, why is creating life for Zayd on Saturday considered impossible?
A) Because life is impossible in itself
B) Because it would lead to ignorance in God's knowledge
C) Because Zayd is dead
D) Because Saturday is a holy day

Key Takeaway
In theological reasoning, an action may be possible in its own nature yet impossible due to its necessary conflict with a divine attribute, as shown by Zayd's life and God's foreknowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Zayd's life is possible in itself but impossible only because of Allah's decree, what does that imply about the things you desperately want but haven't received? Are you treating them as if they are impossible in themselves, when perhaps you should recognize that your responsibility is to strive while trusting Allah's knowledge? Will you surrender your plans to His decree today?
?
The passage shows that a contradiction with divine knowledge makes something impossible. Is there an area in your life where you are living as if you can change what Allah has already decreed, like dwelling on regret or wishing for a different past? How can you align your heart with the reality that Allah's knowledge is perfect and unchanging, and what step can you take to accept His will with contentment?
?
Think of a sin you struggle to leave. Is it truly impossible in itself for you to stop? Or is it only impossible due to your habits and desires? Use Ghazali's distinction: your repentance is possible in its essence, but you make it seem impossible by ignoring that Allah's command is clear. What concrete change can you make today to prove that the impossibility was only ever from your own negligence, not from your nature?

Rational Proof for God's Hearing and Seeing from Perfection
Ghazali's rational argument from perfection for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses a rational argument from perfection to establish that God must possess the attributes of hearing and seeing.

Core Idea
If creatures have perfections like seeing and hearing, the Creator must have them even more perfectly.

This passage from Imam Ghazali's *Iqtisad* presents a concise rational argument for two of God's attributes: hearing and seeing. Ghazali builds his case on two universally accepted principles about perfection, and then anticipates and refutes a potential objection. The argument shows that these attributes are not arbitrary but rationally necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Let Ghazali's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the structure: principles, objection, response.
2
Identify the two principles
The proof rests on two principles (أصلان). Find them in the text and understand how they lead to the conclusion.
The second principle is about seeing and hearing being perfections.
3
Examine the objection and response
Ghazali anticipates a dispute about the first principle. Read his response carefully. Why does he say denying it is irrational?
He invokes universal human reason and instinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المسلك العقلي : فهو أن نقول : معلوم أنَّ الخالق أكمل من المخلوق ، ومعلوم أنَّ البصير أكمل ممن لا يبصر ، والسميع أكمل ممن لا يسمعُ ؛ فيستحيل أن تُثبت وصف الكمال للمخلوق ولا نثبته للخالق فهذان أصلان يوجبان الإقرار بصحة دعوانا ، ففي أيهما النزاع ؟ فإن قيل : النزاع في قولكم : واجبٌ أنْ يكونَ الخالق أكمل من المخلوق . تحريجة : لم يجب أن يكون الخالق أكمل من المخلوق؟ قلنا : هذا ممّا يجب الإقرار به شرعاً وعقلاً ، والأمة والعقلاء مجمعون عليه ، فلا يصدر هذا السؤال من معتقد ، ومن اتسع عقله لقبول قادرٍ يقدِرُ على اختراع ما هو أعلى وأشرفُ منه .. فقد انخلع عن غريزة البشرية ، ونطق لسانه بما ينبو عن قبوله قلبه إن كان يفهم ما يقوله ، ولهذا لا نرى عاقلاً يعتقد هذا الاعتقاد .
As for the rational path: it is that we say: It is known that the Creator is more perfect than the creature, and it is known that the seeing person is more perfect than the one who does not see, and the hearing person is more perfect than the one who does not hear. So it is impossible to affirm the attribute of perfection for the creature and not affirm it for the Creator. These are two principles that require acknowledgment of the correctness of our claim. So in which of them is the dispute? If it is said: The dispute is in your statement: It is necessary that the Creator be more perfect than the creature. Objection: Why must the Creator be more perfect than the creature? We say: This is something that must be acknowledged by Sharia and reason, and the community and rational people are agreed upon it. So this question cannot come from a believer. And whoever's mind is wide enough to accept a powerful being who can invent what is higher and nobler than himself has indeed stripped himself of human instinct, and his tongue utters what his heart, if he understands what he says, refuses to accept. For this reason, we do not see a rational person who holds this belief.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof (al-maslak al-'aqli) for God's attributes of hearing and seeing. The proof rests on two premises: (1) the Creator is more perfect than the creature, and (2) a being who sees or hears is more perfect than one who does not. Therefore, since creatures have these perfections, the Creator must have them as well. After stating these principles, Ghazali anticipates an objection: someone might dispute the first principle, asking why the Creator must be more perfect. The response is strong: this is known by both Sharia and reason, and all rational people agree. Denying it amounts to irrationality and stripping oneself of human nature. This argument shows that the attributes are not arbitrary but rooted in rational necessity.

Key Terms
المسلك العقلي
الكمال
الخالق
المخلوق
البصير
السميع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's attributes of hearing and seeing cannot be known by reason alone; they are purely matters of faith.
Correction: Ghazali demonstrates that rational necessity demands these attributes, as they are perfections that must belong to the most perfect being.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say about the claim that the Creator must be more perfect than the creature?
A) It is a matter of personal opinion
B) It is only known through revelation, not reason
C) It must be acknowledged by both Sharia and reason, and all rational people agree
D) It is a philosophical assumption not required in theology

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's rational proof shows that the attributes of hearing and seeing are necessary for the Creator, as they are perfections found in creatures and He is the most perfect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you notice a beautiful voice or a sharp eye in someone, do your thoughts naturally ascend to God's perfect hearing and seeing, or do you become absorbed in the creature? What would it take to change that habit, starting today?
?
Ghazali says that accepting a Creator who might be less perfect than His creation is against human instinct. Have you ever, even momentarily, felt that your own abilities or successes were independent of God? How does this argument expose that feeling as a contradiction of your own fitrah?
?
If you truly believed that every perfection in creation is a sign of God's greater perfection, how would your worship change? Commit to one specific act today that acknowledges God as the source of all goodness.

Objection: Seeing and Hearing Are Not Perfections
Objection and answer regarding hearing/seeing as perfections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that hearing and seeing are objectively known as perfections through intuitive reason.

Core Idea
Hearing and seeing are objectively perfections, known by intuitive reason.

This lesson addresses a key objection to the argument that the seeing person is more perfect. The objector denies that hearing and seeing are perfections at all. Ghazali responds by appealing to the intuitive intellect (badīha al-'aql), which recognizes knowledge as a primary perfection and hearing/seeing as a secondary perfection that completes knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the structure: an objection followed by Ghazali's response.
The text is partially cut off, but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the objection and response
Find the objector's claim and Ghazali's counter. The objector denies that hearing and seeing are perfections; Ghazali says they are known by intuitive reason.
3
Understand the distinction between perfections
Ghazali distinguishes between knowledge as a first perfection and hearing/seeing as a second perfection that completes knowledge. Reflect on why this distinction matters for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : النزاع في الأصل الثاني ؛ وهو قولكم : إنَّ البصير أكمل ، تحريجة: لا نسلم أن السمع والبصر كمال . قلنا : هذا أيضاً مدرك ببديهة العقل ؛ فإنَّ العلم كمال ، والسمع والبصر كمال ثان للعلم (٢) ، فإنَّا بيَّنا أنَّه نوع استكمال للعلم والتخيل (*)، ومن علم (٢) ...

We say: This too is perceived by the intuitive intellect (badīha al-'aql); for knowledge is perfection, and hearing and seeing are a second perfection of knowledge. For we have explained that it is a kind of completion of knowledge and imagination. And from knowledge...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the second principle of the rational argument—namely, that being seeing or hearing is not necessarily a perfection. The objector denies that hearing and seeing are perfections. Ghazali's response is that this is known by intuitive reason (badīha al-'aql). He distinguishes between knowledge as a primary perfection and hearing/seeing as a secondary perfection (kamāl thānī) that completes and enhances knowledge. This implies that the attributes are real and distinct from knowledge, adding value. The answer is cut off in the available text, but the beginning establishes the rational basis for considering these attributes perfections.

Refuting the Eternal Sound Claim
Ghazali refutes the claim that created sounds are eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that individual letters and sounds, which have temporal order, cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
Temporal succession disproves eternal status.

This passage is a refutation of those who claim that the sounds we generate by our own choice are eternal. Ghazali argues that such a person misunderstands the basic meanings of 'letter' and 'temporal event.' He uses a concrete example from the basmalah to show the absurdity of the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Try to get the overall argument before diving into details.
Notice Ghazali's tone: he says such a person 'should not be addressed or spoken to.'
2
Identify the core argument
Ghazali argues that if you understand what 'letter' (ḥarf) and 'temporal event' (ḥādith) really mean, you would see that your own produced sounds are created and temporal.
He uses the example of the basmalah to illustrate temporal sequence.
3
Understand the basmalah example
Ghazali points out that in 'bismillah', the letter sin comes after ba. If it were not after, it would be a mistake. This temporal posteriority proves it cannot be eternal.
Eternal (al-qadim) means 'that which is not posterior to anything else.'
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this refutes the claim. Can something that is composed of parts in sequence be eternal? Why or why not?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الجملة : من يقولُ : ما أحدثته باختياري من الصوت وتقطيعه وكنت ساكتاً عنه قبله فهو قديم .. فلا ينبغي أن يخاطب ويكلَّمَ ، بل ينبغي أن يعلم المسكينُ أنَّه ليس يدري ما يقوله ، فلا هو يفهم معنى الحرف ، ولا هو يعلم معنى الحادث ، ولو علمهما .. لعلم أنه في نفسه إذا كان مخلوقاً . كان ما يصدر عنه مخلوقاً، وعلمَ أنَّ القديم لا ينتقل إلى ذات حادثة . فلنترك التطويل في الجليات ؛ فإن قول القائل : ( باسم الله ) إن لم تكن السين فيه بعد الباء.. لم يكن قرآناً ، بل كان خطأ ، وإذا كان بعد غيره و متأخراً عنه فكيف يكون قديماً ؟ ونحن نريد بالقديم : ما لا يتأخَّرُ عن غيره أصلاً
And in summary: Whoever says that what I have produced by my choice of sound and its segmentation, while I was silent before it, is eternal — then he should not be addressed or spoken to, but rather the poor fellow should know that he does not know what he is saying. For he neither understands the meaning of the letter, nor does he know the meaning of the temporal event. If he had known them both, he would have known that in itself, if it is created, then what proceeds from him is created, and he would have known that the eternal does not transfer to a temporal essence. So let us leave off lengthy discussion of obvious matters. For the statement of the speaker: "In the name of God (bismillah)" — if the letter seen (sin) in it were not after the ba', it would not be Qur'an, but rather an error. And if it is after something else and posterior to it, how can it be eternal? By 'eternal' (al-qadim) we mean that which is not posterior to something else at all.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that the sounds they generate by their own choice, such as the letters and syllables of speech, are eternal (qadim). Ghazali opens by stating that such a person should not be engaged in debate because he fundamentally misunderstands the terms. He explains that if the person understood the meaning of 'letter' and 'temporal event' (hadith), he would realize that his own actions are created, and that the eternal does not 'transfer' (yantaqil) into a temporal substance. To avoid belaboring obvious points, he gives a concrete example: the basmalah (بسم الله). If the letter sin (س) were placed before the ba (ب), it would not be the Qur'anic phrase but a mistake. Since the sin comes after the ba, it is temporally posterior, so it cannot be eternal. The eternal, by definition, is that which is not preceded by anything else. Thus, the argument demonstrates the logical inconsistency of claiming that individual letters and sounds, which have a temporal order, are eternal.

Key Terms
القديم (al-qadim: the eternal)
الحادث (al-hadith: the temporal event)
الحرف (al-harf: the letter)
الصوت (al-sawt: sound)
التقطيع (al-taqti': segmentation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ghazali is denying the uncreatedness of the Qur'an as the eternal speech of Allah.
Correction: Ghazali is only refuting the claim that the physical letters and sounds (which are created and temporal) are eternal. The eternal speech of Allah is a different concept not addressed here.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why can the basmalah not be eternal?
A) Because it is a creation of God
B) Because the letter sin comes after the ba and is therefore posterior
C) Because the basmalah is not part of the Qur'an
D) Because it was revealed later

Key Takeaway
Temporal order in letters disproves their eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Bismillah', do you reflect that each letter is a temporal gift from Allah, created at that moment? How does this awareness change your relationship with every utterance you make?
?
If you habitually think of the Qur'an as merely an ancient text, how does Ghazali's argument about the sequence of letters challenge you to see each moment of recitation as a fresh act of divine creation?
?
You have likely recited the basmalah countless times without pondering the order of its letters. How can you use this simple sound to train your heart to recognize the temporality of all creation and the eternality of its Creator?

The Fourth Objection: Parts and Eternity
Ghazali answers the objection that the eternal Qur'an cannot have parts and be a miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali resolves the apparent contradiction between the Qur'an being eternal and having surahs, ayahs, and being a miracle.

Core Idea
The word 'Qur'an' is ambiguous, referring to both the eternal attribute and the temporal recitation.

This lesson presents the fourth objection raised against the eternity of the Qur'an: how can the eternal be divided into surahs and verses? And how can it be a miracle? Ghazali's answer hinges on a subtle linguistic point: the term 'Qur'an' is shared between two meanings. Pay close attention as he distinguishes the eternal attribute from its temporal expressions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection and note the three specific points: the Qur'an has parts, the eternal cannot have parts, and a miracle is a created act.
The objection is from those who argue that the Qur'an cannot be both eternal and have structure.
2
Identify Ghazali's key move
Notice how Ghazali introduces the term 'mushtarak' (shared/ambiguous) to resolve the contradiction.
This is the core of his argument.
3
Examine the two meanings
See how he distinguishes between al-qira'a (recitation, temporal) and al-maqru' (the recited, eternal attribute).
When affirming eternality, Muslims refer to al-maqru'; when describing parts, they refer to al-qira'a.
4
Consider the backup argument
Read how Ghazali responds if the opponent denies the ambiguity: he cites the Salaf's statements and a poetic verse.
This shows that the word can indeed mean recitation.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this argument preserves the Qur'an's eternality while acknowledging its textual divisions.
This is a classic theological move to avoid anthropomorphism while affirming revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستبعاد الرابع : قولهم : أجمعت الأمة على أنَّ القرآن معجزة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وأنه كلام الله تعالى ، وأنه سور وآيات ولها مقاطع ومفاتح ، وكيف يكون للقديم مقاطع ومفاتح ؟ وكيف ينقسم بالسور والآيات ؟ وكيف يكونُ القديم معجزة الرسول والمعجزة هي فعل خارق للعادة ، وكل فعل فهو مخلوق ، فكيف يكون كلام الله تعالى قديما ؟

قلنا : أتنكرونَ أنَّ لفظ القرآن مشترك بين القراءة والمقروء أم لا ؟ فإن اعترفتم به فكلُّ ما أورده المسلمون من وصف القرآن بما هو قديم كقولهم : القرآن كلام الله تعالى غيرُ مخلوق أرادوا به المقروء ، وكلُّ ما وصفوه به مما لا يحتمله القديم ككونه سوراً وآيات ولها مقاطع ومفاتح أرادوا به العباراتِ الدالة على الصفة القديمة التي هي قرآن وإذا صار الاسم مشتركاً امتنع التناقض ، فالإجماع منعقد على أن لا قديم إلا الله تعالى ، والله تعالى يقول : حَتَّى عَادَ كَالْعُرْجُونِ الْقَدِيمِ) ولكن نقول : اسم القديم مشترك بين معنيين ، فإذا ثبت من وجه لم يستحل نفيه من وجه آخر ، فكذا اسم القرآن ، وهو جواب عن كل ما يرددونه من الإطلاقات المتناقضة .

فإن أنكروا كونه مشتركاً فنعلم قطعاً إطلاقه لإرادة المقروء ، ودلّ عليه كلامُ السلفِ أَنَّ القرآنَ كلامُ الله سبحانه غيرُ مخلوق مع العلم بأنهم وأصواتهم وقراءتهم وأفعالهم كل ذلك مخلوق

وأما إطلاقه لإرادة القراءة فقد قال الشاعر : ضَحُوا بِأَشْمَطَ عُنْوانُ السُّجُودِ بِهِ يُقَطِّعُ اللَّيْلَ تَسْبيحا وَقُرْآنا
The fourth improbability: Their saying: The community has agreed that the Qur'an is the miracle of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and that it is the speech of God Most High, and that it consists of surahs and verses having sections and openings. How can the eternal have sections and openings? How can it be divided into surahs and verses? How can the eternal be the miracle of the Messenger, while the miracle is a supernatural act, and every act is created, so how can the speech of God Most High be eternal?

We said: Do you deny that the word 'Qur'an' is shared (mushtarak) between the recitation (al-qira'a) and the recited (al-maqru') or not? If you acknowledge it, then everything that Muslims have mentioned in describing the Qur'an as being eternal, like their saying 'The Qur'an is the speech of God, uncreated,' they mean by it the recited. And everything they have described which the eternal cannot admit, such as being surahs and verses having sections and openings, they mean by it the expressions indicating the eternal attribute which is the Qur'an. And if the name becomes shared, contradiction is prevented. For the consensus is established that there is no eternal except God Most High, and God Most High says: 'Until it returned like an old (qadim) palm stalk.' But we say: The name 'eternal' (al-qadim) is shared between two meanings; so if it is affirmed from one aspect, its negation from another aspect is not impossible. Likewise the name 'Qur'an'. This is an answer to all the contradictory usages they repeat.

If they deny that it is shared, then we know with certainty that its usage is intended for the recited. And this is indicated by the speech of the Salaf that the Qur'an is the speech of God, uncreated, while knowing that their persons, voices, recitations, and actions are all created.

As for its usage to mean the recitation, the poet said: 'They sacrificed an old man (ashmat) marked by prostration, who spends the night in glorification and Qur'an (recitation).'
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. First, Ghazali presents the fourth objection (al-istib'ad al-rabi') raised by opponents (likely the Mu'tazila or others): How can the Qur'an be eternal (qadim) when it has surahs, ayahs, sections (maqati') and openings (mafatih)? The eternal cannot be divided. Additionally, the Qur'an is a miracle, and miracles are supernatural acts; every act is created, so how can the speech of God be eternal? Ghazali's answer (qulna) hinges on the ambiguity (ishtirak) of the word 'Qur'an'. He argues that the term is shared (mushtarak) between two meanings: the eternal attribute of God (the recited, al-maqru') and the temporal human recitation (al-qira'a). When Muslims affirm that the Qur'an is uncreated, they mean the recited, the eternal attribute. When they describe it as having surahs, ayahs, etc., they mean the expressions (al-'ibarat) that indicate that attribute. Since the name is shared, there is no contradiction (tanaqud). To further illustrate, he notes that even the word 'qadim' (eternal) is shared between the absolute eternal (God) and something that is old in time, as in the Qur'anic verse 'kal-'urjun al-qadim' (an old palm stalk). Thus, affirmation in one sense does not necessitate negation in another. If the opponent denies that the word is shared, Ghazali then appeals to the consensus of the Salaf: they affirmed that the Qur'an is uncreated while acknowledging that their own voices and recitations are created, thus they used the term differently. Finally, he provides a poetic verse from the poet where 'Qur'an' is used to mean recitation (tilawa). This linguistic evidence supports the shared usage.

Key Terms
الاستبعاد (al-istib'ad: improbability, objection)
مشترك (mushtarak: shared, ambiguous term)
القراءة (al-qira'a: recitation)
المقروء (al-maqru': the recited, the eternal attribute)
المعجزة (al-mu'jiza: miracle)
السور (al-suwar: surahs)
الآيات (al-ayat: verses)
المقاطع (al-maqati': sections)
المفاتح (al-mafatih: openings)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ghazali is compromising the eternity of the Qur'an by calling it 'shared'
Correction: He is clarifying that the term applies to both the eternal attribute and the temporal recitation without contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the key argument Ghazali uses to resolve the objection about the Qur'an having surahs and ayahs?
A) He denies that the Qur'an has surahs and ayahs
B) He claims that the word 'Qur'an' is shared between the recited (eternal) and the recitation (temporal)
C) He argues that the miracle is not part of the Qur'an
D) He says that the Qur'an is created

Key Takeaway
Ghazali resolves the objection by showing that the term 'Qur'an' is linguistically ambiguous, referring to the eternal divine attribute when affirming uncreatedness and to the temporal human recitation when describing its structural parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you ever pause to consider that the words you utter are a created sound, yet they point to an eternal, uncreated reality? How does that awareness affect your reverence for each verse?
?
The opponents in this passage stumbled because they assumed a single meaning for 'Qur'an'. Are there areas in your own faith where you oversimplify complex truths, missing the nuance that saves you from confusion? Will you seek deeper clarity?
?
Ghazali's answer turns on a linguistic distinction that protects the creed. In your daily speech about Allah and His Book, are you careful with your words, or do you risk causing misunderstanding? Commit to one concrete change in how you speak about the Qur'an.

The Decisive Proof for Added Attributes and Two Expressions
Al-Ghazali provides the decisive proof for added attributes and shows two expression methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's decisive proof that affirming a descriptive name for God (like 'knowing') necessarily implies affirming a corresponding attribute (knowledge), and the two linguistic expressions that convey this relationship.

Core Idea
Stating 'God is knowing' already affirms that God has knowledge.

In this passage, al-Ghazali presents the central argument for the reality of God's attributes. He argues that the very act of calling God 'knowing' commits one to the existence of knowledge as an attribute. He then shows how this truth can be expressed either in a detailed or a concise manner, using a vivid analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Get the flow of the argument.
Notice the concise style of al-Ghazali.
2
Understand the decisive proof
Identify why al-Ghazali says that saying 'God is knowing' implies 'God has knowledge'. What is the relationship between the two expressions?
The key point is that the meaning is one.
3
Grasp the two expressions
Learn the long form (explicit statement) and the short form (derivation). Reflect on how they both convey the same reality.
4
Examine the analogy
Consider the analogy of the person, sandal, and foot. How does it illustrate the relationship between the long and short expressions?
The analogy makes the abstract point concrete.
5
Identify key terms
Note the key Arabic terms: برهان, صفات, علم, ذات, عبارة, اشتقاق. Understand their role in the argument.
These terms are crucial for theological discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا تفصيل مذاهب الضلال، والغرضُ إثبات الصفات ، والبرهان القاطع : هو أن من ساعد على أن الله تعالى عالم . . فقد ساعد على أن له علماً؛ فإن المفهوم من قولنا : عالم وله علمٌ . . واحد؛ فإن العاقل يعقل زيادة الصفات دون ذاتاً ، ويعقلُها على حالة وصفة بعد ذلك ، فيكون قد عقل صفة وموصوفاً ، تغاير عن الذات والصفة علم مثلاً ، وله عبارتان : إحداهما طويلة؛ وهي أن نقول : هذه الذات قد قام بها علم والأخرى وجيزة ، أو جزت بالتصريف والاشتقاق؛ وهي أن الذات عالمة كما يشاهد الإنسانُ شخصاً ويشاهد نعلاً ويشاهد دخول رجله في النعل ، فله عبارة طويلة؛ وهي أن يقول : هذا الشخص رجله داخلةٌ في نعله
This is the detailed account of the deviant sects, and the goal is to affirm the attributes. The decisive proof is: whoever concedes that God, exalted, is knowing has conceded that He has knowledge; for the meaning of our saying 'knowing' and 'having knowledge' is one. For the rational person understands the addition of attributes without [the] essence, and understands them as a state and attribute afterwards, so he has understood an attribute and a described entity, distinct from the essence and the attribute, e.g., knowledge. And it has two expressions: one is long, i.e., we say: this essence is possessed of knowledge; and the other is concise, or you express it through derivation, i.e., the essence is knowing, just as one sees a person and sees a sandal and sees the entry of his foot into the sandal, so he has a long expression: to say: this person's foot is inside his sandal.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a proof and conclusion. After detailing the deviant views, al-Ghazali states the goal is to affirm the attributes. He then gives the decisive proof: anyone who says God is knowing necessarily agrees that God has knowledge, because the concepts are identical in meaning. The rational mind grasps the addition of attributes without treating them as separate essences; it understands an attribute and a described thing as distinct yet related. This truth can be expressed in two ways: a long form (explicitly stating 'this essence has knowledge') and a short form (using derivation: 'the essence is knowing'). He illustrates with the analogy of seeing a person and a sandal and the foot inside: the long expression makes explicit what the short expression implies.

ذات

اشتقاق

Misconception: That saying 'God is knowing' does not imply a separate attribute such as knowledge.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that the meaning of 'knowing' and 'having knowledge' is one, so the short expression necessarily implies the attribute.

Checkpoint

A) That God is not knowing.
B) That God has knowledge.
C) That knowledge is God's essence.
D) That knowledge is created.

Key Takeaway
The statement 'God is knowing' necessarily implies that God has knowledge, and this can be expressed either explicitly or through derivation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
