الدعوى الأولى أنه يجوز لله تعالى ألا يخلق، وإذا خلق فلم يكن ذلك واجباً عليه لا يجب على الله تعالى الخلق وإذا خلقهم فله ألا يكلفهم، وإذا كلفهم فلم يكن ذلك واجباً عليه. وقالت طائفة من المعتزلة: يجب عليه الخلق والتكليف بعد الخلق. وبرهان الحق فيه أن نقول: قول القائل: الخلق والتكليف واجب غير مفهوم؛ فإنا بينا أن المفهوم عندنا من لفظ الواجب: ما ينال تاركه ضرر إما عاجلاً وإما آجلاً، أو ما يكون نقيضه محالاً، والضرر محال في حق الله سبحانه، وليس في ترك التكليف وترك الخلق لزوم محال إلا أن يقال: كان ذلك إلى خلاف ما سبق به العلم في الأزل، وما سبقت به المشيئة في الأزل، فهذا حق، وهو بهذا التأويل واجب؛ فإن الإرادة إذا فرضت موجودة، أو العلم إذا فرض متعلقاً بالشيء كان حصول المراد والمعلوم واجباً لا محالة. فإن قيل: إنما يجب عليه ذلك لفائدة الخلق لا لفائدة ترجع إلى الخالق. قلنا: الكلام في قولكم: (لفائدة الخلق) للتعليل، والحكم المعلل هو الوجوب، ونحن نطالبكم بتفهيم الحكم، فلا يغنيكم ذكر العلة، فما معنى قولكم: إنه يجب لفائدة الخلق؟ وما معنى الوجوب ونحن لا نفهم من الوجوب إلا المعاني الثلاثة وهي منعدمة؟ فإن أردتم معنى رابعاً ففسروه أولاً ثم اذكروا علته، فإنا ربما لا ننكر أن للخلق في الخلق فائدة، وكذا في التكليف، ولكن ما فيه فائدة غيره لم يجب عليه إذا لم يكن له فائدة في فائدة غيره، وهذا لا مخرج عنه أبداً. خلق الإنسان في كبد على أنا نقول: إنما يستقيم هذا الكلام في الخلق لا في التكليف. ولا يستقيم في الخلق الموجود، بل في أن يخلقهم في الجنة منعمين من غير غم وحزن وألم، وأما هذا الخلق الموجود فالعقلاء كلهم قد تمنوا العدم؛ وقال بعضهم: ليتني كنت نسياً منسياً، وقال آخر: ليتني لم أك شيئاً، وقال آخر: ليتني كنت هذه التبنة وقد رفعها من الأرض، وقال آخر يشير إلى طائر: ليتني كنت ذلك الطائر، وهذا قول الأنبياء والأولياء وهم العقلاء، وبعضهم يتمنى عدم الخلق، وبعضهم يتمنى عدم التكليف بأن يكون جماداً أو طائراً. فليت شعري؛ كيف يستجيز العاقل أن يقول: للخلق في التكليف فائدة وإنما معنى الفائدة نفي الكلفة، والتكليف في عينه إلزام كلفة، وهو ألم، وإن نظر إلى الثواب فهو الفائدة، وكان قادراً على إيصاله إليهم بغير تكليف؟!
The first claim: that it is permissible for God Almighty not to create, and if He creates, that was not obligatory upon Him. It is not obligatory upon God Almighty to create, and if He creates them, He may not obligate them, and if He obligates them, that was not obligatory upon Him. A group of the Mu'tazila said: creation and obligation (taklif) after creation are obligatory upon Him. The proof of the truth in this is to say: the statement of the speaker: 'creation and obligation are obligatory' is not understood; for we have shown that what is understood by us from the word 'obligatory' (wajib) is: that the abandoner of it incurs harm either immediately or eventually, or that its contrary is impossible (muhal). Harm is impossible with respect to God the Exalted, and there is no impossibility (muhal) involved in abandoning obligation and abandoning creation except if it be said: that would be contrary to what knowledge (ilm) has preceded in eternity, and what will (mashia) has preceded in eternity. This is true, and in this interpretation it is obligatory; for if will (ira) is assumed to exist, or knowledge is assumed to be related to something, then the occurrence of the intended and the known is necessarily obligatory without doubt. If it is said: He is only obligated to do that for the benefit (faida) of creation, not for a benefit returning to the Creator. We say: the discussion is about your statement: 'for the benefit of creation' as a cause, and the judgment caused is obligation, and we demand from you the understanding of the judgment, so mentioning the cause does not suffice you. So what is the meaning of your statement: 'He must do it for the benefit of creation'? And what is the meaning of obligation, while we do not understand from obligation except the three meanings, which are non-existent? If you intend a fourth meaning, then explain it first, then mention its cause, for perhaps we do not deny that there is benefit in creation for creation, and likewise in obligation, but that which has benefit for other than Him is not obligatory upon Him when He has no benefit in the benefit of another. This has no way out ever. 'Indeed We created man in toil' (90:4) — rather we say: this speech applies only to creation, not to obligation. And it does not apply to actual creation, but rather to His creating them in paradise as blissful without grief, sorrow, and pain. As for this actual creation, all the rational ones have wished for non-existence. Some said: 'Would that I were forgotten, out of mind' (19:23). Another said: 'Would that I were nothing.' Another said: 'Would that I were this straw that has been raised from the ground.' Another, pointing to a bird, said: 'Would that I were that bird.' This is the statement of the prophets and saints, and they are the rational ones. Some wish for non-existence of creation, and some wish for non-existence of obligation, such that they would be an inanimate object or a bird. Alas! How can a rational person dare to say that there is benefit for creation in obligation, while the meaning of benefit is the removal of burden, and obligation itself is the imposition of burden, which is pain? And if one looks at the reward (thawab), that is the benefit, and He was able to deliver it to them without obligation?!
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that creation and taklif (obligation) are obligatory upon God. Ghazali begins by stating the first claim: it is not necessary for God to create, and if He creates, He may not impose obligations. The Mu'tazilites counter that after creation, both are obligatory. Ghazali then presents the proof of truth. He argues that the meaning of 'obligatory' (wajib) in Islamic law is understood as something whose abandonment results in harm (darar) to the agent, either in this world or the next, or something whose opposite is impossible. Harm is impossible for God, and abandoning creation and taklif does not entail impossibility—unless it contradicts God's eternal knowledge and will. In that case, it becomes necessary in the sense that what God knows and wills must occur. Ghazali then addresses the objection that obligation is for the benefit of creation. He demands a clear definition of 'obligation' if the Mu'tazilites mean a fourth sense beyond the three known to them. He concedes that there may be benefit for creation in creation and taklif, but that does not make it obligatory upon God, as He derives no benefit. Ghazali further argues that the actual creation, which involves toil and suffering, is not obviously beneficial—many rational people, even prophets, have wished for non-existence. Finally, he points out the contradiction: taklif itself is a burden and pain, so calling it beneficial while claiming the benefit is the removal of burden is incoherent, especially since God could grant reward without taklif.

Key Terms
خلق
تكليف
فائدة
ألم
ثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That creation and taklif are intrinsically beneficial for creatures, making them obligatory on God out of justice.
Correction: Ghazali shows that actual creation involves suffering and that rational beings wish for non-existence, and taklif is itself a burden, so the claim of benefit is dubious. Moreover, God could grant reward without taklif.

Checkpoint
What is the main point Ghazali makes by citing the wishes of prophets and saints?
A) That they were ungrateful
B) That actual creation involves suffering, undermining the claim that it is beneficial
C) That they wished for more obligations
D) That God should have created them differently

Key Takeaway
Actual creation and taklif involve suffering and burden, contradicting the claim that they are beneficial and obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have witnessed or experienced deep suffering. Did you ever, even for a moment, wish you had never been created? What does that wish reveal about your expectation that life should be beneficial?
?
If God had given you paradise without any test, would you have felt grateful? Or would you have felt cheated of the chance to earn something? What does your answer say about your attachment to the idea of earning reward?
?
The prophets themselves wished for non-existence. Are you willing to accept that your own burdens may have no perceivable benefit, and yet still trust in God's wisdom without demanding an explanation?

The Reality and Components of Taklif
Ghazali defines the reality of taklif and explains its conditions and components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of taklif as a type of speech, its four components (source, addressee, object, and the demand itself), and how the same demand is named differently based on the relative status of speaker and addressee.

Core Idea
Taklif is not about the possibility of the act, but about the speech demand itself.

In this lesson, we examine Ghazali's precise definition of taklif (obligation) according to Ahl al-Sunnah. He breaks it down into its essential components: the obligator, the obligated person, the content of obligation, and the act of demanding. Understanding these distinctions will clarify why obligating the impossible is not inherently contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'wa mu'taqad' to 'ikhtilaf al-nisba' carefully. Notice how Ghazali introduces each component.
2
Identify the four components
Find the source (masdar), addressee (mawrid), object (muta'allaq), and the demand (iqtida'). Note the conditions for each.
The condition for the object is only that it be understood, not that it be possible.
3
Understand the naming taxonomy
Notice that the same demand is called taklif, iltimas, or du'a depending on whether the speaker is superior, equal, or inferior to the addressee.
This shows that the essence of the demand is the same; only the relational context changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و معتقد أهل السنة أن التكليف : له حقيقةٌ في نفسه وهو أنه كلام ، وله مصدر وهو المكلف ، ولا شرط فيه إلا كونه متكلماً ، وله مورد وهو المكلف ، وشرطه أن يكون فاهماً للكلام ، فلا يسمى الكلام مع الجماد والمجنون خطاباً ولا تكليفاً ، والتكليف نوع خطاب ، وله متعلق وهو المكلف به ، وشرطه أن يكون مفهوماً فقط، وأما كونه ممكناً .. فليس شرطاً لتحقق الكلام ؛ فإن التكليف كلام ، فإذا صدر ممن يُفْهِمُ مع من يَفْهَمُ فيما يُفهم وكان المخاطَبُ دونَ المخاطب .. سُمِّي تكليفاً ، وإن كان مثله . سمي التماساً ، وإن كان فوقه .. سُمي دعاء وسؤالاً ، فالاقتضاء فالاقتضاء في ذاته واحد ، وهذه الأسامي تختلف عليه باختلاف النسبة. وبرهان جواز ذلك : أن استحالته لا تخلو : إما أن تكون لامتناع تصور ذاته ؛ كاجتماع السواد والبياض ، أو كان لأجل الاستقباح . وباطل أن يكون امتناعه لذاته ؛ فإن السواد والبياض لا يمكن أن يفرضا مجتمعين ، وفرض هذا ممكن ؛ إذ التكليف لا يخلو : إما أن يكون لفظاً وهو مذهب الخصم ، وليس بمستحيل أن يقول الرجل لعبده الزَّمِنِ : ( قُمْ ) ، فهو على مذهبهم أظهر . وأما نحن .. فإنا نعتقد أنه اقتضاء يقوم بالنفس ، وكما يتصور أن يقوم الحيوان دون جرم سابق أو عوض لاحق
And the belief of Ahl al-Sunnah is that taklif (obligation) has a reality in itself: it is speech (kalam). It has a source (masdar), which is the obligator (mukallif), and no condition is required of him except that he be a speaker. It has an addressee (mawrid), which is the obligated person (mukallaf), and its condition is that he understands speech. Thus, speech directed at an inanimate object or an insane person is not called address (khitab) nor obligation (taklif). Taklif is a type of address. It has an object (muta'allaq), which is the thing with which the person is obligated (mukallaf bihi), and its condition is only that it be understood. As for its being possible, that is not a condition for the realization of speech; for taklif is speech. So when it issues from one who makes understand with one who understands concerning something that is understood, and the addressee is below the speaker, it is called taklif. If he is equal, it is called iltimas (request). If he is above, it is called du'a' (supplication) and su'al (asking). The meaning of demand (iqtida') is one in essence; these names differ according to the relationship. And the proof for the permissibility of that (i.e., obligating the impossible) is that its impossibility is either due to the impossibility of conceiving its essence, like the conjunction of blackness and whiteness, or due to its being considered repugnant (istiqbah). It is false that its impossibility is due to its essence; for blackness and whiteness cannot be assumed to be conjoined, while the assumption of this (i.e., obligating the impossible) is possible. For taklif is either verbal—this is the view of the opponent—and it is not impossible for a man to say to his paralyzed slave 'Stand up'; this is even more evident according to their view. As for us, we believe that it is a demand (iqtida') subsisting in the soul (nafs), just as it is conceivable that the demand subsist without a prior body or subsequent compensation.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of taklif (obligation) according to Ahl al-Sunnah and provides a proof for the permissibility of obligating what is impossible (taklif ma la yutaq). The passage is structured in two parts: first, a definitional analysis of taklif and its components; second, a rational proof that such obligation is not inherently impossible. In the first part, Ghazali states that taklif has a reality (haqiqah) in itself: it is speech (kalam). He then identifies four aspects: (1) the source (masdar) is the obligator (mukallif), who only needs to be a speaker; no further condition is attached to him. (2) The addressee (mawrid) is the obligated person (mukallaf), with the condition that he understands speech. Thus, speaking to an inanimate object or a madman does not constitute address (khitab) or obligation. (3) The object (muta'allaq) is the thing with which the person is obligated (mukallaf bihi); its only condition is that it be understandable. Notably, its possibility (imkan) is not a condition for the speech act itself. Ghazali then explains that the same demand (iqtida') changes name depending on the relative status of speaker and addressee: if the speaker is superior, it is called taklif; if equal, iltimas; if inferior, du'a' or su'al. In the second part, Ghazali provides a proof (burhan) for the permissibility of obligating the impossible. He argues that the impossibility of such an obligation must be either due to the impossibility of conceiving its essence (as in the conjunction of black and white) or due to a judgment of repugnance (istiqbah). It cannot be impossible in itself because, unlike the impossible conjunction of opposites, obligating the impossible is conceivable. He then notes that according to the opponent (the Mu'tazila), taklif is merely verbal speech, and it is not impossible for a man to say 'Stand up' to a paralyzed slave. According to Ghazali's view that taklif is a demand subsisting in the soul (nafs), it is even more conceivable that such a demand can exist without requiring the object to be possible. Thus, the proof establishes that obligating the impossible is rationally permissible, though other considerations (like divine wisdom) may govern its occurrence.

Key Terms
تكليف
كلام
مكلف
مكلف به
اقتضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That taklif necessarily requires the obligated act to be possible.
Correction: Ghazali shows that possibility is not a condition for the speech act itself; the only condition for the object (mukallaf bihi) is that it be understood.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the only condition required for the object (muta'allaq) of taklif (the thing with which one is obligated)?
A) That it be possible (mumkin)
B) That it be understandable (mafhum)
C) That it be obligatory (wajib)
D) That it be commanded by revelation

Key Takeaway
Taklif is a type of speech that does not depend on the possibility of its content; its conditions are only that the speaker is a speaker, the addressee understands, and the content is understood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a command from Allah, do you ever think, 'This is too hard, maybe it doesn't really apply to me'? How does Ghazali's point that possibility is not a condition for taklif challenge that excuse?
?
The demand in taklif is the same essence as when you ask Allah in du'a. Does that change how you approach both obeying His commands and making your supplications? Are you treating them as two sides of the same relationship?
?
If taklif requires that you understand the speech, how honestly can you say you strive to understand the Quran and Sunnah before dismissing obligations as impractical or outdated? What is one specific command you have been avoiding understanding deeply?

Proof for Obligating the Impossible
Ghazali presents the proof for the permissibility of obligating the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's rational proof that obligating the impossible is not inherently impossible and how it differs from true impossibilities like the conjunction of opposites.

Core Idea
Obligating the impossible is conceivable, so it is not impossible in itself.

Ghazali now provides a rational proof (burhan) that obligating what cannot be done is not rationally impossible. He distinguishes between impossibility due to self-contradiction and mere repugnance. This argument is crucial for defending the Ash'ari position that divine command can theoretically require the impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'wa burhan jawaz dhalik' to the end. Follow the logical flow.
2
Identify the two possible sources of impossibility
Ghazali states that the impossibility of obligating the impossible must be either due to the impossibility of conceiving its essence (like black and white together) or due to its being considered repugnant (istiqbah).
3
Understand why the first option is false
Unlike the conjunction of opposites, obligating the impossible is conceivable. It is not impossible in itself.
4
Consider the two views on the nature of taklif
Ghazali notes that according to the opponent (Mu'tazila), taklif is verbal speech, and it is not impossible to say 'stand up' to a paralyzed slave. According to his own view (demand in the soul), it is even more conceivable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception: That obligating the impossible is inherently contradictory like a round square.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is obligating the impossible not impossible in itself?
A) Because it is always beneficial
B) Because it is conceivable, unlike the conjunction of opposites
C) Because Allah can do anything
D) Because the Mu'tazila accept it

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of obligating the impossible cannot be due to self-contradiction, so it is rationally permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command of Allah that seems impossible for you to fulfill, do you immediately assume it is not required of you? How does Ghazali's proof challenge that assumption and call you to re-examine your excuses?
?
Have you ever used the phrase 'it's impossible' to avoid a difficult obedience? Reflect on a specific instance. Will you now consider whether that impossibility is in the act itself or in your own reluctance?
?
If Allah can command what is beyond your ability, what does that reveal about your need for His mercy and your reliance on His grace? Does this truth make you more earnest in seeking His help or more careless in assuming the command is not meant for you?

Proof for the Permissibility of Obligating the Impossible
Ghazali proves that obligating the impossible is conceivable by analyzing its impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's proof that obligating the impossible is rationally conceivable and not inherently impossible.

Core Idea
Obligating the impossible is conceivable because its impossibility is not conceptual.

Imam al-Ghazali addresses a key objection from the Mu'tazila: that obligating the impossible is impossible. In this passage, he systematically dismantles that claim by analyzing the source of impossibility. He shows that the impossibility is not conceptual but based on repulsiveness, which does not apply to God. This lays the groundwork for accepting the Ash'ari position that Allah may command what is beyond human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage with its English translation. Get a feel for Ghazali's argumentative style.
Notice how he starts by splitting the possibilities.
2
Identify the two sources of impossibility
Ghazali says impossibility is either conceptual (like black and white) or based on repulsiveness. Understand each.
Conceptual impossibility is inherent; repulsiveness depends on purposes.
3
Analyze the nature of obligation
Ghazali argues that obligation is either verbal utterance or a mental demand. Show why neither leads to impossibility.
He uses the example of a crippled slave to illustrate persistence of demand.
4
Refute the repulsiveness argument
Ghazali argues that repulsiveness is tied to purposes, but God is beyond purposes. Therefore, what is repulsive for humans is not for God.
This closes the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبرهان جواز ذلك: أن استحالته لا تخلو: إما أن تكون لامتناع تصور ذاته; كاجتماع السواد والبياض، أو كان لأجل الاستقباح. وباطل أن يكون امتناعه لذاته; فإن السواد والبياض لا يمكن أن يفرضا مجتمعين، وفرض هذا ممكن; إذ التكليف لا يخلو: إما أن يكون لفظاً وهو مذهب الخصم، وليس بمستحيل أن يقول الرجل لعبده الزَّمِنِ: قُمْ، فهو على مذهبهم أظهر. وأما نحن فإنا نعتقد أنه اقتضاء يقوم بالنفس، وكما يتصور أن يقوم الاقتضاء دون جرم سابق أو عوض لاحق. اقتضاء القيام بالنفس من قادر فيتصور ذلك من عاجز، بل ربما يقوم ذلك بنفسه من قادر ثم يبقى ذلك الاقتضاء وتطرأ الزمانة والسيد لا يدري، ويكون الاقتضاء قائماً بذاته، وهو اقتضاء قيام من عاجز في علم الله تعالى وإن لم يكن معلوماً عند المقتضي، فإن علمه لم يستحل بقاء الاقتضاء مع العلم بالعجز عن الوفاء. وباطل أن يقال ببطلان ذلك من جهة الاستحسان; فإن كلامنا في حق الله تعالى، وذلك باطل في حقه; لتنزهه عن الأغراض ورجوع ذلك إلى الأغراض. أما الإنسان العاقل المضبوط بقالب الأغراض فقد يستقبح منه ذلك، وليس ما يستقبح من العبد يستقبح من الله سبحانه تعالى.
The proof of its permissibility: that its impossibility must either be due to the impossibility of conceiving its essence, like the combination of black and white, or due to repulsiveness. It is false that its impossibility is due to its essence, for black and white cannot be supposed to be combined, while the supposition of this is possible. For obligation is either a verbal utterance — which is the doctrine of the opponent — and it is not impossible for a man to say to his crippled slave 'Stand'; thus it is even more apparent according to their view. As for us, we believe that it is a demand subsisting in the soul. And just as it is conceivable that the demand subsists in the soul without prior violation or subsequent compensation. The demand for standing subsisting in the soul from a capable person — it can be imagined from an incapable person. Rather, sometimes that demand subsists in the soul from a capable person, then that demand remains and the crippling occurs without the master knowing, and the demand remains subsisting in his soul, and it is a demand for standing from one who is incapable in the knowledge of God, even if unknown to the one who demands. For his knowledge does not make the persistence of the demand impossible despite knowing the inability to fulfill. And it is false to say that it is invalid due to repulsiveness; for our discussion concerns God Almighty, and that is false in His case, because He is transcendent above purposes, and that refers to purposes. As for the rational human being who is bound by the mold of purposes, it may be repulsive from him, but what is repulsive from a slave is not repulsive from God, exalted be He.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the permissibility of obligating the impossible (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). It begins by considering the possible sources of impossibility: either conceptual (like combining black and white) or based on repulsiveness (istiqbāḥ). Conceptual impossibility is ruled out because the idea of commanding a cripple to stand is conceivable. Then it examines the nature of obligation: if it is merely verbal utterance (as opponents may hold), it is clearly possible to utter such a command. If it is a mental demand (iqtiḍā’ nafsī), it can persist even when the subject becomes incapable, as illustrated by the example of a master who demands an action from a slave who later becomes crippled without the master's knowledge — the demand remains in his soul. Finally, it refutes the argument from repulsiveness (istiḥsān): repulsiveness is tied to purposes (aghraḍ), but God is transcendent beyond purposes, so what may be repulsive from a human is not repulsive from God.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
اقتضاء
استحالة
استقباح
استحسان
غرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of obligating the impossible is conceptually necessary, like the impossibility of combining black and white.
Correction: Ghazali shows it is not conceptually necessary because the concept is conceivable; the impossibility is either due to repulsiveness (which does not apply to God) or not inherent.

Checkpoint
In Ghazali's proof, what is the first possible reason for the impossibility of obligating the impossible?
A) Due to repulsiveness
B) Due to conceptual impossibility
C) Due to divine prohibition
D) Due to logical contradiction

Key Takeaway
Ghazali proves that obligating the impossible is rationally conceivable because its impossibility is not conceptual and repulsiveness does not apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a command of Allah—like praying on time, giving charity, or controlling your anger—was impossible for you? How does Ghazali's proof challenge the way you rationalized that feeling? Will you now see such situations as tests that may be within your capacity or beyond it, trusting that Allah's command is never based on human standards of fairness?
?
When you hear about tests like the sacrifice of Prophet Ibrahim, do you think 'that's too much'? How does the argument that repulsiveness does not apply to God reshape your understanding of divine trials? Can you sincerely say, 'I will accept whatever Allah commands, even if it seems impossible to my human mind'? What would need to change in your heart to reach that level of submission?
?
Ghazali's proof separates divine obligation from human notions of repulsiveness. Is there an area in your life where you have been judging Allah's decrees as 'unfair' or 'too hard' based on your own sense of what is appropriate? Will you now commit to stop measuring Allah's commands by your own standards and instead embrace them as coming from the All-Wise, even when they overwhelm you?

Disproving Wajib al-Aslah: The Three Categories Argument
Ghazali argues that three categories disprove wajib al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses a thought experiment involving three categories of people in the afterlife to demonstrate that God is not obligated to do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants.

Core Idea
Ghazali's three-category argument exposes a fatal contradiction in the Mu'tazilite claim that God must do al-aslah.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a powerful reducio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God must always do what is most beneficial for His creation. By imagining the logical implications of their own premises—three distinct categories of people in the afterlife—he shows that the doctrine leads to an irresolvable dilemma. This argument is a cornerstone of Sunni kalam on divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the thought experiment carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the three categories: adult disbeliever (eternally in Fire), adult Muslim (high rank in Paradise), and Muslim child who died before puberty (lower rank in Paradise). Understand their respective fates as per Mu'tazilite premises.
The three categories are the foundation on which the argument builds.
2
Trace the two dialogues
First, follow the Muslim child's complaint to God: why was he deprived of the chance to obey and earn a higher rank? God responds that He knew the child would have disobeyed, so He gave him a lower rank as a mercy. Then, follow the adult disbeliever's complaint: since God knew he would disbelieve, why not let him die as a child and be in that lower rank instead of eternal punishment?
Pay attention to how each dialogue uses the Mu'tazilites' own logic.
3
Identify the logical dead end
The adult disbeliever's objection leaves no possible answer from God that preserves the principle of al-aslah. If God says He acted for the person's benefit, the disbeliever counters that dying as a child would have been more beneficial. The doctrine collapses.
This is the crux of the argument: an irresolvable contradiction.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Ghazali states that these three categories exist in reality, and therefore it is definitively clear that al-aslah is not obligatory upon God, nor does it even exist in the world. Understand why the existence of these categories disproves the doctrine.
The argument is not merely hypothetical; it reflects actual possibilities.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
This argument shows that divine actions are not bound by human notions of 'most beneficial.' It upholds God's absolute freedom, wisdom, and justice beyond human comprehension.
It also illustrates the Sunni stance that God's actions are not subject to rational obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عندهم : أن يخلد البالغ الكافر في النار ، وأن يكون للبالغ المسلم في الجنة رتبة فوق رتبة الصبي المسلم. فإذا قالَ الصبيُّ المسلم : يا ربِّ ؛ لم حططت رتبتي عن رتبته ؟ . . فيقول : لأنه قد بلغ فأطاعني ، وأنت لم تطعني بالعبادات بعد البلوغ ؛ فيقول : يا ربِّ ؛ لأنك أمتَني قبل البلوغ، وكان صلاحي في أن تمدني بالحياة حتى أبلغ فأطيعَ فأنالَ رتبتَهُ ، فلِمَ حرمتني هذه الرتبة أبد الآبدين وكنت قادراً على أن تؤهلني لها ؟ فلا يكون له جواب إلا أن يقول : علمتُ أنك لو بلغت .. لعصيت وما أطعت وتعرضت لعقابي وسخطي ، فرأيتُ هذه الرتبة النازلة أولى بك وأصلح لك من العقوبة ؛ فينادي الكافرُ البالغ من الهاوية ويقول : يا ربِّ ؛ أوما علمت أني إذا بلغتُ .. كفرتُ ؟! فلو أمتَني في الصبا وأنزلتني في تلك المنزلة النازلة .. لكان أحبَّ إليَّ من تخليد النار وأصلح لي ؛ فلِمَ أحييتني وكان الموتُ خيراً لي ؟! فلا يبقى له جواب ألبتة ! ومعلوم أن هذه الأقسام الثلاثة موجودةٌ ، وبه يظهر على القطع أن الأصلح للعباد كلّهم ليس بواجب ، ولا هو موجود .
They hold: that the adult disbeliever is eternally in the Fire, and that the adult Muslim has in Paradise a rank above the rank of the Muslim child. So if the Muslim child says: 'O Lord, why did You lower my rank below his rank?' He (God) says: 'Because he reached maturity and obeyed Me, and you did not obey Me with acts of worship after maturity.' He says: 'O Lord, because You caused me to die before maturity, and my wellbeing was in that You would extend my life until I reach maturity so that I would obey and attain his rank. So why did You deprive me of this rank forever and ever, while You were able to qualify me for it?' He has no answer except to say: 'I knew that if you reached maturity, you would disobey and not obey, and you would expose yourself to My punishment and wrath, so I considered this lower rank better for you and more beneficial for you than punishment.' Then the adult disbeliever calls out from the pit and says: 'O Lord, did You not know that when I reached maturity I disbelieved? So if You had caused me to die in childhood and placed me in that lower station, that would have been more beloved to me and more beneficial for me than eternal punishment in the Fire. So why did You give me life while death was better for me?' Then no answer at all remains for him! And it is known that these three categories exist, and by this it becomes definitively clear that what is most beneficial for all servants is not obligatory, nor does it exist.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage uses a famous thought experiment (often attributed to al-Ash'ari's debate with al-Jubba'i) to disprove the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God must do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants. The author first states the three categories recognized by the Mu'tazila: the adult disbeliever eternally in Fire, the adult Muslim in a high rank in Paradise, and the Muslim child in a lower rank. Then he imagines dialogues: The Muslim child complains that he was deprived of the chance to obey and earn a higher rank. God's response is that He knew the child would have disobeyed and deserved punishment, so He gave him a lower rank as a mercy. Then the adult disbeliever protests: since God knew he would disbelieve, why not have let him die as a child and be in that lower rank instead of eternal punishment? In that case, there is no possible answer from God that preserves justice and al-aslah. The conclusion: these three categories exist in reality, so the doctrine that al-aslah is obligatory for God is false; indeed, it is not even realized in the world.

Key Terms
الأصلح (al-aslah)
الواجب (al-wajib)
الصبي (the child)
البالغ (the adult)
الهاوية (the pit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this argument is merely a hypothetical thought experiment that does not apply to God's actual actions.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states that these three categories exist in reality, and the argument uses the Mu'tazila's own premises to show a logical contradiction. It is not a mere hypothetical but a demonstration that the doctrine is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the three categories example in this passage?
A) To prove that God rewards all believers equally.
B) To demonstrate that al-aslah is not obligatory upon God.
C) To show that children cannot enter Paradise.
D) To argue that God must punish all disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's three-category argument demonstrates definitively that God is not obligated to do what is most beneficial for His servants, refuting the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God 'owes' you a better life, a longer life, or more opportunities to do good? How does Ghazali's argument expose that assumption as a subtle form of Mu'tazilism in your own heart?
?
When you see a Muslim who died young, do you feel pity that they missed out on earning more rewards? Or do you trust that God's decree for them was precisely what is best—even if it looks like a loss? Which of those reactions does this passage demand from you?
?
If you knew that a disaster in your life—like losing a loved one or failing an exam—was actually God's way of sparing you from a greater spiritual harm, would you still mourn it? What does your immediate reaction reveal about your belief in al-aslah?

Refuting Obligatory Reward: Service as Duty, Not Transaction
Ghazali argues that reward for service nullifies slavery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that demanding reward for service to God negates the very nature of servitude and worship.

Core Idea
Worship is duty, not a transaction with compensation.

Ghazali begins by addressing a corrupt belief: that God must reward His servants for their acts of worship. He argues that if reward is compensation for work, then service becomes a transaction, which contradicts the essence of slavery. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noticing the flow from their claim to Ghazali's objection.
Pay attention to the key term 'عوض' (compensation).
2
Understand the claim being refuted
The claim is that a master must reward his slave for service. Identify why Ghazali says this is a corrupt belief.
The claim is based on a supposed obligation of reward.
3
Identify Ghazali's argument
Ghazali argues that reward is compensation, which would turn service into a transaction, nullifying the benefit of slavery.
Slavery (raqq) implies duty without compensation.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this applies to worship: if God rewards obligatory worship, it becomes a transaction, not pure servitude.
This refutes the idea that God must reward acts of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا إن نزلنا على فاسد معتقدهم .. فلا نسلَّمُ أَنَّ مَنْ يستخدمُ عبدَهُ يجب عليه في العادة ثواب ؛ لأن الثوابَ يكون عوضاً عن العمل ، فتبطل فائدةُ الرَّقِّ ، وحقٌّ على العبد أن يخدم مولاه لأنه عبده ، فإن كان لأجل عوض .. فليس ذلك خدمة
ومن العجائب قولهم : إنه يجبُ الشكرُ على العباد ؛ لأنهم عباد ، قضاء لحق نعمتِه ، ثم يجب عليه الثواب على الشكر! وهذا محال ؛ لأن المستحق إذا وفَّى .. لم يلزمه عوض ، ولو جاز ذلك .. للزم على الثواب شكر مجدد ، وعلى هذا الشكرِ ثواب مجدَّدٌ ويتسلسل إلى غير نهاية ، ولم يزل العبدُ والربُّ كلُّ واحد منهما أبداً مقيداً بحق الآخر ، وهو محال
If we descend to their corrupt belief, we do not concede that one who employs his slave must customarily give him reward, because reward is compensation for work, so the benefit of slavery is nullified. The slave is obligated to serve his master because he is his slave; if it is for compensation, then it is not service. And among their wonders is their saying: Gratitude is obligatory upon servants because they are servants, fulfilling the right of His blessing, then reward is obligatory upon Him for the gratitude! This is impossible, because if the one entitled fulfills [duty], no compensation is incumbent. If that were allowed, then a new gratitude would be required for the reward, and for this gratitude a new reward, and so on ad infinitum, and the servant and Lord would each be perpetually bound by the other's right, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two related claims: that God must reward His servants for their service, and that He must reward them for their gratitude. Ghazali begins by saying that even if we accept their corrupt belief (that humans can demand rights from God), we do not accept that a master must reward his slave for service, because reward is compensation for work. But the slave's service is not a free transaction; it is an obligation due to slavery. If there were compensation, it would negate slavery. Similarly, regarding gratitude: they claim gratitude is obligatory due to God's blessings, and then God must reward that gratitude. Ghazali points out that this leads to an infinite regress: gratitude for the reward, then reward for that gratitude, etc., making both parties perpetually bound to each other, which is impossible. This demonstrates the absurdity of imposing human contractual obligations onto God.

Key Terms
ثواب (thawāb)
رَقّ (raqq)
عوض (iwad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali denies that God ever rewards; but he only denies the obligation.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny God's voluntary reward, only that it is obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does requiring reward for service nullify the benefit of slavery?
A) Because reward is compensation, making the service a transaction.
B) Because the slave deserves no reward.
C) Because the master is not required to pay.
D) Because slavery is evil.

Key Takeaway
Reward for obligatory service treats worship as a transaction, contradicting its nature as a duty of servitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you worship, do you ever catch yourself calculating the reward as if God owes you something? How does Ghazali's argument expose that as a transactional mindset that contradicts true servitude? What will you do today to worship solely out of duty to your Master?
?
If you were to remove every expectation of reward from your prayer, charity, and fasting, would you still do them? If not, what does that reveal about your heart? Commit now to one act of obedience performed only because Allah is your Lord, not for any return.

The Absurdity of Obligatory Reward for Gratitude: Infinite Regress
Ghazali refutes obligatory reward for gratitude via infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that requiring reward for gratitude leads to an infinite regress, which is impossible for God.

Core Idea
Gratitude for God's blessings cannot be rewarded, or it spirals endlessly.

Ghazali now tackles the claim that God must reward gratitude. He shows that if gratitude is an obligation, and God must reward it, then the reward itself demands new gratitude, and so on forever. This lesson explores the infinite regress (tasalsul) argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Start from 'ومن العجائب' and read through to the end. Notice how Ghazali builds the infinite regress.
Identify the key terms: شكر, ثواب, تسلسل.
2
Understand the claim about gratitude
The claim: gratitude is obligatory upon servants (due to blessings), and then God must reward that gratitude.
Gratitude is presented as a duty (قضاء لحق نعمتِه).
3
Identify the first refutation
Ghazali says if the one entitled (God) fulfills His due, no compensation is required.
The phrase 'المستحق إذا وفَّى' is key.
4
Trace the infinite regress
If reward for gratitude were given, then that reward would require new gratitude, leading to endless chain.
This is the tasalsul argument, showing impossibility.
5
Understand the conclusion
If allowed, both servant and Lord would be perpetually bound to each other's rights, which is impossible.
Ghazali declares the whole notion impossible (محال).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا إن نزلنا على فاسد معتقدهم .. فلا نسلَّمُ أَنَّ مَنْ يستخدمُ عبدَهُ يجب عليه في العادة ثواب ؛ لأن الثوابَ يكون عوضاً عن العمل ، فتبطل فائدةُ الرَّقِّ ، وحقٌّ على العبد أن يخدم مولاه لأنه عبده ، فإن كان لأجل عوض .. فليس ذلك خدمة
ومن العجائب قولهم : إنه يجبُ الشكرُ على العباد ؛ لأنهم عباد ، قضاء لحق نعمتِه ، ثم يجب عليه الثواب على الشكر! وهذا محال ؛ لأن المستحق إذا وفَّى .. لم يلزمه عوض ، ولو جاز ذلك .. للزم على الثواب شكر مجدد ، وعلى هذا الشكرِ ثواب مجدَّدٌ ويتسلسل إلى غير نهاية ، ولم يزل العبدُ والربُّ كلُّ واحد منهما أبداً مقيداً بحق الآخر ، وهو محال
If we descend to their corrupt belief, we do not concede that one who employs his slave must customarily give him reward, because reward is compensation for work, so the benefit of slavery is nullified. The slave is obligated to serve his master because he is his slave; if it is for compensation, then it is not service. And among their wonders is their saying: Gratitude is obligatory upon servants because they are servants, fulfilling the right of His blessing, then reward is obligatory upon Him for the gratitude! This is impossible, because if the one entitled fulfills [duty], no compensation is incumbent. If that were allowed, then a new gratitude would be required for the reward, and for this gratitude a new reward, and so on ad infinitum, and the servant and Lord would each be perpetually bound by the other's right, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two related claims: that God must reward His servants for their service, and that He must reward them for their gratitude. Ghazali begins by saying that even if we accept their corrupt belief (that humans can demand rights from God), we do not accept that a master must reward his slave for service, because reward is compensation for work. But the slave's service is not a free transaction; it is an obligation due to slavery. If there were compensation, it would negate slavery. Similarly, regarding gratitude: they claim gratitude is obligatory due to God's blessings, and then God must reward that gratitude. Ghazali points out that this leads to an infinite regress: gratitude for the reward, then reward for that gratitude, etc., making both parties perpetually bound to each other, which is impossible. This demonstrates the absurdity of imposing human contractual obligations onto God.

Key Terms
شكر (shukr)
ثواب (thawāb)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali is against gratitude itself.
Correction: Ghazali opposes the idea that God must reward gratitude; he does not deny the virtue of gratitude.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what would happen if reward for gratitude were obligatory?
A) It would lead to infinite regress.
B) It would prove God's generosity.
C) It would make gratitude unnecessary.
D) It would be impossible only in this world.

Key Takeaway
The claim that God must reward gratitude leads to an infinite regress, making it rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you thank God or worship Him, do you secretly feel that He now owes you something in return? How does Ghazali's argument expose that assumption as irrational? What would it mean to worship with zero expectation of reward?
?
Are there moments in your life where you have felt resentful toward God because you thought your good deeds went unrewarded? How does this passage challenge that resentment? Will you let go of the ledger of debts you've been keeping with your Creator?
?
Imagine you truly believed that your very next grateful thought is a duty you owe to God, not a favor to Him. How would that shift your posture in prayer today? What practical step can you take to break free from the illusion that God is indebted to you?

Ghazali's Counter-Argument against Obligatory Punishment
Ghazali presents a counter-argument showing the opposite view is also plausible, exposing the fallacy of basing theology on human estimations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses a dialectical counter-argument to expose the fallacy of basing divine justice on human estimations of good and bad.

Core Idea
Ghazali fights fire with fire—then declares both fires false.

This passage is a masterclass in dialectical refutation. Ghazali takes the very method his opponents use—basing moral judgments on human notions of 'good' and 'bad' (istiḥsān and istiqbāḥ)—and turns it against them. He constructs a counter-argument that is even stronger by their own standards, only to ultimately reject both arguments as false. The goal is to clear the ground for a proper understanding of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponents' method
Recall that the opponents argue from human intuition: punishing wrongdoing is 'good,' leaving it unpunished is 'bad.' Ghazali accepts this premise for the sake of argument.
This is a dialectical move, not an endorsement.
2
Follow Ghazali's counter-argument
Read the passage carefully. Ghazali says the opposite view—that punishing a past crime without future benefit is 'ugly'—is actually more consistent with human estimation.
3
Examine the two reasons
The counter-argument gives two reasons: (1) punishment without deterrent is useless, thus ugly; (2) punishing to soothe anger is a sign of deficiency. Understand each.
The second reason is particularly sharp: it shows the selfish motive behind human punishment.
4
Observe the conclusion
Ghazali declares that both the original argument and his counter-argument are false. Neither should be applied to God. The whole exercise is meant to expose the fallacy of relying on human whims.
He calls both positions 'corrupt' and says they follow 'whims and fancies.'
5
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself: Why does Ghazali go to such length? The answer: to purify theology from anthropomorphic projections. God is transcendent, beyond human estimations of good and bad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا نقول : لو سَلك سالك ضدَّ هذا الطريق بعينه .. كان أقوم قيلاً ، وأجرى على قانون الاستحسان والاستقباح الذي تقضي به الأوهام والخيالات كما سبق (١) ؛ وهو أنا نقولُ : الإنسان يقبحُ منه أن يعاقب على جناية سبقت وعَسُر تدارُكُها لوجهين :
أحدهما : أن يكون في العقوبة زجرٌ ورعاية مصلحة في المستقبل ، فيحسنُ ذلك خيفةً من فواتِ غرض في المستقبل ، فإن لم تكن فيه مصلحة في المستقبل أصلاً .. فالعقوبة بمجرد المجازاة على ما سبق قبيح ؛ لأنه لا فائدة فيه للمعاقبِ ولا لأحد سواه ، والجاني متأذ به ، ودفع الأذى عنه حسن ، وإنما يحسنُ الأذى لفائدة ، ولا فائدةً ، وما مضى فلا تدارك له ؛ فهو في غاية القبح .
الوجه الثاني : أن نقول : إذا تأذًى المجني عليه وامتعض واشتدَّ غيظه .. فذلك الغيظُ مؤلم ، وشفاء الغيظ مريح من الألم ، والألم بالجاني أليق ومهما عاقب الجاني.. زالَ منه ألمُ الغيظ واختص بالجاني ، فهو أولى فهذا أيضاً له وجه ما وإن كان دليلاً على نقصان العقل وغلبة الغضب عليه
،
فأما إيجاب العقاب حيث لا تتعلق به مصلحة في المستقبل لأحد في علم الله تعالى ، ولا فيه دفع أذى عن المجني عليه . . ففي غاية القبح ، فهذا أقوم من قول من يقول : إنَّ ترك العقاب في غاية القبح
والكل باطل واتباع لموجب الأوهام التي وقعت بتوهم الأغراض ، والله تعالى متقدّس عنها ، ولكنَّا أردنا معارضة الفاسد بالفاسد ؛ ليتبيَّنَ به بطلان خيالهم .
(١) في الحديث عن سبق الوهم إلى العكس . انظر ( ص ٢٢٧ ) وما بعدها
Moreover, we say: If someone were to follow the exact opposite of this path, it would be more upright in speech and more in accordance with the law of approbation and disapprobation that whims and fancies dictate, as previously mentioned. That is, we say: It is ugly for a person to punish for a past crime whose rectification is difficult, for two reasons: First: If the punishment serves as a deterrent and a care for future benefit, then it is good for fear of losing a future objective. But if there is absolutely no future benefit in it, then punishment merely as requital for what is past is ugly, because there is no benefit in it for the punisher or anyone else, and the criminal is harmed by it, and removing harm from him is good. Harm is only good for some benefit, and there is no benefit. What is past cannot be rectified; thus it is extremely ugly. Second: We say: If the victim is harmed, pained, and his anger is intense, that anger is painful, and soothing the anger is relieving from pain, and inflicting pain on the criminal is more fitting. Whenever he punishes the criminal, his pain of anger goes away and is specifically [transferred] to the criminal, so it is more appropriate. This also has some justification, although it indicates deficiency of intellect and predominance of anger. As for imposing punishment where there is no future benefit for anyone in the knowledge of God, and no removal of harm from the victim, it is extremely ugly. So this is more upright than the saying of one who says: 'Leaving punishment is extremely ugly.' But all of it is false, and following the dictates of whims that occur due to imagining purposes, and God is transcendent above them. We only intended to refute the corrupt with the corrupt, so that the falsity of their fancy becomes clear.
(1) In the discussion about the precedence of whim to the opposite. See page 227 and beyond.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical counter-argument. Ghazali takes the same method his opponents use—basing moral judgments on human estimations of 'good' and 'bad' (istihsan and istiqbah)—and applies it to the opposite conclusion. He argues that if someone were to argue that punishment of past crimes is ugly (qabih) unless it has future benefit, that argument would be even more plausible according to their own standards. He gives two reasons: (1) Punishment without deterrent or future benefit is useless and therefore ugly. (2) If the victim is angry, punishing relieves his anger, but that is a sign of deficiency. Then he concludes that the view that punishment is obligatory without any future benefit or removal of harm is extremely ugly. He states that this opposite argument is actually stronger than the original claim that leaving punishment is ugly. However, he finally declares that both positions are false because they are based on false analogies with human purposes and passions. God is transcendent above such considerations. Ghazali's aim is to show the futility of their reasoning by pitting one flawed argument against another.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān)
استقباح (istiqbāḥ)
وهم (wahm)
مجازاة (mujāzāt)
قبح (qubḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali endorses the counter-argument that punishment is always ugly.
Correction: Ghazali clearly states that both the original and counter-argument are false. He only uses the counter-argument to show that the opponents' method leads to contradictory conclusions, thereby exposing its invalidity.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's counter-argument, why is punishing a past crime ugly if there is no future benefit?
A) Because punishment without benefit is useless and harmful.
B) Because the criminal deserves it.
C) Because God commands it.
D) Because it pleases the victim.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali uses a dialectical counter-argument to show that human moral estimations are unreliable when applied to God; only divine transcendence provides a sound foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel that God's decree is 'unfair' or that someone 'deserves' punishment, whose standard are you using—your own or God's? In what area of your life have you silently set yourself up as the judge of what God should do?
?
Ghazali says both arguments are false because they follow whims. Are there any beliefs about God's justice that you hold simply because they 'make sense' to you, rather than because they are revealed? Can you name one such belief right now and commit to examining it against revelation?
?
If you truly internalized that God is transcendent above human purposes, would your reaction to personal tragedy or injustice change? What would that look like today—will you ask God to align your heart with His transcendent wisdom, even when you cannot see the benefit?

The Sixth Claim: Intellect Requires Benefit
Ghazali states the sixth claim and the initial principle that intellect requires benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's claim that without revelation, intellect alone cannot obligate knowing God, because the intellect only commands actions that have a benefit, and such benefit is absent in this case.

Core Idea
Intellect commands only for benefit; no benefit means no obligation.

This lesson introduces Ghazali's sixth claim, which directly challenges the Mu'tazilite position that the intellect can independently determine moral obligation. The core of Ghazali's argument is a simple but powerful principle: reason never commands something without a purpose or benefit. We'll see how he applies this to the question of knowing God.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and initial statement
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice that Ghazali begins by stating the sixth claim: without revelation, no obligation to know God or thank Him. Then he presents the proof.
The passage contrasts with the Mu'tazila position.
2
Identify the principle about benefit
Ghazali says the intellect necessitates reflection only if there is a benefit. If one admits no benefit, the intellect cannot command it.
This is the core premise.
3
See how the Mu'tazila position implies futility
If the intellect commands without any benefit, that would be a judgment of ignorance, not reason. Reason does not command futile actions.
Ghazali labels actions without benefit as 'عبث' (futility).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى السادسة: ندعي أن الشرع لو لم يرد لكان لا يجب على العباد معرفة الله تعالى ولا شكر نعمته، خلافاً للمعتزلة حيث قالوا إن العقل بمجرده موجب. وبرهانه: هو أن نقول: العقل يوجب النظر وطلب المعرفة لفائدة مرتبة عليه، أو مع الاعتراف بأن وجوده وعدمه في حق الفوائد عاجلاً وآجلاً بمثابة واحدة. فإن قلتم: يقضي بالوجوب مع الاعتراف بأنه لا فائدة فيه قطعاً عاجلاً وآجلاً فهذا حكم الجهل لا حكم العقل؛ فإن العقل لا يأمر بالعبث، وكل ما هو خال عن الفوائد كلها فهو عبث. وإن كان لفائدة فلا يخلو: إما أن ترجع إلى المعبود، أو إلى العبد؛ ومحال أن ترجع إلى المعبود تعالى وتقدس عن الفوائد، وإن رجعت إلى العبد فلا يخلو: إما أن يكون في الحال، أو في المآل؛ أما في الحال فهو تعب محض لا فائدة فيه. وأما في المآل فالمتوقع هو الثواب، ومن أين علم أنه يثاب على فعله؟ بل ربما يعاقب عليه، فالحكم عليه بالثواب حماقة لا أصل له. فإن قيل: يخطر بباله أن له رباً: إن شكره أثابه وأنعم عليه، وإن كفر ... عاقبه عليه، ولا يخطر بباله ألبتة جواز العقوبة على الشكر. والاحتراز عن الضرر الموهوم في قضية العقل كالاحتراز عن المعلوم؟ قلنا: نحن لا ننكر أن العاقل يستحثه طبعه على الاحتراز من الضرر موهوماً ومعلوماً، فلا يمنع من إطلاق اسم الإيجاب على هذا الاستحثاث؛ فإن الاصطلاحات لا مشاحة فيها، ولكن الكلام في ترجيح جهة الفعل على جهة الترك في تقرير الثواب والعقاب، مع العلم بأن الشكر وتركه في حق الله تعالى سيان، لا كالواحد منا؛ فإنه يرتاح بالشكر والثناء، ويهتز له ويستلده، ويتألم بالكفران ويتأذى به، فإذا ظهر استواء الأمرين في حق الله تبارك وتعالى فالترجيح لأحد الجانبين محال، بل ربما يخطر بباله العقاب على الشكر لا نقيضه، وهو أنه يعاقب على الشكر لوجهين: أحدهما: أن اشتغاله به تصرف في فكره وقلبه بإتعابه وصرفه عن الملاذ والشهوات، وهو عبد مربوب خلقت له شهوة وممكن من الشهوات، فلعل المقصود أن يشتغل بلذات نفسه واستيفاء نعم الله تعالى، وألا يتعب نفسه فيما لا فائدة فيه، فهذا الاحتمال أظهر. الثاني: أن يقيس نفسه على من يشكر ملكاً من الملوك؛ بأن يبحث عن صفاته وأخلاقه ومكانه وموضع نومه مع أهله وجميع أسراره الباطنة مجازاة على إنعامه عليه؛ فيقال له: أنت بهذا الشكر مستحق لحز الرقبة؛ فما لك ولهذا الفضول؟ ومن أنت حتى تبحث عن أسرار الملوك وصفاتهم وأفعالهم وأخلاقهم؟ ولماذا لا تشتغل بما يهمك؟ فالذي يطلب معرفة الله تعالى فإنه ينبغي أن يعرف دقائق صفاته تعالى وأفعاله وحكمته وأسراره في أفعاله، وكل ذلك مما لا يؤهل له إلا من منصب؛ فمن أين عرف العبد أنه مستحق لهذا المنصب؟ فاستبان أن مآخذهم أوهام رسخت فيهم من العادات يعارضها أمثالها ولا محيص عنها. تحريجة: إنما قلنا فإن قيل: فإن لم يكن مدرك الوجوب مقتضى العقول أدى ذلك إلى بوجوبه عقلاً لكيلا تبطل المعجزة؛ فهي ثابتة به.
The sixth claim: We claim that if the Law (al-sharʿ) had not come, it would not be obligatory upon servants to know God Most High nor to thank His grace, contrary to the Muʿtazila who said that the intellect alone makes it obligatory. Its proof is that we say: The intellect necessitates reflection (al-naẓar) and seeking knowledge for a benefit connected to it, or with the admission that its existence and non-existence with regard to benefits immediately and in the future are equal. If you say: It decrees obligation while admitting that there is absolutely no benefit in it immediately and in the future—this is the judgment of ignorance, not the judgment of intellect; for the intellect does not command futility (al-ʿabath), and everything devoid of all benefits is futility. And if there is a benefit, it must either revert to the Worshipped (al-maʿbūd) or to the servant; it is impossible that it revert to the Worshipped—exalted and sanctified is He above benefits—and if it reverts to the servant, it must either be in the present state or in the final outcome. As for the present state, it is pure toil with no benefit in it. As for the final outcome, the expected thing is reward; from where does he know that he will be rewarded for his action? Rather, perhaps he will be punished for it. So judging that he will be rewarded is foolishness with no basis. If it is said: It comes to his mind that he has a Lord: if He thanks Him, He rewards Him and bestows favor upon Him, and if he disbelieves ... He punishes him for it, and it never occurs to his mind at all the possibility of punishment for thankfulness. And is guarding against imagined harm in the judgment of the intellect like guarding against known harm? We say: We do not deny that a rational person is impelled by his nature to guard against harm whether imagined or known, so this does not prevent applying the term 'obligation' (al-ījāb) to this impulsion; for terminologies (al-iṣṭilāḥāt) are not to be disputed. But the discussion is about preferring the side of action over the side of abandonment in establishing reward and punishment, while knowing that thankfulness and its abandonment are equal with respect to God Most High, unlike one of us; for he is comforted by thankfulness and praise, is moved by it and delights in it, and is pained by ingratitude and harmed by it. So when the equality of the two matters with respect to God Blessed and Most High becomes apparent, then preferring one side over the other is impossible. Rather, perhaps it occurs to his mind punishment for thankfulness, not its opposite, namely that He punishes for thankfulness for two reasons: One of them: That his occupation with it is a disposal of his thought and heart by tiring him and turning him away from pleasures and appetites, while he is a servant owned, for whom appetite was created and who is enabled to have appetites; perhaps the intention is that he occupy himself with the pleasures of his self and the full enjoyment of God's blessings, and not tire himself in what has no benefit. So this probability is more apparent. The second: That he analogizes himself to one who thanks a king among kings such that he investigates His attributes, His character, His place, the location of His sleep with His family, and all His inner secrets as a reward for His favor upon him; then it is said to him: You, by this thankfulness, deserve the severing of the neck; what business do you have with this meddling? And who are you to investigate the secrets of kings, their attributes, their actions, and their character? Why do you not occupy yourself with what concerns you? So the one who seeks knowledge of God Most High ought to know the fine details of His attributes, His actions, His wisdom, and His secrets in His actions, and all of that is something for which none is qualified except one with status; so from where does the servant know that he is deserving of this status? So it became clear that their sources are illusions deeply rooted in them from customs, which are countered by their likes, and there is no escape from them. A caution: We only said—if it is said: If the means of obligation is not the requirement of intellects, then that leads to it being obligatory by intellect so that the miracle (al-muʿjiza) would not be invalidated; for it is established by it.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the sixth claim in Ghazali's Iqtisad. The claim is that without revelation (al-sharʿ), human beings are not obligated to know God or thank Him, contrary to the Muʿtazila who assert that the intellect alone imposes that obligation. Ghazali provides a proof structured as a series of dialectical exchanges.

First, he states the proof: the intellect necessitates reflection (al-naẓar) only for a benefit (fāʾida). If one admits that the act of reflection has absolutely no benefit, then the intellect cannot command it; that would be a judgment of ignorance, not reason, because reason does not command futility (al-ʿabath). If there is a benefit, it must either revert to God (impossible, since He is transcendent and above benefit) or to the servant. If to the servant, it is either immediate or in the final outcome. Immediate benefit is pure toil; ultimate benefit is reward, but one cannot know he will be rewarded—he might be punished. So no benefit is certain.

Then an objection is raised: a person naturally fears punishment for ingratitude, so the intellect requires thankfulness to avoid harm. Ghazali responds that while a person does guard against imagined harm, this does not amount to a rational obligation (ījāb) in the technical sense. The key point is that thankfulness and its abandonment are equal in God's right—God does not benefit from thanks nor suffer from ingratitude. Unlike humans, God does not take pleasure in praise or feel pain from ingratitude. Therefore, the intellect cannot prefer one over the other.

Furthermore, Ghazali suggests that punishment for thankfulness is conceivable for two reasons. First, engaging in thankfulness may divert the servant from permissible pleasures, perhaps against God's intention that he enjoy blessings. Second, delving into knowledge of God's attributes and secrets is like a subject investigating a king's private affairs, which is punishable. Thus, seeking knowledge of God without divine permission may be an act of impertinence.

Finally, Ghazali notes that these opponents' arguments are rooted in customary illusions. He ends with a caution: if it is said that without intellect-based obligation miracles would be invalid, the reply is that miracles are established by revelation itself, not by intellect alone.

Key Terms
العقل
الوجوب
النظر
الفائدة
العبث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does the intellect require for an action to be obligatory?
A) Divine command
B) Benefit
C) Certainty
D) Tradition

Key Takeaway
Intellect does not command futile actions; thus without a benefit, no obligation exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever performed an act of worship or sought religious knowledge without asking yourself what benefit it brings? What does Ghazali’s argument reveal about your own intentions?
?
If the intellect requires benefit to command an obligation, what benefit do you personally seek from knowing God? Is your pursuit of faith driven by real advantage or mere habit?
?
Consider a time when you felt morally obligated to do something based on your own reasoning alone, without reference to revelation. How does this passage challenge your assumption that your intellect alone can dictate duty?

The Benefit Analysis: No Certain Benefit for Servants
Ghazali examines the possible recipients and types of benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why any potential benefit from knowing God cannot be certain: it cannot benefit God, and for the servant it is either immediate toil or uncertain future reward.

Core Idea
No benefit for God, no certain benefit for the servant.

Having established that the intellect requires a benefit, Ghazali now examines what possible benefit could come from knowing God. He systematically eliminates possibilities, showing that none provide a certain benefit. This analysis is crucial to his overall argument against the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two recipients of benefit
Ghazali says any benefit must revert either to God or to the servant. Think about why benefit to God is impossible.
God is transcendent and does not benefit from creation's actions.
2
Eliminate benefit for God
It is impossible for benefit to revert to God because He is exalted above any need or benefit.
This step is dismissed quickly.
3
Eliminate immediate benefit for the servant
If the benefit is for the servant in the present, it is pure toil with no real benefit.
Ghazali calls it 'تعب محض' (pure toil).
4
Show that future reward is uncertain
The only possible future benefit is reward, but one cannot be sure of reward; punishment is equally possible. Thus, no certain benefit.
Ghazali calls reward expectation 'حماقة لا أصل له' (foolishness with no basis).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى السادسة: ندعي أن الشرع لو لم يرد لكان لا يجب على العباد معرفة الله تعالى ولا شكر نعمته، خلافاً للمعتزلة حيث قالوا إن العقل بمجرده موجب. وبرهانه: هو أن نقول: العقل يوجب النظر وطلب المعرفة لفائدة مرتبة عليه، أو مع الاعتراف بأن وجوده وعدمه في حق الفوائد عاجلاً وآجلاً بمثابة واحدة. فإن قلتم: يقضي بالوجوب مع الاعتراف بأنه لا فائدة فيه قطعاً عاجلاً وآجلاً فهذا حكم الجهل لا حكم العقل؛ فإن العقل لا يأمر بالعبث، وكل ما هو خال عن الفوائد كلها فهو عبث. وإن كان لفائدة فلا يخلو: إما أن ترجع إلى المعبود، أو إلى العبد؛ ومحال أن ترجع إلى المعبود تعالى وتقدس عن الفوائد، وإن رجعت إلى العبد فلا يخلو: إما أن يكون في الحال، أو في المآل؛ أما في الحال فهو تعب محض لا فائدة فيه. وأما في المآل فالمتوقع هو الثواب، ومن أين علم أنه يثاب على فعله؟ بل ربما يعاقب عليه، فالحكم عليه بالثواب حماقة لا أصل له. فإن قيل: يخطر بباله أن له رباً: إن شكره أثابه وأنعم عليه، وإن كفر ... عاقبه عليه، ولا يخطر بباله ألبتة جواز العقوبة على الشكر. والاحتراز عن الضرر الموهوم في قضية العقل كالاحتراز عن المعلوم؟ قلنا: نحن لا ننكر أن العاقل يستحثه طبعه على الاحتراز من الضرر موهوماً ومعلوماً، فلا يمنع من إطلاق اسم الإيجاب على هذا الاستحثاث؛ فإن الاصطلاحات لا مشاحة فيها، ولكن الكلام في ترجيح جهة الفعل على جهة الترك في تقرير الثواب والعقاب، مع العلم بأن الشكر وتركه في حق الله تعالى سيان، لا كالواحد منا؛ فإنه يرتاح بالشكر والثناء، ويهتز له ويستلده، ويتألم بالكفران ويتأذى به، فإذا ظهر استواء الأمرين في حق الله تبارك وتعالى فالترجيح لأحد الجانبين محال، بل ربما يخطر بباله العقاب على الشكر لا نقيضه، وهو أنه يعاقب على الشكر لوجهين: أحدهما: أن اشتغاله به تصرف في فكره وقلبه بإتعابه وصرفه عن الملاذ والشهوات، وهو عبد مربوب خلقت له شهوة وممكن من الشهوات، فلعل المقصود أن يشتغل بلذات نفسه واستيفاء نعم الله تعالى، وألا يتعب نفسه فيما لا فائدة فيه، فهذا الاحتمال أظهر. الثاني: أن يقيس نفسه على من يشكر ملكاً من الملوك؛ بأن يبحث عن صفاته وأخلاقه ومكانه وموضع نومه مع أهله وجميع أسراره الباطنة مجازاة على إنعامه عليه؛ فيقال له: أنت بهذا الشكر مستحق لحز الرقبة؛ فما لك ولهذا الفضول؟ ومن أنت حتى تبحث عن أسرار الملوك وصفاتهم وأفعالهم وأخلاقهم؟ ولماذا لا تشتغل بما يهمك؟ فالذي يطلب معرفة الله تعالى فإنه ينبغي أن يعرف دقائق صفاته تعالى وأفعاله وحكمته وأسراره في أفعاله، وكل ذلك مما لا يؤهل له إلا من منصب؛ فمن أين عرف العبد أنه مستحق لهذا المنصب؟ فاستبان أن مآخذهم أوهام رسخت فيهم من العادات يعارضها أمثالها ولا محيص عنها. تحريجة: إنما قلنا فإن قيل: فإن لم يكن مدرك الوجوب مقتضى العقول أدى ذلك إلى بوجوبه عقلاً لكيلا تبطل المعجزة؛ فهي ثابتة به.
The sixth claim: We claim that if the Law (al-sharʿ) had not come, it would not be obligatory upon servants to know God Most High nor to thank His grace, contrary to the Muʿtazila who said that the intellect alone makes it obligatory. Its proof is that we say: The intellect necessitates reflection (al-naẓar) and seeking knowledge for a benefit connected to it, or with the admission that its existence and non-existence with regard to benefits immediately and in the future are equal. If you say: It decrees obligation while admitting that there is absolutely no benefit in it immediately and in the future—this is the judgment of ignorance, not the judgment of intellect; for the intellect does not command futility (al-ʿabath), and everything devoid of all benefits is futility. And if there is a benefit, it must either revert to the Worshipped (al-maʿbūd) or to the servant; it is impossible that it revert to the Worshipped—exalted and sanctified is He above benefits—and if it reverts to the servant, it must either be in the present state or in the final outcome. As for the present state, it is pure toil with no benefit in it. As for the final outcome, the expected thing is reward; from where does he know that he will be rewarded for his action? Rather, perhaps he will be punished for it. So judging that he will be rewarded is foolishness with no basis. If it is said: It comes to his mind that he has a Lord: if He thanks Him, He rewards Him and bestows favor upon Him, and if he disbelieves ... He punishes him for it, and it never occurs to his mind at all the possibility of punishment for thankfulness. And is guarding against imagined harm in the judgment of the intellect like guarding against known harm? We say: We do not deny that a rational person is impelled by his nature to guard against harm whether imagined or known, so this does not prevent applying the term 'obligation' (al-ījāb) to this impulsion; for terminologies (al-iṣṭilāḥāt) are not to be disputed. But the discussion is about preferring the side of action over the side of abandonment in establishing reward and punishment, while knowing that thankfulness and its abandonment are equal with respect to God Most High, unlike one of us; for he is comforted by thankfulness and praise, is moved by it and delights in it, and is pained by ingratitude and harmed by it. So when the equality of the two matters with respect to God Blessed and Most High becomes apparent, then preferring one side over the other is impossible. Rather, perhaps it occurs to his mind punishment for thankfulness, not its opposite, namely that He punishes for thankfulness for two reasons: One of them: That his occupation with it is a disposal of his thought and heart by tiring him and turning him away from pleasures and appetites, while he is a servant owned, for whom appetite was created and who is enabled to have appetites; perhaps the intention is that he occupy himself with the pleasures of his self and the full enjoyment of God's blessings, and not tire himself in what has no benefit. So this probability is more apparent. The second: That he analogizes himself to one who thanks a king among kings such that he investigates His attributes, His character, His place, the location of His sleep with His family, and all His inner secrets as a reward for His favor upon him; then it is said to him: You, by this thankfulness, deserve the severing of the neck; what business do you have with this meddling? And who are you to investigate the secrets of kings, their attributes, their actions, and their character? Why do you not occupy yourself with what concerns you? So the one who seeks knowledge of God Most High ought to know the fine details of His attributes, His actions, His wisdom, and His secrets in His actions, and all of that is something for which none is qualified except one with status; so from where does the servant know that he is deserving of this status? So it became clear that their sources are illusions deeply rooted in them from customs, which are countered by their likes, and there is no escape from them. A caution: We only said—if it is said: If the means of obligation is not the requirement of intellects, then that leads to it being obligatory by intellect so that the miracle (al-muʿjiza) would not be invalidated; for it is established by it.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the sixth claim in Ghazali's Iqtisad. The claim is that without revelation (al-sharʿ), human beings are not obligated to know God or thank Him, contrary to the Muʿtazila who assert that the intellect alone imposes that obligation. Ghazali provides a proof structured as a series of dialectical exchanges.

First, he states the proof: the intellect necessitates reflection (al-naẓar) only for a benefit (fāʾida). If one admits that the act of reflection has absolutely no benefit, then the intellect cannot command it; that would be a judgment of ignorance, not reason, because reason does not command futility (al-ʿabath). If there is a benefit, it must either revert to God (impossible, since He is transcendent and above benefit) or to the servant. If to the servant, it is either immediate or in the final outcome. Immediate benefit is pure toil; ultimate benefit is reward, but one cannot know he will be rewarded—he might be punished. So no benefit is certain.

Then an objection is raised: a person naturally fears punishment for ingratitude, so the intellect requires thankfulness to avoid harm. Ghazali responds that while a person does guard against imagined harm, this does not amount to a rational obligation (ījāb) in the technical sense. The key point is that thankfulness and its abandonment are equal in God's right—God does not benefit from thanks nor suffer from ingratitude. Unlike humans, God does not take pleasure in praise or feel pain from ingratitude. Therefore, the intellect cannot prefer one over the other.

Furthermore, Ghazali suggests that punishment for thankfulness is conceivable for two reasons. First, engaging in thankfulness may divert the servant from permissible pleasures, perhaps against God's intention that he enjoy blessings. Second, delving into knowledge of God's attributes and secrets is like a subject investigating a king's private affairs, which is punishable. Thus, seeking knowledge of God without divine permission may be an act of impertinence.

Finally, Ghazali notes that these opponents' arguments are rooted in customary illusions. He ends with a caution: if it is said that without intellect-based obligation miracles would be invalid, the reply is that miracles are established by revelation itself, not by intellect alone.

Key Terms
المعبود
العبد
الحال
المآل
الثواب
العقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is immediate benefit from knowing God impossible?
A) Because it is pure toil
B) Because it is enjoyable
C) Because it leads to punishment
D) Because it benefits God

Key Takeaway
No certain benefit can be identified for knowing God through intellect alone, so no rational obligation exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in intellectual reflection about God, do you expect immediate spiritual reward or comfort? After reading Ghazali's words that present state is 'pure toil', will you continue seeking knowledge even if it feels like toil, or do you retreat because you expect ease?
?
You base many of your religious practices on the hope of reward. But Ghazali forces you to ask: 'From where do you know that you will be rewarded?' If your certainty does not come from revelation, is it mere foolishness? Examine your own heart: is your hope in God's promise or in your own reasoning?
?
If you were to lose all hope of reward and certainty of benefit, would you still seek God? Ghazali's argument strips away every worldly and otherworldly incentive. Confront that possibility: is your pursuit of God for God Himself, or for what you hope to gain?
