Rational Judgments
intermediate
10 min
The Objection of Imagined Harm and Ghazali's Response
Ghazali responds to the objection that fear of punishment creates obligation, and explains why even that does not constitute rational obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the natural fear of punishment does not create a rational obligation, and how Ghazali further argues that punishment for thankfulness is conceivable.

Core Idea
Fear of harm is natural, not rational obligation; thankfulness might even lead to punishment.

In this lesson, Ghazali addresses a common objection: doesn't the fear of punishment for ingratitude compel the intellect to require thankfulness? He distinguishes between natural impulse and rational obligation, and goes further to argue that, from a purely rational standpoint, punishment for thankfulness is at least as plausible as reward. This deepens his critique.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection about fear
The objector says that a person naturally fears punishment for ingratitude, so the intellect must require thankfulness to avoid harm.
This is based on imagined harm, not certain harm.
2
Ghazali's distinction between natural impulse and rational obligation
Ghazali agrees that a rational person guards against imagined harm, but this is a natural impulse, not a rational judgment that creates obligation in the technical sense.
Terminologies can be agreed upon, but the real issue is preferring action over inaction.
3
Explore the two reasons why punishment for thankfulness is plausible
First, thankfulness may divert from permitted pleasures. Second, investigating God's attributes is like prying into a king's secrets, which is punishable.
These show that reward is not the only possibility.

The sixth claim: We claim that if the Law (al-sharʿ) had not come, it would not be obligatory upon servants to know God Most High nor to thank His grace, contrary to the Muʿtazila who said that the intellect alone makes it obligatory. Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the sixth claim in Ghazali's Iqtisad. The claim is that without revelation (al-sharʿ), human beings are not obligated to know God or thank Him, contrary to the Muʿtazila who assert that the intellect alone imposes that obligation. First, he states the proof: the intellect necessitates reflection (al-naẓar) only for a benefit (fāʾida). If there is a benefit, it must either revert to God (impossible, since He is transcendent and above benefit) or to the servant. If to the servant, it is either immediate or in the final outcome. Immediate benefit is pure toil; ultimate benefit is reward, but one cannot know he will be rewarded—he might be punished. So no benefit is certain.

Then an objection is raised: a person naturally fears punishment for ingratitude, so the intellect requires thankfulness to avoid harm. Ghazali responds that while a person does guard against imagined harm, this does not amount to a rational obligation (ījāb) in the technical sense. The key point is that thankfulness and its abandonment are equal in God's right—God does not benefit from thanks nor suffer from ingratitude. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect can determine obligation based on imagined harm.
Correction: Ghazali shows that imagined harm does not create a rational obligation; it is merely a natural impulse, and even punishment for thankfulness is conceivable.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does the natural fear of punishment not create a rational obligation?
A) Because it is a natural impulse, not a rational judgment
B) Because the intellect cannot command anything
C) Because reward is certain
D) Because punishment never occurs

Key Takeaway
Rational obligation requires certainty of benefit, which is absent without revelation; natural impulses do not constitute obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever performed an act of worship assuming it automatically obligates God to reward you? How does Ghazali's argument challenge that assumption, and what should replace it?
?
If thankfulness to God could potentially lead to punishment rather than reward, how does that change the way you approach your acts of worship and knowledge-seeking? Write one specific change you will make.
?
What ingrained custom or habit in your own life might be causing you to assume that your good deeds are always beneficial? How can you free yourself from that illusion based on this lesson?

Caution: Miracles Are Not Invalidated
Ghazali adds a caution that his argument does not invalidate miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's final caution that his denial of rational obligation does not undermine the validity of miracles.

Core Idea
Miracles are established by revelation, not rational obligation.

Ghazali concludes with a brief caution. An opponent might argue that if intellect does not obligate knowing God, then the evidential value of miracles would be lost. Ghazali dismisses this, pointing out that miracles themselves are established through revelation, not through pure reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the caution
Read the short Arabic phrase. Ghazali mentions that if the means of obligation were not from intellect, that would imply miracles are invalid, but they are established.
The word 'تحريجة' means caution.
2
Understand its role
This is a brief response to a potential objection. Ghazali implies that miracles are known through revelation, so his argument does not affect their validity.
This concludes the sixth claim.

الدعوى السادسة: ندعي أن الشرع لو لم يرد لكان لا يجب على العباد معرفة الله تعالى ولا شكر نعمته، خلافاً للمعتزلة حيث قالوا إن العقل بمجرده موجب. وبرهانه: هو أن نقول: العقل يوجب النظر وطلب المعرفة لفائدة مرتبة عليه، أو مع الاعتراف بأن وجوده وعدمه في حق الفوائد عاجلاً وآجلاً بمثابة واحدة. فإن قلتم: يقضي بالوجوب مع الاعتراف بأنه لا فائدة فيه قطعاً عاجلاً وآجلاً فهذا حكم الجهل لا حكم العقل؛ فإن العقل لا يأمر بالعبث، وكل ما هو خال عن الفوائد كلها فهو عبث. وإن كان لفائدة فلا يخلو: إما أن ترجع إلى المعبود، أو إلى العبد؛ ومحال أن ترجع إلى المعبود تعالى وتقدس عن الفوائد، وإن رجعت إلى العبد فلا يخلو: إما أن يكون في الحال، أو في المآل؛ أما في الحال فهو تعب محض لا فائدة فيه. وأما في المآل فالمتوقع هو الثواب، ومن أين علم أنه يثاب على فعله؟ بل ربما يعاقب عليه، فالحكم عليه بالثواب حماقة لا أصل له. فإن قيل: يخطر بباله أن له رباً: إن شكره أثابه وأنعم عليه، وإن كفر ... عاقبه عليه، ولا يخطر بباله ألبتة جواز العقوبة على الشكر. والاحتراز عن الضرر الموهوم في قضية العقل كالاحتراز عن المعلوم؟ قلنا: نحن لا ننكر أن العاقل يستحثه طبعه على الاحتراز من الضرر موهوماً ومعلوماً، فلا يمنع من إطلاق اسم الإيجاب على هذا الاستحثاث؛ فإن الاصطلاحات لا مشاحة فيها، ولكن الكلام في ترجيح جهة الفعل على جهة الترك في تقرير الثواب والعقاب، مع العلم بأن الشكر وتركه في حق الله تعالى سيان، لا كالواحد منا؛ فإنه يرتاح بالشكر والثناء، ويهتز له ويستلده، ويتألم بالكفران ويتأذى به، فإذا ظهر استواء الأمرين في حق الله تبارك وتعالى فالترجيح لأحد الجانبين محال، بل ربما يخطر بباله العقاب على الشكر لا نقيضه، وهو أنه يعاقب على الشكر لوجهين: أحدهما: أن اشتغاله به تصرف في فكره وقلبه بإتعابه وصرفه عن الملاذ والشهوات، وهو عبد مربوب خلقت له شهوة وممكن من الشهوات، فلعل المقصود أن يشتغل بلذات نفسه واستيفاء نعم الله تعالى، وألا يتعب نفسه فيما لا فائدة فيه، فهذا الاحتمال أظهر. الثاني: أن يقيس نفسه على من يشكر ملكاً من الملوك؛ بأن يبحث عن صفاته وأخلاقه ومكانه وموضع نومه مع أهله وجميع أسراره الباطنة مجازاة على إنعامه عليه؛ فيقال له: أنت بهذا الشكر مستحق لحز الرقبة؛ فما لك ولهذا الفضول؟ ومن أنت حتى تبحث عن أسرار الملوك وصفاتهم وأفعالهم وأخلاقهم؟ ولماذا لا تشتغل بما يهمك؟ فالذي يطلب معرفة الله تعالى فإنه ينبغي أن يعرف دقائق صفاته تعالى وأفعاله وحكمته وأسراره في أفعاله، وكل ذلك مما لا يؤهل له إلا من منصب؛ فمن أين عرف العبد أنه مستحق لهذا المنصب؟ فاستبان أن مآخذهم أوهام رسخت فيهم من العادات يعارضها أمثالها ولا محيص عنها. تحريجة: إنما قلنا فإن قيل: فإن لم يكن مدرك الوجوب مقتضى العقول أدى ذلك إلى بوجوبه عقلاً لكيلا تبطل المعجزة؛ فهي ثابتة به.
The sixth claim: We claim that if the Law (al-sharʿ) had not come, it would not be obligatory upon servants to know God Most High nor to thank His grace, contrary to the Muʿtazila who said that the intellect alone makes it obligatory. Its proof is that we say: The intellect necessitates reflection (al-naẓar) and seeking knowledge for a benefit connected to it, or with the admission that its existence and non-existence with regard to benefits immediately and in the future are equal. If you say: It decrees obligation while admitting that there is absolutely no benefit in it immediately and in the future—this is the judgment of ignorance, not the judgment of intellect; for the intellect does not command futility (al-ʿabath), and everything devoid of all benefits is futility. And if there is a benefit, it must either revert to the Worshipped (al-maʿbūd) or to the servant; it is impossible that it revert to the Worshipped—exalted and sanctified is He above benefits—and if it reverts to the servant, it must either be in the present state or in the final outcome. As for the present state, it is pure toil with no benefit in it. As for the final outcome, the expected thing is reward; from where does he know that he will be rewarded for his action? Rather, perhaps he will be punished for it. So judging that he will be rewarded is foolishness with no basis. If it is said: It comes to his mind that he has a Lord: if He thanks Him, He rewards Him and bestows favor upon Him, and if he disbelieves ... He punishes him for it, and it never occurs to his mind at all the possibility of punishment for thankfulness. And is guarding against imagined harm in the judgment of the intellect like guarding against known harm? We say: We do not deny that a rational person is impelled by his nature to guard against harm whether imagined or known, so this does not prevent applying the term 'obligation' (al-ījāb) to this impulsion; for terminologies (al-iṣṭilāḥāt) are not to be disputed. But the discussion is about preferring the side of action over the side of abandonment in establishing reward and punishment, while knowing that thankfulness and its abandonment are equal with respect to God Most High, unlike one of us; for he is comforted by thankfulness and praise, is moved by it and delights in it, and is pained by ingratitude and harmed by it. So when the equality of the two matters with respect to God Blessed and Most High becomes apparent, then preferring one side over the other is impossible. Rather, perhaps it occurs to his mind punishment for thankfulness, not its opposite, namely that He punishes for thankfulness for two reasons: One of them: That his occupation with it is a disposal of his thought and heart by tiring him and turning him away from pleasures and appetites, while he is a servant owned, for whom appetite was created and who is enabled to have appetites; perhaps the intention is that he occupy himself with the pleasures of his self and the full enjoyment of God's blessings, and not tire himself in what has no benefit. So this probability is more apparent. The second: That he analogizes himself to one who thanks a king among kings such that he investigates His attributes, His character, His place, the location of His sleep with His family, and all His inner secrets as a reward for His favor upon him; then it is said to him: You, by this thankfulness, deserve the severing of the neck; what business do you have with this meddling? And who are you to investigate the secrets of kings, their attributes, their actions, and their character? Why do you not occupy yourself with what concerns you? So the one who seeks knowledge of God Most High ought to know the fine details of His attributes, His actions, His wisdom, and His secrets in His actions, and all of that is something for which none is qualified except one with status; so from where does the servant know that he is deserving of this status? So it became clear that their sources are illusions deeply rooted in them from customs, which are countered by their likes, and there is no escape from them. A caution: We only said—if it is said: If the means of obligation is not the requirement of intellects, then that leads to it being obligatory by intellect so that the miracle (al-muʿjiza) would not be invalidated; for it is established by it.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the sixth claim in Ghazali's Iqtisad. The claim is that without revelation (al-sharʿ), human beings are not obligated to know God or thank Him, contrary to the Muʿtazila who assert that the intellect alone imposes that obligation. Ghazali provides a proof structured as a series of dialectical exchanges.

First, he states the proof: the intellect necessitates reflection (al-naẓar) only for a benefit (fāʾida). If one admits that the act of reflection has absolutely no benefit, then the intellect cannot command it; that would be a judgment of ignorance, not reason, because reason does not command futility (al-ʿabath). If there is a benefit, it must either revert to God (impossible, since He is transcendent and above benefit) or to the servant. If to the servant, it is either immediate or in the final outcome. Immediate benefit is pure toil; ultimate benefit is reward, but one cannot know he will be rewarded—he might be punished. So no benefit is certain.

Then an objection is raised: a person naturally fears punishment for ingratitude, so the intellect requires thankfulness to avoid harm. Ghazali responds that while a person does guard against imagined harm, this does not amount to a rational obligation (ījāb) in the technical sense. The key point is that thankfulness and its abandonment are equal in God's right—God does not benefit from thanks nor suffer from ingratitude. Unlike humans, God does not take pleasure in praise or feel pain from ingratitude. Therefore, the intellect cannot prefer one over the other.

Furthermore, Ghazali suggests that punishment for thankfulness is conceivable for two reasons. First, engaging in thankfulness may divert the servant from permissible pleasures, perhaps against God's intention that he enjoy blessings. Second, delving into knowledge of God's attributes and secrets is like a subject investigating a king's private affairs, which is punishable. Thus, seeking knowledge of God without divine permission may be an act of impertinence.

Finally, Ghazali notes that these opponents' arguments are rooted in customary illusions. He ends with a caution: if it is said that without intellect-based obligation miracles would be invalid, the reply is that miracles are established by revelation itself, not by intellect alone.

Key Terms
المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the basis for the validity of miracles?
A) Intellect alone
B) Revelation
C) Consensus
D) Analogy

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's denial of rational obligation does not undermine miracles because they are known through revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever feel the need to rationally prove every aspect of your faith? How does Ghazali's separation between rational obligation and revelation free you from that burden, and what does that imply for your trust in Allah's messages?
?
When you hear about a miracle from the Prophet (peace be upon him), do you sometimes feel doubt because you cannot explain it intellectually? After this lesson, will you allow the revelation itself to be sufficient ground for belief? Write down one specific miracle of the Prophet that you struggle to accept, and commit today to believing it solely because Allah has informed us through authentic transmission.
?
Ghazali's caution protects miracles from being dismissed by those who demand rational necessity. Is there any miracle in your own life story—an answer to a prayer, a guidance—that you have been hesitant to call a miracle because it seemed 'coincidental'? How would Ghazali's argument encourage you to recognize it as a true sign from Allah?

The True Meaning of Obligation
Ghazali defines obligation as God's preponderance, not rational benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between natural impetus and true obligation (wujub) as defined by Ghazali, and why obligation cannot be based on rational benefit alone.

Core Idea
True obligation is not about avoiding harm, but about God's declaration of reward and punishment.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to an objection that obligation is merely a rational judgment to avoid harm. He clarifies that while rational people naturally avoid harm, the term 'obligation' in Islamic theology refers to the preponderance (tarjih) of one action over another in terms of reward and punishment, established by God. Since for God, gratitude and ingratitude are equal, preponderance cannot arise from human benefit alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the objector's claim that avoidance of imagined harm is like avoidance of known harm.
This sets up the debate about the basis of obligation.
2
Understand Ghazali's distinction
Ghazali admits natural impetus to avoid harm but shifts the discussion to true obligation (wujub) as preponderance (tarjih) in reward and punishment.
He is redefining the terms of the debate.
3
Note the equality of gratitude and ingratitude for God
For God, thankfulness and ingratitude are equal in terms of benefit or harm to Him, so rational benefit cannot determine preponderance.
This is key: obligation is not about human benefit.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this means obligation must be defined by divine command, not rational judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاحتراز عن الضرر الموهوم في قضية العقل كالاحتراز عن المعلوم ؟ قلنا : نحن لا ننكر أن العاقل يستحثُه طبعه على الاحتراز من الضرر موهوماً ومعلوماً، فلا يمنع من إطلاق اسم الإيجاب على هذا الاستحثاث ؛ فإن الاصطلاحاتِ لا مُشاحَّةَ فيها ، ولكن الكلام في ترجيح جهة الفعل على جهة الترك في تقرير الثواب والعقاب ، مع العلم بأن الشكر وتركه في حقٌّ الله تعالى سيان ، لا كالواحد منا ؛ فإنه يرتاح بالشكر والثناء ، ويهتز له ويستلده ، ويتألمُ بالكفران ويتأذَّى به ، فإذا ظهر استواء الأمرين في حق الله تبارك وتعالى . . فالترجيح لأحد الجانبين محال ، بل ربَّما يخطر بباله العقاب على الشكر لا نقيضُه ، وهو أنَّه يعاقب على الشكر لوجهين : يبعد عقلا أحدهما : أن اشتغالَهُ به تصرُّفٌ فى فكره وقلبه بإتعابه وصَرْفِهِ عن الملاذُ والشهوات ، وهو عبد مربوب خلقت له شهوةٌ ومُكِّن من الشهوات ، فلعلَّ المقصود أن يشتغل بلذَاتِ نفسِهِ واستيفاء نِعَمِ الله تعالى ، وألا يتعب نفسَهُ فيما لا فائدة الله تعالى فيه ، فهذا الاحتمال أظهر الثاني : أن يقيس نفسَهُ على من يشكرُ ملكاً من الملوك ؛ بأن يبحث عن صفاته وأخلاقه ومكانه وموضع نومه مع أهله وجميع أسراره الباطنة مجازاة على إنعامه عليه ؛ فيقال له : أنت بهذا الشكرِ مستحق لحز الرقبة ؛ فما لك ولهذا الفضول ؟ ومن أنت حتى تبحث عن أسرار الملوك وصفاتهم وأفعالهم وأخلاقهم ؟ ولماذا لا تشتغل بما يهمُّكَ ؟ فالذي يطلب معرفة الله تعالى فإنه ينبغي أن يعرف دقائق صفاتِهِ تعالى وأفعاله وحكمته وأسراره في أفعاله ، وكلُّ ذلك مما لا يؤهل له إلا من منصب ؛ فمن أين عرف العبد أنه مستحق لهذا المنصب ؟ له فاستبان أن مآخذهم أوهام رسخت فيهم من العاداتِ يعارضها أمثالها ولا محيص عنها
And [the objection]: Is avoidance of imagined harm in a matter of reason like avoidance of known harm? We said: We do not deny that the rational person is urged by his nature to avoid harm, imagined and known, and this does not prevent applying the term 'obligation' to this urging; for there is no dispute over terminologies. But the discussion is about the preponderance of the side of action over the side of inaction in establishing reward and punishment, while knowing that thankfulness and its abandonment are equal with respect to God Almighty, not like one of us; for he is delighted by thanks and praise, stirred by it and finds pleasure in it, and feels pain by ingratitude and is harmed by it. So when the equality of the two matters becomes apparent in the right of God Almighty, then preponderance of one side over the other is impossible. Rather, perhaps it may occur to one that punishment for thankfulness is not its opposite, and that He punishes for thankfulness for two reasons, rationally remote: The first: that occupying oneself with it is a manipulation of one's mind and heart by tiring oneself and diverting from pleasures and desires, and he is a servant owned, for whom desire was created and who was enabled to have desires; perhaps the intended purpose is that he should occupy himself with his own pleasures and the full enjoyment of God's blessings, and not tire himself in what has no benefit for God Almighty; this possibility is more apparent. The second: that he measures himself against one who thanks a king from among kings, by researching his attributes, his character, his place, his sleeping place with his family, and all his inner secrets, as a reward for his favor upon him; so it is said to him: You, by this thanks, deserve the cutting of the neck; What do you have to do with this meddling? And who are you that you research the secrets of kings and their attributes and their actions and their characters? And why do you not occupy yourself with what concerns you? So one who seeks knowledge of God Almighty, he ought to know the fine points of His attributes and His actions and His wisdom and His secrets in His actions, and all that is not qualified for except one of position; so from where does the servant know that he deserves this position? So it became clear that their premises are illusions ingrained in them from habits, countered by their likes, and there is no escape from that.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section defining the nature of obligation (wujub). The objector suggests that avoiding imagined harm is like avoiding known harm, implying a rational basis for obligation. Ghazali responds by distinguishing between natural impetus and true preponderance (tarjih) in reward and punishment. He argues that since for God gratitude and ingratitude are equal, preponderance cannot come from reason alone. He then explores two hypothetical reasons why God might punish for thankfulness—neither is persuasive. First, that thankfulness involves effort and distraction from pleasures, but this is a weak analogy. Second, that it resembles investigating a king's secrets, which is impertinent. Ghazali concludes that the objector's premises are rooted in habit and illusion, not rational necessity.

Key Terms
إيجاب
ترجيح
ثواب وعقاب
سيان
قضية العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Obligation is simply what reason deems necessary to avoid harm.
Correction: Ghazali explains that while natural avoidance of harm is real, true obligation (wujub) refers to God's preponderance in reward and punishment, which is not determined by human reason.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali identify as the real issue in defining obligation?
A) Natural avoidance of harm
B) Preponderance in reward and punishment, established by God
C) The benefit of the action itself
D) The habit of avoiding harm

Key Takeaway
Obligation is not about what reason dictates for our benefit; it is about God's determination of reward and punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your acts of worship do not benefit God at all, why do you perform them? Is it out of fear of punishment, hope for reward, or pure submission to His command? Which motive dominates your heart right now?
?
When you sin, do you sometimes console yourself by thinking 'it doesn't harm God'? What does that reveal about your understanding of obligation—and about your relationship with Him?
?
True obligation means acting because God commanded, not because your reason finds it beneficial. What is one area of your life where you are still seeking rational justification to obey, rather than submitting unconditionally? Will you surrender that to Him today?

Rational Judgments

Two Hypothetical Reasons for Punishment for Thankfulness
Ghazali presents and refutes two reasons for punishment for thankfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two hypothetical arguments for why God might punish thankfulness, and why these arguments are rationally weak.

Core Idea
Even if punishment for thankfulness is conceivable, the arguments for it are rationally remote.

After establishing that preponderance cannot be rationally determined, Ghazali anticipates an objection that God might punish for thankfulness. He presents two such possible reasons—neither convincing. The first suggests that thankfulness involves effort and distraction from pleasures. The second compares it to investigating a king's secrets. Both arguments show that the objector's premises are rooted in habit and illusion, not rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's first reason for potential punishment
The first reason: thankfulness tires the servant and diverts from pleasures, assuming God wants the servant to enjoy pleasures without effort.
Ghazali calls this possibility 'more apparent' but still rationally remote.
2
Read the second reason
The second reason: thankfulness is like investigating a king's secrets, which is impertinent; but seeking knowledge of God is not analogous.
3
Understand the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that these objections are based on illusions ingrained by habit, not sound rational arguments.
This reinforces that true obligation is not grounded in human reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاحتراز عن الضرر الموهوم في قضية العقل كالاحتراز عن المعلوم ؟ قلنا : نحن لا ننكر أن العاقل يستحثُه طبعه على الاحتراز من الضرر موهوماً ومعلوماً، فلا يمنع من إطلاق اسم الإيجاب على هذا الاستحثاث ؛ فإن الاصطلاحاتِ لا مُشاحَّةَ فيها ، ولكن الكلام في ترجيح جهة الفعل على جهة الترك في تقرير الثواب والعقاب ، مع العلم بأن الشكر وتركه في حقٌّ الله تعالى سيان ، لا كالواحد منا ؛ فإنه يرتاح بالشكر والثناء ، ويهتز له ويستلده ، ويتألمُ بالكفران ويتأذَّى به ، فإذا ظهر استواء الأمرين في حق الله تبارك وتعالى . . فالترجيح لأحد الجانبين محال ، بل ربَّما يخطر بباله العقاب على الشكر لا نقيضُه ، وهو أنَّه يعاقب على الشكر لوجهين : يبعد عقلا أحدهما : أن اشتغالَهُ به تصرُّفٌ فى فكره وقلبه بإتعابه وصَرْفِهِ عن الملاذُ والشهوات ، وهو عبد مربوب خلقت له شهوةٌ ومُكِّن من الشهوات ، فلعلَّ المقصود أن يشتغل بلذَاتِ نفسِهِ واستيفاء نِعَمِ الله تعالى ، وألا يتعب نفسَهُ فيما لا فائدة الله تعالى فيه ، فهذا الاحتمال أظهر الثاني : أن يقيس نفسَهُ على من يشكرُ ملكاً من الملوك ؛ بأن يبحث عن صفاته وأخلاقه ومكانه وموضع نومه مع أهله وجميع أسراره الباطنة مجازاة على إنعامه عليه ؛ فيقال له : أنت بهذا الشكرِ مستحق لحز الرقبة ؛ فما لك ولهذا الفضول ؟ ومن أنت حتى تبحث عن أسرار الملوك وصفاتهم وأفعالهم وأخلاقهم ؟ ولماذا لا تشتغل بما يهمُّكَ ؟ فالذي يطلب معرفة الله تعالى فإنه ينبغي أن يعرف دقائق صفاتِهِ تعالى وأفعاله وحكمته وأسراره في أفعاله ، وكلُّ ذلك مما لا يؤهل له إلا من منصب ؛ فمن أين عرف العبد أنه مستحق لهذا المنصب ؟ له فاستبان أن مآخذهم أوهام رسخت فيهم من العاداتِ يعارضها أمثالها ولا محيص عنها
And [the objection]: Is avoidance of imagined harm in a matter of reason like avoidance of known harm? We said: We do not deny that the rational person is urged by his nature to avoid harm, imagined and known, and this does not prevent applying the term 'obligation' to this urging; for there is no dispute over terminologies. But the discussion is about the preponderance of the side of action over the side of inaction in establishing reward and punishment, while knowing that thankfulness and its abandonment are equal with respect to God Almighty, not like one of us; for he is delighted by thanks and praise, stirred by it and finds pleasure in it, and feels pain by ingratitude and is harmed by it. So when the equality of the two matters becomes apparent in the right of God Almighty, then preponderance of one side over the other is impossible. Rather, perhaps it may occur to one that punishment for thankfulness is not its opposite, and that He punishes for thankfulness for two reasons, rationally remote: The first: that occupying oneself with it is a manipulation of one's mind and heart by tiring oneself and diverting from pleasures and desires, and he is a servant owned, for whom desire was created and who was enabled to have desires; perhaps the intended purpose is that he should occupy himself with his own pleasures and the full enjoyment of God's blessings, and not tire himself in what has no benefit for God Almighty; this possibility is more apparent. The second: that he measures himself against one who thanks a king from among kings, by researching his attributes, his character, his place, his sleeping place with his family, and all his inner secrets, as a reward for his favor upon him; so it is said to him: You, by this thanks, deserve the cutting of the neck; What do you have to do with this meddling? And who are you that you research the secrets of kings and their attributes and their actions and their characters? And why do you not occupy yourself with what concerns you? So one who seeks knowledge of God Almighty, he ought to know the fine points of His attributes and His actions and His wisdom and His secrets in His actions, and all that is not qualified for except one of position; so from where does the servant know that he deserves this position? So it became clear that their premises are illusions ingrained in them from habits, countered by their likes, and there is no escape from that.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section defining the nature of obligation (wujub). The objector suggests that avoiding imagined harm is like avoiding known harm, implying a rational basis for obligation. Ghazali responds by distinguishing between natural impetus and true preponderance (tarjih) in reward and punishment. He argues that since for God gratitude and ingratitude are equal, preponderance cannot come from reason alone. He then explores two hypothetical reasons why God might punish for thankfulness—neither is persuasive. First, that thankfulness involves effort and distraction from pleasures, but this is a weak analogy. Second, that it resembles investigating a king's secrets, which is impertinent. Ghazali concludes that the objector's premises are rooted in habit and illusion, not rational necessity.

Key Terms
شكر
كفران
عقاب
عادة
وهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God might punish for thankfulness based on plausible reasoning.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the arguments for such punishment are rationally remote and based on habit, not necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, the first reason for punishment for thankfulness is that it involves
A) Investigating secrets
B) Tiring oneself and diverting from pleasures
C) Ingratitude
D) Harm to others

Key Takeaway
The arguments that God might punish thankfulness are not rationally sound; they stem from habit and illusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find your acts of worship tiring, do you ever resent the effort? What does it reveal about your heart if you feel God is burdening you? How can this passage reshape that feeling?
?
Have you ever caught yourself comparing your relationship with Allah to a human transaction, as if your worship is a favor to Him? In what subtle ways does the second reason expose that pride?
?
Do you rely more on what feels familiar or customary in your faith than on rational certainty? Ask yourself: Are there beliefs about God's punishment or reward that you hold simply because 'everyone says so'? What would it take to uproot those illusions?

The Chain of Obligation: God as the Sole Obligator
Ghazali answers an objection by assigning distinct roles in obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali assigns distinct roles to God, prophet, miracle, intellect, and nature in the process of obligation, and why God alone is the true obligator.

Core Idea
Only God obliges; all else are instruments in a divinely ordained chain.

This passage confronts a subtle objection: if the intellect drives us to act out of fear, does that make the intellect the source of obligation? Ghazali answers by carefully assigning each factor its proper role. Read his response to see how the chain of obligation works step by step, ending with God as the sole obligator.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector claims that since the intellect anticipates punishment and impels caution, the intellect itself becomes the obligator. Notice the force of this argument.
The objector's reasoning seems plausible at first glance.
2
Identify Ghazali's key move
Ghazali redefines obligation as preponderance and asserts that only God can preponderate acts by linking reward or punishment. This shifts the focus from the intellect to God.
3
Trace the chain of causes
Follow the sequence: God (preponderator) → prophet (informer) → miracle (evidence) → looking → intellect (tool) → nature (motivator). Each has a distinct function.
Notice that nature motivates only after understanding, which requires the intellect, which requires hearing from the prophet.
4
Observe the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that no created thing is the source of obligation; all are instruments. He then summarizes the correct terminology.
The final line may be cut off but the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فقد رجع الأمر إلى أنَّ العقل هو الموجب من حيث إنه بسماع النظر في ذلك على كلامه ودعواه يتوقَّع عقاباً ، فيحملُهُ العقل على الحذر ، ولا يحصل إلا بالنظر ، فيجب عليه النظرُ ؟
قلنا : الحق الذي يكشف الغطاء عن هذا من غير اتباع رسم وتقليد أمر أن الوجوب - كما بان - عبارةٌ عن نوع رجحان في الفعل ، والموجب هو الله تعالى ؛ لأنه المرجح ، والرسول مخبر عن الترجح ، والمعجزة دليل على صدقه في الخبر ، والنظرُ سبب في معرفة الصدق ، والعقل آلة النظر ولفهم معنى الخبر ، والطبع مستحثّ على الحذر بعد فهم المحذور بالعقل ، فلا بد من طبع تخالفه العقوبة الموعودة ويوافقه الثواب الموعود ليكون مستحقاً ، ولكن لا يستحثُ ما لم يفهم المحذور ولم يقدره ظناً أو علماً ، ولا يفهم إلا بالعقل ، والعقلُ لا يفهم الترجيح بنفسه بل بسماعه من الرسول ، والرسولُ ليس يرجُحُ الفعل على الترك بنفسه ، بل الله هو المرجح ، والرسول مخبرٌ ، وصدق الرسول لا يظهر بنفسه بل بالمعجزة والمعجزة لا تدلُّ ما لم يُنظر فيها ، والنظر بالعقل
فإذن ؛ قد انكشفت المعاني
والصحيح في الألفاظ أن يقال : الوجوب هو الرجحان ، والموجب هو الله تعالى ، والمخبرُ هو الرسول ، والمعرَّفُ للمحذور وصدق الرسول
If it is said: Then the matter reverts to the intellect being the obligator, in that by hearing the consideration of that regarding his speech and claim, it anticipates punishment, so the intellect impels him to caution, and caution is only attained through looking, so looking becomes obligatory for him? We say: The truth that removes the veil from this, without following custom and imitation, is that obligation - as has become clear - is an expression for a type of preponderance in the act. The obligator is God Almighty, because He is the preponderator. The prophet is an informer about the preponderance. The miracle is evidence of his truthfulness in the information. Looking is a means of knowing the truthfulness. The intellect is the instrument of looking and for understanding the meaning of the information. Nature (al-tab‘) is a motivator for caution after understanding the danger by the intellect. So there must be a nature that is averse to the promised punishment and agrees with the promised reward, so that it becomes deserving. But it does not motivate unless it understands the danger and appraises it as probable or certain. And it only understands by the intellect. And the intellect does not understand the preponderance by itself, but rather by hearing it from the prophet. And the prophet does not preponderate the act over abstention by himself, but God is the preponderator. The prophet is an informer. And the truthfulness of the prophet does not become apparent by itself, but by the miracle. And the miracle does not indicate unless looked into, and looking is by the intellect. So then, the meanings have been uncovered. And the correct usage in words is to say: obligation is preponderance, the obligator is God Almighty, the informer is the prophet, and the identifier of the danger and of the truthfulness of the prophet is [the intellect? - the text cuts off].
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a second objection and its answer. The objector argues that if the prophet informs of punishment for not looking, the intellect understands this and feels caution, thus impels looking – so the intellect becomes the source of obligation. Ghazali's answer distinguishes the roles: obligation is preponderance, and God alone is the preponderator (the one who makes an act preponderant by linking reward/punishment). The prophet only informs of this preponderance; the miracle verifies his truthfulness; looking is the means to know that truthfulness; the intellect is the tool for looking and understanding; and human nature (tab‘) is what motivates caution after understanding. But nature only motivates after understanding, and understanding comes from the intellect. However, the intellect does not grasp the preponderance by itself – it requires hearing from the prophet. So no single created thing is the source of obligation; all are instruments in a chain that ends with God as the sole obligator. Ghazali concludes by summarizing the correct terminology: obligation is preponderance, the obligator is God, the informer is the prophet, and the identifier of the danger and of the prophet's truthfulness is [the intellect?].

طبع
موجب
مرجح
مخبر
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect itself obliges actions because it anticipates reward or punishment.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that the intellect only understands and is a tool; obligation (preponderance) comes from God alone.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali in this passage, what is the role of human nature (al-tab‘) in the process of obligation?
A) It preponderates the act over abstention
B) It motivates caution after understanding the danger
C) It is the source of the obligation itself
D) It verifies the truthfulness of the prophet

Key Takeaway
Only God truly obliges; all other factors—prophet, miracle, intellect, and human nature—are instruments in the process.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that only God truly obliges, yet how many times do you act as if human approval, fear of people, or social pressure is the real obligator in your life? What would change today if you truly internalized that only God's command carries ultimate weight?
?
The intellect is described as an instrument, not the source of obligation. Are you using your intellect to submit to God's revelation, or have you made your own reasoning the final judge of right and wrong? What is one area where you need to subordinate your intellect to the divine command?
?
You have heard the prophet's message and witnessed the signs, but have you honestly and actively looked with your intellect, or have you been content to follow the custom of your family or society? What is one specific belief or practice you hold that you have never critically examined in light of the evidence? Commit to examining it today.

The Prophet's Role and the Objection from Reason
Ghazali presents an analogy and an objection regarding the basis of obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the physician analogy to illustrate that the prophet only conveys the message, and the objection that reason might itself impose obligation.

Core Idea
The prophet only conveys; reason alone cannot impose obligation without revelation.

Ghazali opens with a striking analogy: a patient who refuses treatment until he receives rational proof would die, but the doctor is blameless. Similarly, the prophet's duty is simply to convey and guide; people's success or failure is their own. However, this raises an objection: if hearing the prophet's warning leads reason to anticipate punishment, does that mean reason itself makes reflection (naẓar) obligatory? This lesson explores that initial tension.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy
Read the physician-patient analogy carefully. Notice how it frames responsibility: the patient's refusal does not harm the doctor.
The analogy parallels the prophet's relationship to his community.
2
Understand the prophet's role
Identify Ghazali's conclusion that the prophet only conveys and guides; he is not responsible for outcomes.
3
Examine the objection
The objection argues that reason, upon hearing the prophet's warning, anticipates punishment and thus imposes caution—making reflection obligatory by reason alone.
Note the phrasing: 'reason impels him to caution'.
4
Reflect on the tension
Consider why the objection is problematic: if reason can impose obligation, what role remains for revelation?
This sets up Ghazali's answer in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشفى ، فإن أستاذي غني عن بقائك ، وأنا أيضاً كذلك ، فعند هذا لو التالف المريض : هذا يجب علي بالعقل أو بقولك ، وما لم يظهر لي هذا لم أشتغل بالتجربة . . كان مُهْلِكاً نفسَهُ ، ولم يكن على الطبيب ضرر . فكذلك النبي قد أخيرَهُ الله تعالى بأن الطاعة شفاء ، والمعصية داء ، وأنَّ الإيمان مسعد ، والكفر مهلك ، وأخبره بأنه غني عن العالمين سعدوا أم شقوا ، فإنما شأن الرسول أن يبلغ ويرشد إلى طريق المعرفة وينصرف ، فمن نظَرَ . . فلنفسه ، ومن قصَّرَ .. فعليها ، وهذا واضح . تحريجة ومدار فإن قيل : فقد رجع الأمر إلى أنَّ العقل هو الموجب من حيث إنه بسماع النظر في ذلك على كلامه ودعواه يتوقَّع عقاباً ، فيحملُهُ العقل على الحذر ، ولا يحصل إلا العقل بالنظر ، فيجب عليه النظرُ ؟ هو هو قلنا : الحق الذي يكشف الغطاء عن هذا من غير اتباع رسم وتقليد أمر أن الوجوب - كما بان - عبارةٌ عن نوع رجحان في الفعل ، والموجب هو الله تعالى ؛ لأنه المرجح ، والرسول مخبر عن الترجح ، والمعجزة دليل على صدقه في الخبر ، والنظرُ سبب في معرفة الصدق ، والعقل آلة النظر ولفهم معنى الخبر ، والطبع مستحثّ على الحذر بعد فهم المحذور بالعقل ، فلا بد من طبع تخالفه العقوبة الموعودة ويوافقه الثواب الموعود ليكون مستحقاً ، ولكن لا يستحثُ ما لم يفهم المحذور ولم يقدره ظناً أو علماً ، ولا يفهم إلا بالعقل ، والعقلُ لا يفهم الترجيح بنفسه بل بسماعه من الرسول ، والرسولُ ليس يرجُحُ الفعل على الترك بنفسه ، بل الله هو المرجح ، والرسول مخبرٌ ، وصدق الرسول لا يظهر بنفسه بل بالمعجزة والمعجزة لا تدلُّ ما لم يُنظر فيها ، والنظر بالعقل فإذن ؛ قد انكشفت المعاني والصحيح في الألفاظ أن يقال : الوجوب هو الرجحان ، والموجب هو الله تعالى ، والمخبرُ هو الرسول ، والمعرَّفُ للمحذور وصدق الرسول هو العقل ، والمستحثُ على سلوك سبيل الخلاص هو الطبع ، فهكذا ينبغي أن يفهم الحق في هذه المسألة ، ولا يلتفتُ إلى الكلام المعتاد الذي لا يشفي الغليل ولا يزيل الغموض
It cures, for my teacher is independent of your remaining, and I also am so. At this point, if the patient perished [saying]: 'This is obligatory upon me by reason or by your saying, and as long as this is not clear to me I will not engage in experiment,' he would have killed himself, and there would be no harm to the physician. Likewise, the Prophet — God informed him that obedience is a cure, disobedience a disease, that faith brings happiness, unbelief destruction, and informed him that God is independent of the worlds whether they are happy or miserable. The mission of the messenger is only to convey and guide to the path of knowledge and then depart. So whoever reflects — for his own soul, and whoever falls short — against it. This is clear. A twist and a pivot? Then it is said: 'Then the matter returns to the point that reason is what imposes obligation, insofar as by hearing the consideration of that (the revelation) concerning his speech and claim, it anticipates punishment, so reason impels him to caution, and (caution) is not attained except by reason through reflection, so reflection is obligatory upon him?' We reply: The truth that unveils this without following convention and imitation is that obligation — as has been shown — is an expression of a type of preponderance in the action, and the Obligator is God Most High because He is the one who gives preponderance. The messenger is the informer about the preponderance. The miracle is evidence of his truthfulness in the report. Reflection is the cause of knowing the truthfulness. Reason is the instrument of reflection and of understanding the meaning of the report. Nature is the impeller to caution after understanding the danger by reason. There must be a nature that opposes the promised punishment and agrees with the promised reward so that he becomes deserving. But (nature) does not impel unless it understands the danger and estimates it by conjecture or knowledge, and it does not understand except by reason. Reason does not understand the preponderance by itself but by hearing it from the messenger. The messenger does not give preponderance to action over abandonment by himself; rather, God is the one who gives preponderance. The messenger is an informer. The truthfulness of the messenger is not apparent by itself but by the miracle. The miracle does not indicate unless one reflects on it. Reflection is by reason. Therefore, the meanings have been unveiled. The correct expression is to say: Obligation is the preponderance. The Obligator is God. The informer is the messenger. The identifier of the danger and the truthfulness of the messenger is reason. The impeller to follow the path of salvation is nature. This is how the truth in this matter should be understood, and one should not pay attention to ordinary talk that does not quench the thirst nor remove ambiguity.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument that clarifies the mechanism of obligation (al-wujūb) and the precise roles of God, the prophet, reason (al-ʿaql), and nature (al-ṭabʿ). The passage begins with an analogy: a patient who refuses treatment until it is proven to him by reason or the doctor's word would kill himself, but the doctor is not at fault. Similarly, the prophet's duty is only to convey and guide; people's failure or success is their own. Then an objection is raised: if reason is what makes obligation effective because it anticipates punishment and thus impels caution, then reflection (al-naẓar) becomes obligatory by reason alone. In answer, Ghazali lays out the true picture: obligation is a type of preponderance (rujḥān) in the action, and the Obligator (al-mūjib) is God alone, who gives preponderance. The messenger (al-rasūl) informs of this preponderance. The miracle (al-muʿjiza) proves his truthfulness. Reflection is the cause of knowing that truthfulness. Reason is the instrument for reflection and for understanding the meaning of the message. Nature (al-ṭabʿ) is what impels one to caution after understanding the danger through reason. Nature must exist to make the person deserving of reward or punishment, but it does not impel unless it understands the danger, and it only understands through reason. Reason itself does not perceive preponderance by itself; it learns it from the messenger. The messenger does not create preponderance; God does. The miracle's indication depends on reflection, which is carried out by reason. The passage concludes with a final summary of correct terminology: obligation is preponderance, the obligator is God, the informer is the messenger, the identifier of danger and truthfulness is reason, and the impeller to salvation is nature. This is the truth, and one should ignore conventional speech that fails to resolve the issue.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were the patient in the analogy, would you refuse treatment until you understand the medical reasoning yourself? How does that parallel your approach to divine commands—do you hesitate until you fully grasp the wisdom, or do you act on the prophet's guidance?
?
The objection suggests that reason alone obligates reflection. But in your life, what actually compels you to obey God—the rational fear of punishment or the conviction from revelation? Which one drives your actions right now, and are you satisfied with that motive?

The Roles of God, Prophet, Reason, Miracle, and Nature in Obligation
Ghazali resolves the objection by delineating the roles of God, prophet, reason, miracle, and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's precise delineation of the roles of God, the prophet, reason, miracle, reflection, and nature in the process of obligation.

Core Idea
Obligation originates from God; all other actors are instruments in conveying or understanding it.

Ghazali now responds to the objection by systematically assigning each element its proper role. He defines obligation as preponderance (rujḥān) and identifies God as the sole Obligator. The messenger informs of this preponderance, the miracle proves his truthfulness, reflection verifies the miracle, reason understands the warning, and nature impels action after understanding. This clarifies the entire mechanism and resolves the tension between reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's definition of obligation
Notice that obligation is defined as a type of preponderance in the action, and the Obligator is God alone, who gives that preponderance.
This distinguishes God's role from all others.
2
Identify each role
List the roles: messenger (informer), miracle (evidence), reflection (cause of knowing truthfulness), reason (instrument), nature (impeller). Understand how each contributes.
Each role is necessary and distinct.
3
Understand the necessity of nature
Nature impels to caution after understanding the danger through reason. It must exist for reward or punishment to be deserved.
Nature is the motivational force, not the source of obligation.
4
Summarize the corrected terminology
Ghazali concludes with a clear summary: obligation=preponderance, Obligator=God, informer=messenger, identifier of danger=reason, impeller=nature.
This is the correct understanding, free from ambiguity.

تشفى ، فإن أستاذي غني عن بقائك ، وأنا أيضاً كذلك ، فعند هذا لو التالف المريض : هذا يجب علي بالعقل أو بقولك ، وما لم يظهر لي هذا لم أشتغل بالتجربة . . كان مُهْلِكاً نفسَهُ ، ولم يكن على الطبيب ضرر . فكذلك النبي قد أخيرَهُ الله تعالى بأن الطاعة شفاء ، والمعصية داء ، وأنَّ الإيمان مسعد ، والكفر مهلك ، وأخبره بأنه غني عن العالمين سعدوا أم شقوا ، فإنما شأن الرسول أن يبلغ ويرشد إلى طريق المعرفة وينصرف ، فمن نظَرَ . . فلنفسه ، ومن قصَّرَ .. فعليها ، وهذا واضح . تحريجة ومدار فإن قيل : فقد رجع الأمر إلى أنَّ العقل هو الموجب من حيث إنه بسماع النظر في ذلك على كلامه ودعواه يتوقَّع عقاباً ، فيحملُهُ العقل على الحذر ، ولا يحصل إلا العقل بالنظر ، فيجب عليه النظرُ ؟ هو هو قلنا : الحق الذي يكشف الغطاء عن هذا من غير اتباع رسم وتقليد أمر أن الوجوب - كما بان - عبارةٌ عن نوع رجحان في الفعل ، والموجب هو الله تعالى ؛ لأنه المرجح ، والرسول مخبر عن الترجح ، والمعجزة دليل على صدقه في الخبر ، والنظرُ سبب في معرفة الصدق ، والعقل آلة النظر ولفهم معنى الخبر ، والطبع مستحثّ على الحذر بعد فهم المحذور بالعقل ، فلا بد من طبع تخالفه العقوبة الموعودة ويوافقه الثواب الموعود ليكون مستحقاً ، ولكن لا يستحثُ ما لم يفهم المحذور ولم يقدره ظناً أو علماً ، ولا يفهم إلا بالعقل ، والعقلُ لا يفهم الترجيح بنفسه بل بسماعه من الرسول ، والرسولُ ليس يرجُحُ الفعل على الترك بنفسه ، بل الله هو المرجح ، والرسول مخبرٌ ، وصدق الرسول لا يظهر بنفسه بل بالمعجزة والمعجزة لا تدلُّ ما لم يُنظر فيها ، والنظر بالعقل فإذن ؛ قد انكشفت المعاني والصحيح في الألفاظ أن يقال : الوجوب هو الرجحان ، والموجب هو الله تعالى ، والمخبرُ هو الرسول ، والمعرَّفُ للمحذور وصدق الرسول هو العقل ، والمستحثُ على سلوك سبيل الخلاص هو الطبع ، فهكذا ينبغي أن يفهم الحق في هذه المسألة ، ولا يلتفتُ إلى الكلام المعتاد الذي لا يشفي الغليل ولا يزيل الغموض
It cures, for my teacher is independent of your remaining, and I also am so. At this point, if the patient perished [saying]: 'This is obligatory upon me by reason or by your saying, and as long as this is not clear to me I will not engage in experiment,' he would have killed himself, and there would be no harm to the physician. Likewise, the Prophet — God informed him that obedience is a cure, disobedience a disease, that faith brings happiness, unbelief destruction, and informed him that God is independent of the worlds whether they are happy or miserable. The mission of the messenger is only to convey and guide to the path of knowledge and then depart. So whoever reflects — for his own soul, and whoever falls short — against it. This is clear. A twist and a pivot? Then it is said: 'Then the matter returns to the point that reason is what imposes obligation, insofar as by hearing the consideration of that (the revelation) concerning his speech and claim, it anticipates punishment, so reason impels him to caution, and (caution) is not attained except by reason through reflection, so reflection is obligatory upon him?' We reply: The truth that unveils this without following convention and imitation is that obligation — as has been shown — is an expression of a type of preponderance in the action, and the Obligator is God Most High because He is the one who gives preponderance. The messenger is the informer about the preponderance. The miracle is evidence of his truthfulness in the report. Reflection is the cause of knowing the truthfulness. Reason is the instrument of reflection and of understanding the meaning of the report. Nature is the impeller to caution after understanding the danger by reason. There must be a nature that opposes the promised punishment and agrees with the promised reward so that he becomes deserving. But (nature) does not impel unless it understands the danger and estimates it by conjecture or knowledge, and it does not understand except by reason. Reason does not understand the preponderance by itself but by hearing it from the messenger. The messenger does not give preponderance to action over abandonment by himself; rather, God is the one who gives preponderance. The messenger is an informer. The truthfulness of the messenger is not apparent by itself but by the miracle. The miracle does not indicate unless one reflects on it. Reflection is by reason. Therefore, the meanings have been unveiled. The correct expression is to say: Obligation is the preponderance. The Obligator is God. The informer is the messenger. The identifier of the danger and the truthfulness of the messenger is reason. The impeller to follow the path of salvation is nature. This is how the truth in this matter should be understood, and one should not pay attention to ordinary talk that does not quench the thirst nor remove ambiguity.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument that clarifies the mechanism of obligation (al-wujūb) and the precise roles of God, the prophet, reason (al-ʿaql), and nature (al-ṭabʿ). The passage begins with an analogy: a patient who refuses treatment until it is proven to him by reason or the doctor's word would kill himself, but the doctor is not at fault. Similarly, the prophet's duty is only to convey and guide; people's failure or success is their own. Then an objection is raised: if reason is what makes obligation effective because it anticipates punishment and thus impels caution, then reflection (al-naẓar) becomes obligatory by reason alone. In answer, Ghazali lays out the true picture: obligation is a type of preponderance (rujḥān) in the action, and the Obligator (al-mūjib) is God alone, who gives preponderance. The messenger (al-rasūl) informs of this preponderance. The miracle (al-muʿjiza) proves his truthfulness. Reflection is the cause of knowing that truthfulness. Reason is the instrument for reflection and for understanding the meaning of the message. Nature (al-ṭabʿ) is what impels one to caution after understanding the danger through reason. Nature must exist to make the person deserving of reward or punishment, but it does not impel unless it understands the danger, and it only understands through reason. Reason itself does not perceive preponderance by itself; it learns it from the messenger. The messenger does not create preponderance; God does. The miracle's indication depends on reflection, which is carried out by reason. The passage concludes with a final summary of correct terminology: obligation is preponderance, the obligator is God, the informer is the messenger, the identifier of danger and truthfulness is reason, and the impeller to salvation is nature. This is the truth, and one should ignore conventional speech that fails to resolve the issue.

Key Terms
رجحان
موجب
مخبر
معجزة
نظر
عقل
طبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that reason alone imposes obligation or that the prophet creates obligation.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that God alone is the Obligator; the prophet only informs, reason only understands, and nature only impels.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the role of nature (al-ṭabʿ) in the process of obligation?
A) It is the obligator that gives preponderance.
B) It impels to caution after understanding the danger by reason.
C) It is the informer of the preponderance.
D) It is the instrument of reflection.

Key Takeaway
Obligation is preponderance from God; the prophet informs, reason understands, and nature impels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated a miracle you heard or read about as if it directly obligated belief and action, without pausing to reflect rationally on its meaning and source? Will you commit to using reason as Ghazali insists, or will you continue to skip that step?
?
When you feel a natural urge to avoid what you believe is harmful or to seek what is promised as good, do you examine whether that urge is based on a true understanding from the messenger, or just your own assumptions? What would change if you traced every impulse back to its source?
?
You claim to believe that God is the ultimate obligator—but if you were asked to articulate how His obligation reaches you through the chain Ghazali outlines, could you? If not, are you truly treating Him as the source, or are you just saying it while relying on something else? Will you memorize and apply this chain starting today?

Denying Only the Impossible: Ghazali's Principle
Ghazali argues that what is not proven impossible should not be denied due to mere improbability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that only proof of impossibility, not improbability, justifies denial of a claim.

Core Idea
Improbability is not a valid reason to deny a claim; only impossibility is.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a common fallacy: rejecting miraculous or unseen events because they seem unlikely. He draws on examples from revelation, dreams, and human creation to show that subjective improbability is not a sound criterion for denial. Instead, he establishes a key principle of Islamic theology: what is not proven impossible should not be denied.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Pay attention to Ghazali's tone and the examples he uses.
The passage is dense; reading twice helps.
2
Identify the examples
Note the three examples Ghazali gives: the state of revelation, dream experiences, and the creation of man from a drop. Ask why each supports his argument.
3
Understand the logical implication
Ghazali shows that rejecting improbabilities would lead to rejecting universally accepted things like dreams and human creation. This highlights the flaw in the fallacy.
He uses a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Grasp the concluding principle
The final sentence is the key: 'What is not proven impossible should not be denied merely due to improbability.' Memorize this principle.
This is a foundational rule in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كانت تكون عنده حاضرةً في وقت ظهور برحاء الوحي، فإنكار هذا مصدره الإلحاد وإنكارُ سعة القدرة وقد فرغنا من إبطاله. ويلزم منه أيضاً إنكارُ ما يشاهده النائم ويسمعه من الأصوات الهائلة المزعجة، ولولا التجربة.. لبادرَ إلى إنكار كل من سمع من النائم حكاية أحواله. فتعساً لمن ضاقت حوصلته عن تقدير اتساع القدرة لهذه الأمور المستحقرة بالإضافة إلى خلق السماوات والأرض وما بينهما مع ما فيهما من العجائب، والسبب الذي ينفّرُ طباع أهل الضلال عن التصديق بهذه الأمور بعينه منفّر عن التصديق بخلق الإنسان من نطفة قذرة مع ما فيه من العجائب والآيات لولا أنَّ المشاهدة تضطره إلى التصديق بذلك. فإذن؛ ما لا برهان على إحالته لا ينبغي أن يُنكر بمجرد الاستبعاد.
[The revelation] used to be present with him at the time of the appearance of the intensity of revelation. Denying this stems from atheism and denial of the extent of [divine] power, and we have already finished refuting it. It also necessarily follows from it the denial of what the sleeper sees and hears of terrifying and disturbing sounds, and were it not for experience, everyone who heard from a sleeper a tale of his states would hasten to denial. Perish one whose intellect is too narrow to appreciate the vastness of [divine] power for these trivial matters compared to the creation of the heavens and the earth and what is between them, with the wonders they contain. The very cause that repels the natures of the people of misguidance from believing in these matters also repels them from believing in the creation of man from a filthy drop, with the wonders and signs it contains, were it not that observation compels him to believe that. Therefore, what there is no proof of its impossibility should not be denied merely due to improbability.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is an argument against denying the possibility of miracles and eschatological events based on subjective improbability. Ghazali begins by referencing the state of the Prophet during revelation (intensity) and states that denying such phenomena stems from atheism and denial of the extent of God's power, which he has already refuted. He then points out a logical consequence: if one denies based on improbability, one would also have to deny the experiences of sleepers (dreams and terrifying sounds), which are widely attested through experience. He criticizes those whose minds cannot grasp that God's power can extend to these relatively minor matters, which are trivial compared to the creation of the heavens and earth. He further argues that the same mindset that rejects these miraculous reports would also reject the creation of man from a drop of semen, were it not for constant empirical observation. The conclusion is a key methodological principle: what is not proven to be impossible should not be rejected merely because it seems improbable.

Key Terms
إحالة (impossibility)
استبعاد (improbability)
سعة القدرة (extent of divine power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Improbability alone can justify rejecting a claim or report.
Correction: Ghazali states that only evidence of impossibility (burhan 'ala ihalatihi) is grounds for denial; mere improbability (isti'bad) is not sufficient.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the correct criterion for denying something?
A) Mere improbability
B) Evidence of impossibility
C) Lack of empirical witness
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Do not deny a claim merely because it seems improbable; only deny if there is proof of impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a claim about the unseen—like the reality of angels or miracles—simply because it felt far-fetched? In light of this principle, what would you need to do before rejecting such a claim? Will you commit to seeking proof of impossibility before dismissing anything from revelation?
?
Is there any aspect of your faith that you secretly struggle to accept because it seems unlikely to you? How does Ghazali's argument challenge your hesitation? What step will you take today to overcome that internal resistance?
?
The passage says the same cause that repels people from believing miracles also repels them from believing in creation from a drop—something you accept because you see it. What does that tell you about your own reliance on observation? Are you willing to trust God's power beyond what you see? How will you practice that trust this week when faced with something that seems improbable but is affirmed in revelation?

Condition and Cause: Two Types of Necessity
Ghazali defines condition and cause as types of necessary relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between a condition (shart) and a cause ('illa) as two different types of necessary relations.

Core Idea
A condition is necessary but not sufficient; a cause produces its effect necessarily.

Ghazali introduces two key concepts for understanding necessity in the world: condition and cause. While both involve a necessary link, they function differently. This passage defines each with an example, preparing you to analyse causal relationships in theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Notice how Ghazali moves from an example (knowledge, life, will) to a general definition of condition, then to cause.
The passage has some textual issues, but the main ideas are clear.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where Ghazali defines 'condition' and 'cause'. For condition, note the phrase 'لا بد منه لوجود الشيء' (indispensable for existence) and 'ليس وجود الشيء به بل عنده ومعه' (not by it, but with it). For cause, note the claim about the relationship between cause and effect.
The definition of cause includes a nuance about multiple causes.
3
Apply the distinction to the example
Consider the example: life is a condition for knowledge, and knowledge is a condition for will. Does life cause knowledge? No, it is merely a condition. This illustrates the difference.
Think of other examples: a key is a condition for opening a lock, but does not cause the lock to open by itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علم الشخص مع حياته، حياته، وإرادته مع علمه .. فيلزم ـ لا محالة ـ من تقدير انتفاء الحياة انتفاء العلم، ومن تقدير انتفاء العلم انتفاء الإرادة، ويعبر عن هذا بالشرط، وهو الذي لا بدَّ منه لوجود الشيء، ولكن ليس وجود الشيء به، بل عنده ومعه. العلة علاقة العلة مع الثالث: العلاقة التي بين العلة والمعلول، ويلزم من تقدير عدم عدم المعلول إن لم يكن للمعلول إلا علَّةٌ واحدةٌ، فإنْ تُصوَّرَ أن تكون له علة المعلول أخرى .. فيلزمُ من تقدير نفي كلِّ العلل نفي المعلول، ولا يلزم من نفي علة بعينها نفي المعلول مطلقاً، بل يلزم نفي معلول تلك العلة على الخصوص
The knowledge of a person with his life, his life, and his will with his knowledge... So it necessarily follows from the supposition of the negation of life the negation of knowledge, and from the supposition of the negation of knowledge the negation of will. And this is called the condition (shart), which is what is indispensable for the existence of a thing, but the existence of the thing is not by it, rather with it and alongside it. The cause: the relationship of the cause with the third: the relationship between cause and effect, and it follows from the supposition of the non-existence of the non-existence of the effect if the effect has only one cause. But if it is conceived that it has another cause of the effect, then it follows from the supposition of the negation of all the causes the negation of the effect, but it does not follow from the negation of a specific cause the negation of the effect absolutely, rather the negation of the effect of that particular cause follows.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage defines two types of necessary relations: condition (shart) and cause ('illa). The author begins with an example: knowledge, life, and will. The presence of life is a condition for knowledge, and knowledge is a condition for will. The condition is necessary for the existence of the thing, but the thing does not exist by means of the condition; rather, the condition accompanies it. Then the author moves to the cause-effect relationship. He states that if an effect has only one cause, then the negation of that cause necessitates the negation of the effect. However, if the effect has multiple causes, then negating one specific cause does not necessitate the negation of the effect absolutely; it only necessitates the negation of the effect that is specific to that cause. The third type of relation is unclear due to text corruption. This conceptual framework prepares for the application to the case of killing and death.

Key Terms
شرط
علة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a condition and a cause are the same thing, both implying full necessity.
Correction: A condition is necessary but insufficient; the thing exists with the condition, not by it. A cause, on the other hand, directly produces its effect. The passage clarifies this subtle but important difference.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what follows from the negation of a specific cause when an effect has multiple causes?
A) The negation of the effect absolutely.
B) The negation of the effect of that particular cause.
C) The negation of all effects.
D) Nothing follows.

Key Takeaway
A condition is a necessary prerequisite, while a cause is a sufficient producer; they represent two distinct types of necessity.
